J'OR PUBLIC INFORMATION ONLY, the SHARE LIST HAVING BEEN CLOSED.
Abridged Prospectus of the Sir JobO Fraoklii) Sold-njiijiDg Co.,
WOOD’S POINT, VICTORIA.
To be registered as a No Liability Company under Part 11. Companies Act 1890.
CAPITAL, £32,000, in 32,003 Shares of £1 each.— l2,ooo Shares paid to 20s. each to be handed to the
vendors, and ±5OO in cash, in payment lor the mine. 20,000 Shares to be offered to the public at 2s. per share,
payable Is. on application, and Is. on allotment. The whole of the proceeds from the sale of shares, less flotation
expenses, and the balance of the cash to vendors, to be placed to the credit of the company.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS: Hon. John A. Wallace, M.L.C., Melbourne; A. W. Harston, Melbourne; T. W. Mason,
Melbourne ; Sidney Smith, Melbourne ; W T . Tonkin, Wood’s Point; William Jamieson, Matlock; Sa.m. R. Walter,
Melbourne; John E. Gard, Bendigo. MANAGER (pro. tern.) : J. P. Cameron (Meudell & Cameron), Broken Hill
Chambers, Queen-street, Melbourne.
THIS Company is being I l ined to purchase the well-known gold-mine of the Sir John Franklyn
Company, lease No. 3531), Beeohworth, comprising about 22J acres, situated on the Never Mind
Spur, near Wood’s Point, for the purpose of opening up and developing the property at a greater
depth than the mine has yet been worked.
The cost of workiug the mine can be reduced to a minimum on account of the facilities afforded by
tunnels. The property was extensively worked in the early days, and again in the years 1877 to 1880.
During the latter period alone the total amount of gold won exceeded 17,0000 z. The stone in the
lower workings is much mineralised, and with proper concentrating plant would now be highly
profitable.
Extracts from the Reports of A. B. Ainsworth, Esq., District Mining Surveyor, Alexandra :
“ An adit level, some 1139 ft. long, has been driven towards the dyke through hard rock, at a
depth of some 200 ft. below the old workings, and thus a block of ground about 200 ft. deep and 1600 ft.
in length, on the strike of the main dyke, through the lease block—through which practically only one
vein has been partially stoped, and hardly any part methodically prospected by crosscuts, &c. —finds
issue on the face of the hill, and can thus be economically explored and developed. Indeed, this
expensive piece of work affords a key—the only one—to the entire hill, and gives a special value of
position to the block.
“ Persons well acquainted with the mine assert the existence, to their knowledge, of one block of
ground within easy reach of this adit level, from which large yields are anticipated.
“ I regret that time has not permitted of sections of this promising mining property being pre-
pared, as they would have shown clearly the value to incomers of the dead work done and the working
facilities extant from the adit level to the surface. It may be remarked, as regards this property, that
the mine has hitherto responded handsomely to intelligent outlays of capital and good management,
like other of our standard mines, notably the New Loch Fyne, Hope, and Great Eldorado, which are
again being worked and are proving themselves first-class ventures at small outlays, after long years
of idleness, and I trust it will form another link in the chain of successes that promise to secure to
this much neglected but exceptionally rich district a fair trial and a commensurate prosperous mining
career, in harmony with its early developments, which equalled or excelled, for a few years, those of
any other field in Victoria.”
Original reports, &e., may be seen at the offices of
MEUDELL and CAMERON, Agents for the Company.

This company is being formed to acquire Lease No. 248/93M, situated at North Dundas, imme-
diately to the west of and adjoining the property of the Curtin and Davis Extended Company. The
lease comprises 69 acres or thereabouts.
rhe North-east Dundas tramway passes through a portion of tho company’s property at the
north, so that there will be excellent facilities for conveying the ore from the mine, which can be
perhaps less expensively worked than any other on the field
The following report has been received from Mr. W. Euddon, M.E., the manager of the Comet S Iver-mining Company No Liability:-
Dundas, May 25, 1896.
F. E. Harris, Esq., Austral-chambers, Mel-
bourne.
Dear Sir, —At you request I visited and in-
spected the property known as the South-West
Curtin and Davis, situated at North-east Dundas
and to the south-west of the Curtin and Davis
S.M. Co.
The property consists of one 69-acre section
situated on the north-west slope of a very steep
hill, the elevation being from base to summit
1150 ft.
I find on examination that three lodes have been
discovered. No. 1 lode is situated near the sum-
mit of the hill, and next the eastern boundary of
the property. At present there has not been
much attention given to this lode by way of pros-
pecting, and by its strike I am of opinion it will
prove to be a continuation of tfie No. 2 lode.
No. 2 lode-—This has been the principal develop-
ment-on the property, and the greatest interest
has been shown in the opening and developing the
same. It is situated about the centre of the pro-
perty, tiie lode having been opened and exposed
for a distance of 9 chains along the side of the
hill. The lode is well defined and between two
good walls. The oountry rock on the footwall is
composed of a very close slate, the hanging-wall is
composed of a hard conglomerate. The lode at
the surface is e imposed of gossan, which at the
depth of a few feet gives place to iron and copper,
pyrites and phyl ore, very rich in silver, assays
from which have given the following results :
Silver, ftoin 160 oz. to 2910 z. per ton ; copper, from
12 per cent, to 28 per cent, per ton.
At the time of my visit the lode had been
stripped and based for a distance of 2 to 3 chains,
the ore being left standing in places from 3ft. to
4ft. high, which would average from Ift. to 18in.
wide for a distance of 120 ft. A shaft has been
sunk to a depth of 20ft. on the lode at this point,
the average width of lode in this shaft being Ift
of clean ore. From 6to 8 tons of ore have been
won in sinking the shaft. This ore, I estimate,
should bulk, according to assays taken by the
manager (who kindly supplied me with the returns
from the same), 160 oz. silver and 16 per cent,
copper per ton.
The strike of No. 2 lode is 12deg. east of north,
with an easterly underlay of about Ift. in 3ft. into
the hill. At present a tunnel is being driven into
the hill to intersect this lode at a depth of about
80ft. The tunnel should reach the lode in about
80ft. to 90ft. driving, and should the lode be met
at this level, which I have no doubt it will be, a
considerable quantity of ore can be raised cheaply.
Adits can be driven lower down the hill, and as
much as 400 ft. to 500 ft. of backs can be obtained,
thus doing away with expensive machinery.
No. 3 lode is situated near tlm northern boun-
dary of property, and midway between east and
west boundary. At present little or no prospect-
ing has been done other than by a cut where the
lode is exposed. The lode contains about 3ft. of
lode matter, 6in. to Sin. of the hanging-wall por-
tion being composed of iron and copper pyrites.
Strike, of lode IBdeg. west of north, with rut
easterly underlay.
The property as a whole I consider a very valu-
able one, No. 2 lode especially showing 1 a good
body of rich ore, with fully 100 ft. stripped along
the line ready for breaking down, which I estimate
should produce 100 tons of marketable ore.
The North-east Dundas tram passes through the
property at the foot of the hill 400 ft. below the
outcrop of No. 2 lode, and the ore from same can
be easily and cheaply conducted to the Govern-
ment tram by means of self-acting tram. Taking
into consideration the position of this property
and the splendid facilities offered for carrying on
cheap mining by driving adits, together with the
rich ores proved to exist on the property, I have,
no hesitation in recommending it to the public as
one that will in the,near future, with a reasonable
amount of capital expended to further develop,
become a valuable property.
I am, dear sir, yours, &c.,
(Signed) W. BUDIION.
LITERARY NOTES.
The writer once shared a railway compart-
ment for a couple of hours with Wil-
liam Bede Dailey—one/ ofi the finest and
most cultivated intelligences that Australia
has nourished. The talk fell on books.
Dailey said that, looking back »ver his
life, he could plainly trace successive
phases of intellectual development—just
as, looking at the ' side of a cliff,
one can sometimes distinguish the suc-
cession of superposed geological strata.
Fiction was his first love in literature—he
was a glutton of novels when a boy. Then
he drifted in. j poetry, and from poetry into
history, and from history into science, and
from science onward to philosophy. “ And
now?” “Now,” said Dailey, “I enjoy
literature in all its manifestations. But if
there is one class of books I prefer to
another, I.think it must be”—with a flash
of his teeth—“ why, New Books ! ”
* * * •
There is no disputing the seduction of
New Books for Australians. The “new”
books are always “ out” at the libraries, and
one may keep an “old ” book thrice as long;
while to every well-regulated metropolitan
bookshop is attached a staff of customers who
know the weekly European mail-day better
than the paid employees, and can calculate to
a nicety the time when the cold chisel will
creak musically into the tops of the precious
cases behind the counter. Then and there
they cluster like ants round a honeycomb,
happy to be permitted to hold the hammer.
“It is their hour,” as Mr. Pinero puts it.
“There is only one hour in a bookman’s
week,” says the Notorious Mrs. Ebbsinith—
“ one supreme hour. His poor life is like
the arch of a crescent: so many days
lead up to that hour, so many weary days
decline from it. No matter what he
may strive for, there is a moment when
the Case Mail taps him upon the shoulder
and says, “ Man, this hour is the best that
Literature has to spare you.” It may come
to him in calm or in tempest, lighted by the
steady radiance of William Lecky or by the
glitter of the evil stars of Marie Corelli ; but
however it comes, be it good or evil, it is his
hour —let him dwell upon every second of
• * * *
Doubtless Dailey and Pinero knew what
they were talking about. In the gloss and
crackle of new hooks there is a bewitching
beaute du diable which will stir liter-
ary blood in spite of all the maxims of ex-
perience. You have been stirred by it before:
you know it won’t last : you half turn away
to the time-tested second-hand shelf : then
the leaves flutter as some curious Columbus-
customer peers between the uncut pages, and
your heart flutters too —you are undone.
Poor little bird, once more in the coils of
the serpent ! and . . . how delightful it
is ! in spite of right-thinking Emerson, with
his
Rule I.—Never read any book that is not a
year old—
a rule which he was himself not too careful
to keep. And the best of it is that all the
booksellers advertise that * ‘ customers are not
pressed to buy.” They don’t care for your
money, but they like you to be an ornamen-
tal presence in the shop, and lean pictur-
esquely against the shelves, and add a certain
tone and distinction to the rows of classics.
The booksellers’ own special newspapers are
continually urging the backward subscriber
to dare to be a Daniel— better than a Daniel!
—and try and make an attractive little den
for literary lions to come and pick their
horrid little bones in. “And d—n the
sales ! ” said one vigorous organ recently.
So the New-Book lover is always in a glow
with thinking of himself as a knight bravely
spurring his hobby to the assistance of some
bookshopping damn-sell in distress.
• * * •
The case may be summed up with the help
of some quite unseasonable verses written
“ for The Bulletin ” and called
The newest book of specially Australian
interest is “ A First Fleet Family,” by Louis
Becke and Walter Jeffery. In contrast to
Becke’s earlier books, this is dull and dis-
appointing. Part is history ; part fiction ;
but the fiction is merely laid on top of the
history, not woven into it to make a histori-
cal novel, so the book is neither fish, flesh,
nor good red herring. This is a pity ; for
the authors have taken much pains to make
a decent narrative ; and the central incident
—the escape of William Bryant and party
from Port Jackson convict - settlement in
1791, and their extraordinary voyage to
Timor in a small boat—has inspiration enough
for anything.
* * * •
Inspiration, however, is precisely what the
book lacks : it is heavy and mechanical
throughout. The first seven chapters sup-
ply a romantic English introduction in
the style of Conan Doyle and the
minor historical novelists of the Strand
Magazine, and of no particular interest.
Then comes an account of the first
fleet’s voyage to Australia, and the early days
of the convict-settlement. This is appar-
ently paraphrased, for the most part, from
the second edition of Collins’s “Account
of the English Colony in N.S. Wales ”
(based on Lieut.-Gov. King’s papers), and
gains nothing in the paraphrasing. Becke
invents an account of the voyage to Timor ;
and the tale closes with more dubious Eng-
lish romance. Barring the apocryphal Ser-
geant Dew, the supposed teller of the story,
over whom a good deal of trouble has been
taken, the characters are lay figures. The
epithets and descriptions do not picture them
to the mind : there is no moving invention in
the book, no creative imagination. Try
their hardest, the authors can never write
convincingly. Here, for example, is part of
what is intended for “ a purple passage ” —
the description of the death of the heroine’s
infant :—
For many days before, Will and I had seen that
the grey shadows of death were clouding the once
bright face and sweet blue eyes of our boy, and
oft at night, when our boat sped silently along
under the starlit sky, I would sit holding his
slight, wasted form in my arms, and my tears of
repentance for my mad and cruel folly would
fall upon his pinched and deathlike features.
And, as if the dear child knew what it was that
so wrecked my wicked heart, he would sometimes
open his fast-fading eyes, and his little hand
would seek mine, and give it a faint pressure, and
then the white lids would droop and close again.
Almost as the boat touched the sand, the small,
tender form trembled gently in my arms, his eyes
met mine for the last time in this world, and, with
a sigh that stabbed me to the heart, he died.
This fine-journalese is supposed to be uttered
by an anguished mother, a last-century Eng-
lish peasant ! Not thus does Becke write
when he feels and knows his situation : not
thus die the wife of Macy O’Shea and the
victims of Tia Kau.
* * * *
When Mrs. Watson, the heroine of Lizard
Island (N.Q.) fled with her baby in 1881
to perish miserably of thirst and hunger,
the imagination of her sufferings fired one
“8.K.” to publish in a Cooktown paper
these poor little ungrammatical lines: —
Ob, for a drink from the waters of Dee !
Or a breath from the ice crest of Bonnie Ben Nee.
Oh, for a sough through a green birken tree,
To cool the hot brows of my Baby and me.
Thou God of our fathers,
If some one must dee,
In mercy save Baby,
And tak’ awa’ me.
Cling close to me, darling,
While I go to rest !
Thou mayst still suckle life
From my poor starved breast.
Cling close to me, darling,
As the last night comes down,
I see heaven open to lower me a crown;
I still feel thy dear head,
Still see thy blue ’ee !
And now God save Baby,
And tak’ awa’ me.
Compare the truth, and pathos, and reality,
and poetry of this with the pretensions of
the passage printed above. This is a cry
from the heart, to the heart. The woman
who wrote it. by dint of deep, emotion, iden-
tified herself with Mrs. Watson, thought and
felt with her, as the authors of “A First
Fleet Family ” have never succeeded in
thinking with Mary Broad. Their book
represents a lot of work and ability ; but it
fails in one thing—it isn’t alive.
Some further notes of Stevenson, from
Mrs. Strong’s reminiscences in Scribner’s :
S. said he thought his own gift lay in the grim
and terrible —that some writers touch the heart,
he clutched at the throat. I said I thought “Pro-
vidence and the Guitar ” a very pretty story, full
of sweetness and the milk of human kindness.
“ E’t it is not so sweet as ‘ Markheirn ’ is grim.
There I feel myself strong.”
“ At least,” I said, “ you have no mannerisms.”
He took the book out of my hand and read “ it
was a wonderful clear night of stars.” “Oh,” he
said, “ how many, many times I have written ‘ a
wonderful clear night of stars’! ”
But I maintained that this, in itself, was a good
sentence and presented a picture to the mind.
“ It is the mannerisms of the author who can’t
say ‘says he’ and ‘says she ’ that I object to;
whose characters hiss, and thunder, and ejaculate
and syllable ”
“ O my dear,” he said, “ deal gently with me-*
I once fluted ! ”
* • • •
Again : —
I asked Louis why painters, who live in much
the same atmosphere as literary men, are less in-
teresting and more narrow-minded ; at least, that
had been my experience. He offered an explana-
tion that sounded reasonable enough. The study
of painting or music does not expand the mind in
any direction save one. Literature, with its study
of human nature, events, and history, is a con-
stant education, and in that career a man cannot
stick at one place as the painter and musician
almost invariably do. He studies his one pin’s
point of a talent, enlarging that, perhaps, and
deepening it, but in no other direction does his
mind work. The bank-clerk, who e daily life is
spent in adding-up figures, knows that his intelli-
gence is cramped, and is more apt to devote his
leisure to study and improvement; but the painter
believes his work to be a culture, and thinks he
needs no more.
RENAISSANCE.
In my ears it is sounding to-day,
The song of the Spring !
How my heart leaps, and urges the blood in swift
surges to greet the sweet Spring !
And my pulse, from low undertones rising to
thunder tones, trumpets the challenge of
mystical May,
Of witching September, while Winter’s dull ember
Glows fierce in the glamour of Spring,
The passion-fed furnace of Spring!
And my Love, she is tingling to-day
To the touch of the Sprint !
How she t embles and thrills as from numberless
rills pou s the full tide of Spring !
And her eyes are veiled oceans whence amorous
potions I quaff in a fury, as one who is fey :
She bears me no malice, but holds up for chalice
Her lips brimming over with Spring,
All ruddied and coralled with Spring J
O my Youth ! let us make holiday,
Give a garland to Spring !
For you flower from her root and your ecstasies
shoot from the sources of Spring !
Not a throb gay or tragic her alcnemy magic has
missed from the cycles of worlds passed
away :
Kiss of atoms thick-thronging, sighs of spheres
mad with longing,
A Deity burning, a Universe yearning—
All summed up in Spring!
Now, New Books are the Spring of Litera-
ture.
The hot-hearted sun,
And the brazen-faced sea
Have no pity or mercy
For Baby and me.
The white gulls skim swiftly,
The bright sails fly free,
But they bring us no succour
For Baby and me.
Thou Great One and Good One
Who rulest the sky,
Send rain to the parched lips
Of Baby and I.
Nevertheless, “A First Fleet Family ” is
quite likely to be “a success,” especially in
England. “ For,” as an English publisher’s
Australian representative remarked the other
day, “ to get the public tasfc* in literary
matters, deduct just 33 per oent. from a
Bulletin judgment.” And 33 per cent, is a
sufficiently profitable margin.
BROWN EYES-A FANCY.
Her dreamy eyes are deep
As the fathomless eyes of sleep,
And will no love declare,
Nor laugh nor weep ;
And daring souls who dive
Into the water brown,
To win the secrets there,
Sink and drown,
Or else are chained alive
A thousand fathoms down.
Ronald Campbell Magpie, in The Bookman.

“ Orroroo, Adelaide, Aug. 25, 1894. Gentlemen,—l
hereby acknowledge receipt of cheque, my share in
estate, with thanks. I also thank you for prompt manner
you have conducted the business.—Yours respectfully,
Amy Nutt.”
“ Port Darwin Camp, South Australia, July 7, 1894.—1
purchased your ‘ Register’ and found my uncle’s name as
having funds in Chancery. I thereupon instructed you to
investigate the matter, the result of which was I recovered
my share, and advise all to procure your ‘ Register ’ and
have their claims properly investigated by Dougal and
Co., London.—Yours faithfully, A. Warland.”
“ Queen-street, Auckland, 16th June, 1894. —Dear Sirs,—
The ‘ Register’ compiled by you is of very great value. In
my own case, my mother was entitled to money lying un-
claimed for 50 years in Chancery. Seeing your book 1
noticed my mother’s name, and was successful in obtain-
ing what should have been paid more than half a century
ago. It is to be regretted your book is not more generally
known.—Yours faithfully, E. T. Dufaur (Solicitor).”

bequeathed to my mother, with thanks. —Yours, &c.,
Luke Farrow.”

BEFORE YOU
T ■ 3B A 3=» ?
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,’ and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Coolgardie justices have a new penalty for
“ drunk and disorderly, yer washups ” —ss., or
seven days.
“AS YOU LIKE IT.”
Dreams in the glamour of an old romance,
Woven around a perilous love forlorn ;
-d ond eyes that yearn upon the waves’ expanse
x or a wan sail in shadowy gulfs outworn ;
immured long vacant years,
Waiting for love in some forgotten keep
Untenanted by anything but tears,
Unvisited by anything but sleep.
Where are ye fled, 0, passionate, wild days ?
vi'uj® 18 yOUr ma gi. c that w as wont to be
x lushed on the summits in the morning rays,
And in the twilight of the western sea ?
Where are the hearts that thrilled for Rosalind ’
_Wl, e are the eyes that Celia has blest?
" hy do we beat the air for ever dinned
With the great anguish of a great unrest ?
Return, O Time, the jewels thou hast thieved,
-IhoiigTi Shakespeare come not (he is a caress
t’ the fond spirit); surely we have grieved
Even enough to move a god’s distress !
Return the ark of life, the large domain
Over all gaiety, the wine, the song ;
Even if Pan shall never rule again,
May we not smile at life, alert and strong ?
If every leaf within the wild Ardennes,
If every brook that babbles to the deer
Murmurs the charm that lulled the forest glens,
Shall we not sit, and dream, and love, and hear?
Sweetness and light to spirits that are dim,
Sad with the whole of querulous excess,
Float with the music of the sylvan hymn,
Lost in Joy’s rosy-tangled wilderness.
M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.
LEAF MUSIC.
Listen, the winds are playing
A fugue in the orchard trees.
They creep through the boughs of apple
And linger among their leaves,
And touch, with a gentler straying,
Leaves over-soon decaying.
The winds come singing, singing,
Through leaves with a silken sheen.
This song is a silver treble,
With an alto note washed in.
It sounds like an apple flinging
On grass the sun is stinging.
But oh, when winds come rushing
Through wattle, when day’s at noon !
Set soft, like a mother’s murmur
Into ears when the eyelids droop.
Set low, like a, bee’s hum hushing
The flowers his lips are brushing.
OR gleaming, dripping wattle !
The wind, when it blows through you
Is a velvet-throated singer,
And it sings to a golden tune.
All noon, where the noon-lights mottle,
I lie and listen, wattle.
And, list’ning, pass the border
Where only a child may stray,
Into the land of fairies ...
The years since I went that way !
Ah, wattle ! are you the warder
That guards that dim, dear border ?
The wind loves warm-leaved apples,
Warm-hued in the light or shade.
The song has a deeper setting
That blows through a leafy spray
Gone red as the blood-red maples,
Or sunset sky at Naples.
But stealing through the edges
Of gums, with the curl still green,
An exquisite treble dances
And thrills to the lightest breeze.
Not Pan’s own reeds nor sedges
Ring sweet as young gums’ edges.
LOUIS MACK.
QUATRAINS.
I. —Labour.
Work, if you would be happy, gay, and free ;
Would taste Life’s gladness and its brightness see.
Flowers may attract the butterfly ; they yield
Their sweetness only to the toiling bee.
11. Luxury.
i O ye, whose best ambition ’tis to own
■ The largest, costliest pearl or precious stone,
See how the oyster, or the fabled toad,
Can vex your souls and wake your envious moan.
111. — Politics.
Shout for your “ rights,” O people, and rejoice
In godlike Premiers, champions of your choice;
But stay your clamorous joy and loud demands,
And sometimes list to Duty’s “ still small voice.”
IV. Rontgen.
Sans clothes and flesh, why cower beneath the gaze
Of the Professor with his clear “ x rays ”?
He cannot see your soul-scars, nor discern
Your moral twists, or dark, deceptive ways.
Y. —More Light.
“ More light,” was Goethe’s cry ; and still “More
light ”
Cry we, who dwell enwrapt in moral night.
&od! For “x rays” to pierce through vain
pretence
And make all shams transparent to our sight!
Vl.— The New Woman.
New Woman—Bah ! Her ways are old as Eve’s —
She trusts the guide who flatters and deceives ;
But, now, Man shares not in her fall, nor weaves,
To hide her shame, a robe of forest leaves !
Maoriland.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.

A disgraceful display of ruffianism occurred on
Saturday at Homebush in connection with the
second Junior football match, Homebush v. Orient-
al. From tl)p start Oriental made the game very,
“willing," and the referee, Mr. H. Hazel, with a
commendable determination to enforce tha rules
regarding rough play, ordered two of the visiting
team off the field. This 1 action, coupled with the
subsequent defeat of the Oriental team by 12
po.nts to nil, so enraged the players_and their
supporters that they proceeded to “deal it ouf’
to the referee at the conclusion of the maicai,
kicking and striking him in the most brutal
nsr, and showering upon him mud and other misA
sues. In self-defence Mr. Hazel was compelled'
to draw a revolver, with which he had coma
armed.

AUCKLAND, Thursday .—To check the arrival
of Chinese with fraudulent letters of naturalisa-
tion belonging to Chinamen departed from the
colony, the Government have decided not to is-
sue anv more letters of naturalisation to Chinese
pending the amendment of the existing law. by
which Chinamen to become naturalised must be
photographed for identification

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
• Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns m
lenqth, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that

SATURDAY, JUNE 20, 1896.
An Imperial Customs Union.
The first of the London Statist’s two prize
essays on the subject of an Imperial Zoll-
verein has now come to hand, and a serious
consideration of its contents only serves to
accentuate the remoteness and hopelessness
of the whole business so long as J. Bull
remains what he is. The idea of an Imperial
Zollverein was, in the first place, a modified
and more practical version of the nearly
extinct dream of Imperial Federation. The
former means union on a cash basis of
common interest; the latter implied union
on a basis of loyalty, and drivel, and histori-
cal glare, and howl about “the Flag.” It
provided, first and foremost, that the Empire
was to be governed in all large matters by a
central Parliament made up of representa-
tives of all the white races of Victoria’s
dominions ; and as the United Kingdom
contains a majority of these white races, this
necessarily implied that Australia, Maori-
land, Canada, and other outlying provinces
were to surrender their self-governing powers
almost bodily to J. Bull. In addition
they were to bear a proportion of the cost of
the British armaments, and, whatever terms
might be made when the alliance was first
entered into, the fixing of that proportion
must depend ultimately on the vote of the
British majority. Also, the colonies were to
have a share in the right to barrack for British
traditions and over the glories of Poitiers
and Agincourt and Balaclava —with Fontenoy
and Steinkirk and Neerwinden and Patay
and Bouvines thrown in if desired. Like-
Avise, they were offered the privilege of cheer-
ing for a country which they have no special
desire to cheer about, and the right to whoop
over what somebody else did at Inkermann
and elsewhere, and a sort of unspecified
authority to profess that they were the person
Avho did it, or ivho would have done it if they
had been there, or at least that they were
the small brother Samuel of the person who
really was there. And, as the price seemed
too high a one to pay for the privilege of
being anybody’s small brother, the negotia-
tion naturally came to nothing. It was a
shadowy affair at the beginning, and it never
amounted to more than a shadow.
The proposal to link the straggling empire
together by a Customs Union was the same
scheme, with the glare and whoop and dazzle
left out and sundry new considerations in-
serted. The idea was that the various parts
of the empire should extend to each other
special trade privileges which would be denied
to foreign countries. They would be so
adjusted, if possible, as to be mutually
advantageous, and as secession would mean
the loss of these privileges, it would, there-
fore, be to the interest of the colonies to
adhere to the mother country, and to the in-
terest of the mothdr country that they should
aclhere. If such a union was constructed on
the principle of Freetrade, or something
appproaching it, within the empire, and
definite Protection against all outside States,
it certainly looks as if it might be workable.
Assuming that Australian wool, tallow, wine,
meat, dairy produce, and other staples were
substantially protected in the British
markets against Argentina, Russia, France,
Spain, the United States, and all other ex-
traneous competitors, and that protection
was dependent on Australia’s continued con-
nection with the empire and on its willing-
ness to admit purely British manufactures
freely into Australia, it is quite possible that
the British market would become so valuable
that it would be worth this country’s while to
admit British goods free, and to belong to
the empire, and even to make considerable
sacrifices, if necessary, to hold the empire
together. And for the sake of holding the
empire together, and in return for a practical
monopoly of the colonial trade, it is quite
possible that the arrangement would be worth
Britain’s also while. There is no certainty
about the matter on either side, but the
experiment certainly looks as if it might be
feasible. But J. Bull is addicted to giving
very little and asking very much ; and, more-
over, the circumstances of Britain and the
other provinces of the empire are so wholly
dissimilar that agreement with a person of
the Bull calibre seems next to impossible.
* * * *
Freetrade undoubtedly suits Britain better
than it does any other country in the world.
In the last two centuries all the other coun-
tries of Europe have been devastated with
fire and sword, their commerce wrecked,
their towns besieged, and their industries
reduced to dust and ashes over and over
again ; while Britain escaped invasion and
rose to affluence on their ruins. Britain is
now the great creditor nation of the world,
and has an annual income of probably
£150,000,000 a year from its investments in
other parts of the globe. Therefore, though
Freetrade has practically killed its agricul-
ture, and its manufactures are not increas-
ing, and its exports are vastly less than its
imports, and its markets are flooded with
the goods of every nation on earth to the
serious detriment of its own artisans, the
aggregate wealth of the country keeps in-
creasing. The interest on foreign invest-
ments more than makes up all deficiencies.
Great Britain is becoming the retired old
woman among nations—the affluent old
woman who lives on a safe annuity and who
doesn’t require to work. If nothing happens
to overturn the present order of things
British exports will remain stationary or
diminish, and imports will keep increasing,
and the agricultural class will almost entirely
die out, and the manufacturing class will
begin to do likewise—and yet the aggregate
wealth of Britain will keep on expanding
indefinitely by the great law of compound
interest. If Britain entered into a Customs
Union whereby her ports were practically
closed against all States lying outside the
empire it would give a great stimulus to
the prosperity of the colonies. It would also
give an immense lift to the British artisan
and the decaying British agriculturist ; and,
in all probability, it would wreck a very
large proportion of the country’s foreign
investments.
* ♦ * *
Britain’s debtors pay her mostly in goods;
they can’t pay her any other way, for all the
coin on earth wouldn’t liquidate the bill for
more than ten years, or thereabouts. There-
fore, if Britain, by giving a heavy tariff pre-
ference to the empire, practically refused to
receive these goods, her debtors outside the
empire would mostly go broke. Such a pro-
ceeding would certainly do much for the
agricultural and artisan class of the United
Kingdom—it would find work for the bone
and sinew of the nation in growing the wheat
which is now grown in Russia and America
and Hungary, and in forging the iron which
now comes from Belgium, and in making all
the miscellaneous goods which, under present
conditions, are “made in Germany.” But
it would to a great extent sink the old-
woman-with-an-annuity aspect of the nation.
Britain is the one country occupied by a
civilised white race with a decent standard of
living to which Freetrade is not wholly
disastrous. The tendency of Freetrade, in
every country where decent wages and mode-
rate hours of labor prevail, is to kill off local
industries by the competition of toilers in
other countries who work longer hours and
receive less wages, and whose products are
consequently cheaper. But through past
accumulations —the result of enterprise and
plunder, and superlative good fortune—
Britain has an income independent of per-
sonal exertion, which, despite all these
drawbacks, keeps the country from growing
poorer in the aggregate. And no other
country occupies anything even distantly
resembling this position, therefore the fact
that Freetrade has compensating features in
England is not the slightest evidence in its
favor elsewhere.
The situation of the other provinces of the
Empire is the exact reverse of ail this. They
have no income from foreign investments.
They are large debtors instead of large credi-
tors, and have to send away vast sums
annually as interest instead of receiving
them. They must export more than they
import to pay their interest, unless they
keep going further and further into debt,
whereas the normal condition of Britain is to
import more than she exports. To do this
they must foster local industries to provide
exports and to enable them to do without
foreign goods as far as possible, and they
must keep down imports by artificial means,
because their conditions are wholly artificial.
The Freetrade theory that trade takes care
of itself and is best left alone, and that ex-
ports and imports naturally balance each
other, because exports pay for imports and
nobody will send to a country more value
than he receives from it, is possibly accurate
enough under natural conditions —these being
that a country neither owes money abroad
nor has money owing to it. But, as these
conditions don’t prevail anywhere, the theory
amounts to nothing. And under these wholly
diverse circumstances the project of estab-
lishing an Imperial zollverein which will be
satisfactory to all parties is very like trying
to add together half-a-mile and half-a-crown
and produce an understandable result. Ob-
viously, one party must make concessions,
and J. Bull, as the individual who wants an
Imperial Union and as the one who is most
able to afford concessions, must be that party
to a very great extent if anything is to be
done.
* * * *
The Statist’s first prize essay, already
published, presumably received the prize
because, in the opinion of the two eminent
judges, it was a fair and reasonable solution
of the difficulty. It is, therefore, interesting
as showing what J. Bull in general regards,
or is supposed to regard, as a fair solution.
The theory set forth can be compressed into
a very short space. It is proposed in the
first instance that Britain shall place a 3 per
cent, duty on live and dead meat, cheese,
butter, wheat, flour, hemp, sugar, leather,
wool, tallow, skins, mahogany and ivory—
only that and nothing more. So far as Aus-
tralasia is concerned, the 3 per cent, differ-
ence on these articles is not sufficient to make
up for the advantages in freight possessed by
Russia, Denmark, Holland, France, the
United States, and other nearer rivals, and
it hardly even puts this country on a level
with Argentina or Brazil. Three per cent,
when meat sells at 3d. per lb. is something
like one-third of a farthing per lb., which is
the weakest kind of Protection on record.
Against this it is suggested that the depen-
dencies should levy duties of about 10 per
cent, on imported manufactured goods not
of British origin. This means that the
dear old mother country is to give 3
per cent, protection to colonial pro-
duce, and receive about 10 per cent,
protection on British produce in return.
These duties are so arranged that Britain is
to raise £700,000 a year by her preferential
tariff. Australasia, with one-tenth the popu-
lation, is to raise £293,000, or more than
one-third as much, which means that Aus-
tralasia is to contribute more than four times
as much for each inhabitant as Britain does.
India, with about 60 times the population of
Australasia, is to supply £130,000, or less
than half as much in the total, and less than
one-hundredth part as much per inhabitant.
And Canada, with more population than
Australasia, is to supply £137,000 a year, or
about one-third as much per inhabitant as
this country. The aggregate to be raised by
the preferential duties is £1,400,000 a year,
and this amount is to be “ administered ” by
a Council in London, said Council consisting
of the Agents-Gene.ral and the members of
the British Government —the latter consti-
tuting a substantial majority. It is to be
applied, apparently, to defence or other
Imperial purposes, so that the money will
really go to relieve J. Bull of part of his
present expenditure while the colonies are
not relieved of anything.
* * * *
The sum total of this awful proposal, there-
fore, is about as follows : J. Bull is to give
colonial produce protection against foreign
produce in his markets to the extent of 3 per
cent. He is to get protection in colonial
markets to the extent of 10 per cent, in re-
turn. He is to get all the money raised by
both systems of protection, and to do with
it very much as he pleases. The protection
doesn’t cover equal ground in both cases ;
out of 219 articles imported in large quanti-
ties into Great Britain the colonies are to get
no preference whatever on 201, and on the
other 18 are to receive a preference which
won’t make up for the disadvantages under
which Australasia, at all events, labors in
the matter of long and expensive sea-freights.
In return they are to give Britain a large and
solid preference on practically everything,
and give Britain the money raised thereby.
The fact that two eminent and allegedly en-
lightened Britons gave a £5OO prize to the
man who concocted this incredible scheme
implies that they, at least, see nothing un-
reasonable about it, and presumably most
other Britons will view it as more or less
reasonable also. Under these circumstances
the Customs Union is decidedly “off.” As
already mentioned, there are so many differ-
ences in the conditions of Britain and the
other parts of the empire that to arrive at a
basis of union concessions must be made
somewhere. The idea of the Statist prize-
man is that the other parts of the empire
shall make all the concessions, and that
Britain shall receive them and be paid for
receiving them, which about breaks even J,
Bull’s massive record for gall and self-
assertion.
The Moral of Meagher.
N.S.W. Supreme Court has erased from its roll
the name of lawyer Meagiier. Meagher said
he suppressed his knowledge of Dean’s guilt
because it was a confidential communication
from client to solicitor. Presumably, he un-
suppressed his knowledge later on because it
was not a confidential communication from
client to solicitor, or because he came to
the conclusion that a communication from
client to solicitor w-as nothing like as privileged
as he had supposed it to be. As the communi-
cation itself didn’t show any signs of wear and
tear, Meagher’s public confession was prob-
ably due, in part at least, to a glim-
mering of the idea that a lawyer has
no more business to protect a criminal than
has any ordinary citizen. Astounding as it may
appear, this idea is perfectly correct. In fact,
a lawyer has rather less business to shield crime
than an ordinary citizen, because he is officially
supposed to be an instrument in the country’s
scheme for securing justice. He is “a member
of the Court ” ; and it is because he is a mem-
ber of the Court, and a wheel in the justice-
machine, that he is segregated from the com-
munity generally, and his profession is called
“honourable” and is closely guarded from
assault and intrusion, and he is allowed numer-
ous class privileges and advantages. There-
fore, there is a special obligation on a lawyer to
right wrong, and to clear the innocent, and to
bring the guilty to justice ; and this obligation
is still theoretically acknowledged from the
bench.
* * * *
In practice, however, the monstrous conven-
tion has been qucm-established that a lawyer
has a right to defend for a fee a person whom
he knows to be guilty, and not only to urge the
circumstances in extenuation of his guilt, but
to suppress his guilt, to deceive the Court into
a belief in his innocence—thus often throwing
the odium of guilt upon an innocent person —
and to bring public justice to nought.
That is to say, that while it is held
a disgraceful thing for a private citizen to
tell a lie, even with a good object, a lawyer
may lie openly, unblushingly, without disgrace,
even with honor, for the object of protecting a
guilty person from the community’s justice ;
that while it is a wrong thing and criminal for
a private citizen to perjure himself, like John-
son, in a court of justice—if Johnson did
perjure himself —even to secure those nearest
and dearest to him from the machinations of a
scoundrel, it is not wrong and not criminal for
a lawyer, who is specially sworn to uphold
justice, to sell his conscience for money, and
perjure himself brazenly, deliberately, per-
sistently, to advance the machinations
of a similar scoundrel; that, while it is a
private citizen’s duty not only to refrain from
committing crime or assisting the criminal, but
to denounce crime and do his utmost to bring
the criminal to justice, it is a lawyer’s privilege
to conceal his knowledge of crime, to aid and
abet the criminal, to impute falsehood to wit-
nesses whom he knows to be truthful, to im-
pute guilt to persons whom he knows to be
innocent, to do his utmost to hoodwink the
Judge, and to cheat the jury, and to clothe his
client-wolf in sheep’s clothing, and send him
forth “ without a stain on his character,” en-
couraged to commit fresh crimes by the know-
ledge that those crimes will similarly be dis-
guised, and compounded, and defended—for a
fee —by such an honorable member of “the
honorable profession ” of the Law.
* * * *
The bench, as has been said, denies the
validity of this convention; public opirion
revolts from it; yet nevertheless it exists, and
—as the excuses offered on behalf of Rofe and
Meagher more or less testify—there is a con-
tinual effort on the part of many members of
the legal profession to extend its influence and
acceptance. How many barristers take the
stand of Abraham Lincoln, and refuse to sup-
port a case unless convinced of the client’s
innocence or right ? How many would refuse
to urge a “Not Guilty” plea on behalf of a
felon whom they knew to be guilty, if the
brief was accompanied by a thumping re-
tainer? The Bulletin has had a pretty large
experience of lawyers, and it doubts if it could
find one unable to compound with his scruples
if only the retainer were big enough. Yet a
lawyer who consciously endeavors to aid a felon
to escape from justice is obviously an accessory
to his crime : if the felon does escape the lawyer
is an accessory to any crimes he may commit
thereafter; and the law rightly holds that
criminal and accessory are equally, or almost
equally, guilty. But who talks of giving a law-
yer ten years’ penal servitude when he aids a
murderer, whom he knows to be a murderer, to
save his neck or his liberty ?
* * * *
Yet even a guilty person, it is urged, has a
right to have every possible extenuating cir-
cumstance put forward in his favor. He lias ;
and the lawyer who knows his client’s guilt
may extenuate it, but can never have the right
to conceal it. Whatever his obligation to his
client, his obligation to the community is para-
mount. This is a dogmatic solution of the
ethical problem, but, if the community is to
continue, no other solution is possible. It is
true that, ethically, a man may do as he pleases.
He is under no obligation to accept majority-
standards in framing which he has not been
consulted, and which he finds it inconvenient
or irrational to observe. In the last logical
resort, he must be his own ethics-artificer. Yet
a man may not do as he pleases. Civilisation
only becomes possible by the surrender of a
portion of the rights of each for the benefit of
all. A modern community tramples many in-
dividual rights under foot—unjustly, from the
individual’s point of view ; but the only alter-
native is that the community shall itself be
trampled under foot. In the last practical ie-
sort, the community must fix the etluca
standards of the individuals who compose it.
Thus the benefit of the community is th®
final test to which the Meagher case and a


similar cases must be brought. It is to the
common standards of right and wrong that,
consciously or unconsciously, we all argue and
refer. Doubtless the community-standards are
often wrong, but as a body they represent the
best results of the experience of humanity,
and in the main they are profitable to the
greatest number of individuals. And the
lawyer, as an officer of the community, sworn
to secure its justice, is of all persons the one to
whom we look for observance of its standards.
The doctor and the priest are frequently placed
in- the lawyer’s predicament of being divided
between opposing interests and claims, aud
their position is the more difficult because more
often they have to weigh the rights of one in-
dividual against another, when only circum-
stances can guide the judgment. Where the
rights of the individual conflict with those of
the community, doctor and priest are bound
equally with the lawyer at all costs to consider
the community first. In the case, for example,
set by the Australasian Medical Gazette :
Should a doctor, under any circumstances, dis-
close the confidences of his patient ? the answer
is “ Yes, if the benefit of the community re-
quires it.” A doctor called, for example, to
attend in hiding a murderer like Deeming, and
realising whom he had to deal with, should
have no hesitation in delivering him up to
public justice. A doctor in the situation of
Dr. Playfair, discovering from a patient
something which he considers of vital impor-
tance to friend or relation, must act to the best
of his individual judgment. “ Etiquette of
the profession ” has not the paramount claim of
“ benefit of the community,” and in a strife
between the moral claims of individuals there
is not, either in theory or in practice, any obli-
gatory standard to refer to. In all other cases,
the community is bound to insist that the com-
munity comes first and the individual nowhere.
Salus populi suprema lex. And this is the
Moral of Meagher.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Judge Windeyer and the Privy Council.
Judge Windeyer has used the Colonial Office
backstairs to some purpose. S.M. Herald of
June 15 has an inspired paragraph, stating
that he has almost clutched the prize he yearns
for—a judicial seat in the Privy Council. One
thing alone is wanting —Australasia’s approval.
Singularly enough, considering Judge WiN-
deyer’s “ distinguished services,” as apparently
alleged by Premier Reid, Mr. Chamberlain
hesitates. Did the ghosts of Mount Rennie
accompany Judge Windeyer Up those back-
stairs ? Has some rumor reached the far
Antipodes of that hideous travesty of justice,
when, in the closing days of 1886, four youths,
of the average age of 18 years only, were sent to
the hangman, and five to prison for life, chiefly
on the practically unsifted evidence of witnesses
afterwards known as an arsonist and a forger,
for the offence of assaulting a girl of doubtful
character, doubtful in the identification of
her assailants? Did Mr. Chamberlain catch
a whiff of the blood which spurted from the
necks of the gibbeted lads after that mockery
of a trial during which prisoners, lawyers, and
jurymen slept from sheer fatigue—a trial which
W. B. Dalley, formerly Attorney-General of
New South Wales, Minister of the Crown, and
Privy Councillor, compared to such as
“ could only be discovered in some of the darkest pages
of a very dark period of Irish history, when counsel, to
whose hands were committed the lives and liberties of
human beings, appealed in vain to the tribunal for some
consideration, and for some regard for human weakness”—
a trial of which the Sydney Morning Herald,
the leading daily newspaper of New South
Wales, said that —
No case has been heard of in modern times in which
English judges have compelled jurors and counsel in
criminal cases to sit all through the night, in spite of re-
peated protests and requests for adjournment
It may be so : the altar of Mount Rennie still
smokes. But, as Mr. Chamberlain asks Aus-
tralasia’s opinion, Australasia has an easy
answer. We do not desire to be represented
upon the Privy Council at all. The Privy
Council jurisdiction is useless, expensive, ludi-
crous, obnoxious. Nothing but custom can
excuse the gigantic folly of dragging Austral-
asian cases to the other end of the earth for
trial, while suitors and counsel plod wearily
after them. We want Australasian justice
administered in Australasia, where the
facts and conditions are thoroughly known,
where interminable cost and delay can be
avoided. We will do nothing, by making
a quasi-endorsement of the Privy Council’s
authority, to retard the day—now close at
hand—when its jurisdiction over Australasia
shall be swept away altogether. Still less will
we pa> a tuft-liunting Judge a heavy salary to
sit in England and give decisions overturning,
or even criticising, the consensus of Supreme
Court Judges here. And least of all will we
nominate to the Privy Council Judge Win-
deyer, who time and again has, by his unique
method of conducting criminal trials, brought
the administration of alleged justice into naked
contempt, and who, of all Australian Judges
since the convict-days, with a single exception,
has proved himself the unfit test for his high
position, the„one whose acts this country desires
to hurry most quickly into oblivion.
A Divorce-Court Moral.
In a Melbourne divorce-suit last week Chief
Justice Madden laid down the rule that no
married actress can be trusted to preserve her
virtue “on tour” unless accompanied by her
husband. An actress’s husband, it seems, “ is
not to suspect his wife, but he is expected to
realise that if left without him aud the restrain-
ing influences which he could., or ought to,
exercise, she may be too frail for temptation.”
The learned Judge allowed for special induce-
ments to adultery in theatrical life. A grass
widow actress on tour“ ‘ has to travel aboutand
constantly associate by night as Well as'day
with a class of men often attractive, and not
infrequently Jax in. their views of sexual
morality.” It was here that Mr. Madden’s
reasoning proved frail. Society women-Rsso-
ciate by day and night with attractive, immoral
men who are not their husbands, travel much
in their company, and openly flirt with
them. Nevertheless, no Judge has ever
been known to discover the same excuse for
a flighty countess that Mr. Madden found
for the professional lady who went astray.
A noble earl is not considered to “contribute
to his own wrong ” when he allows his wife to
do the Continent in the company of a pleasure-
seeking “set,” or pay a round of visits to
country houses where the most accomplished of
lady-killers abound. An actor is not a more
insidious corrupter of matronly morals than
the average military captain, judging from
divorce-court records, therefore the granting of
a more special travelling allowance to actresses
than to women of another class, exposed to
equally strong temptation, is absurdly illogical,
or distinctly rough on the theatrical profession.
Probably Mr. Madden intended his judgment
to be rough on the profession, although he
didn’t say so. And he was not wrong in this.
Female virtue is chiefly the product of Mrs.
Grundy, and among communities where that
restraining influence is very little respected
or feared, the fair sex is proportionately
careless of its good name. Actors, so
far from being attractive, are posi-
tively repellent, on account of their gay-
Lothario reputation—to women who, as the
saying goes, “respect themselves.” The sup-
posed lax morality of the gaudy gentlemen
debars them from many chances of conquest
which come in the way of the average ware-
houseman, curate, or bank-clerk. Certainly it
had nothing to do with the case that Justice
Madden was discussing. The one powerful
factor in that case, which his Honor over-
looked, was Mrs. Grundy. Had he been deal-
ing with the indiscretion of a Toorak priestess
of fashion he would have granted a decree nisi
to the husband, but the respondent being an
actress the petition was dismissed. Why dis-
guise the truth ?
That Brewery Case.
On Wednesday of last week N.S.W. Assembly
set itself to straggle feebly around the case of
Tooth and Co., the Sydney brewers who were
lately accused of defrauding the revenue, and
after several hours’ straggling left the matter
very much wffiere it was at the beginning. The
present Assembly is a weak affair at the best,
and its utter incapacity to get at the real mean-
ing of anything was painfully apparent on this
occasion. Premier Reid alleged at great length
that the Collector of Customs was a man of un-
blemished character—a fact which nobody had
called in question ; and that it was absurd to
allege—which nobody had alleged—that he
would conspire with any brewery to defraud
the revenue. He also pointed out that it was
proved Tooth and Co. had, in the last four
years, paid duty on every gallon of beer shown
by their books to be sold —though a considerable
sum (,£508) was only paid after an informer had
drawn the department’s attention to certain
serious discrepancies ; which assurance was an-
other matter that had no connection whatever
with the complaint. Minister of Justice Gould
also enthusiastically alleged, when the case was
first mentioned in the Assembly, that Tooth
and Co. had paid to the Customs Department
all the duty owing by them, and that the Cus-
toms officers saw no possible reason to prosecute
because all the duty had been paid, and there
was therefore no grievance whatever. And
Sydney Daily Telegraph, in a leading article,
took up the matter in the same strain :
Two employes of the firm, after leaving its service, made
certain allegations to the effect that it had used means for
fraudulently evading the excise duty. . . . Application
was made for a criminal summons. This the magistrate
declined to issue, for the very good reason that the Cus-
toms Department, against which the fraud was alleged to
have been committed, denied that it had been defrauded.
. . . The Customs Department ought to know its own
business, &c., &c.
All of this is mainly remarkable for the manner
in which it flies off at a tangent and never gets
near the matter really at issue. The accusation
(unheard and, of course, wholly unproved so
far) was that Tooth and Co. had wrongfully
obtained a rebate of duty on a large quantity of
beer which did not go out of the brewery at
all. Therefore, when the Customs Department
declined to prosecute because the firm had paid
full duty on all the beer which did go out, and
Minister Gould indignantly refused to inter-
fere because the duty on the beer which went
out was honorably paid, and Premier Reid
whooped up the same irrelevant and well-worn
assertion, they were all religiously keeping
clear ol the real question. And when the
Daily Telegraph proudly points out that the
Customs Department denied that it was
defrauded it also dodges the fact that the fraud
which the informer said had been committed
and the fraud which the Department, according
to Minister Gould’s explanation in Parliament,
said had not been committed, were two totally
different alleged frauds. And the statement that-
the Customs office “may be expected to know its
own business ”is also wholly irrelevant. The
charge was that the drawback paid on
certain liquors which went bad and
have since been destroyed was wrong-
fully obtained, because some of the liquor
was not locally-manufactured beer. Now
that the liquor in question has disappeared
down the everlasting sink, the Customs de-
partment can’t tell more than any other out-
sider what it consisted of, except by smelling
the entry in the book ; and the persistence with
which all parties keep stating that duty was
paid on all the good liquor which was sold from
the brewery—instead of talking about the bad
liquor which wasn’t sold—is remarkable. And
the whole-souled scorn heaped on the “infor-
mers” by Premier Reid was also somewhat
misplaced, seeing that one of these informers
had so far made out a case of non-payment that
i lie Customs department charged Tooth and
Co. £5OB for three years’ back duty on a
certain line of goods upon his informa-
tion, and Tooth and Co. paid the ,£5OB
—“under protest.” But they did pay it,
and it does not appear to be on record, so far,
that they have had it refunded. When one
case was so good as all-this, the flat, refusal, of
the Government to even talk about the other
looks ridiculous. In the interests both of the
public aiid of Tooth and Co. the case should
be publicly heard in the police-court, and the
evidence fully reported in the press.
The N.S.W. Protectionist Party.
E. W. O’Sullivan, M.L.A., writes as follows: —
Dear Bulletin,— From time to time I notice in your
journal paragraphs and articles adverse to the Protec-
tionist-party of N.S. Wales. This is all the more
remarkable, considering the stand The Bulletin has
always taken with regard to Protection. Of course, I am
well aware that, while advocating protective views, you
are also in the advance guard of rational Radicalism, and
the gist of your complaints against the Protectionists of
New South Wales appears to be that, while standing up
for Australian industries, we are not abreast of the times
with regard to other progressive views.
But where, may I ask, is your warrant for the implica-
tion ? If you will run your memory back for about
10 years, you will find that almost every progressive
idea which is now before the public, or has been
legislated upon during the period referred to, has
emanated from men with protective views. For ex-
ample, it was Mr. Dibbs, a Protectionist afterwards,
who stopped the wholesale sacrifice of the public lands by
auction. In 1886 I introduced the Workshops and Fac-
tories Bill, and Dr. Renwick afterwards took up the matter
and passed it through the Legislative Assembly He was
then Minister for Education in a Government in which
were Messrs. Dibbs, Lyne, Copeland, Garvan, Suttor,
and Fletcher— all prominent Protectionists. It was a
Protectionist Ministry, led by Mr. Dibbs, which placed the
great democratic principle of “ one-man-one-vote ” upon
the statute-book, and the agitation for that law was
initiated by another Protectionist, Mr. W. H. Traill.
That Ministry also initiated the Courts of Conciliation
and Arbitration, and assisted in the passage of the Land
Boilers Bill, the extension of the Employers’ Liability
Bill to seamen, and other industrial reforms, through the
Lower House. It was the Dibbs Government that first
took up the question of retrenchment (as an adminis-
tration) after that matter and military reform had been
initiated by myself. It was a Protectionist, Mr.
Heydon, who first brought the subject of Payment
of Members before the Legislative Assembly ; and the Pro-
tectionist-party, almost to a man, supported this other
great democratic principle from start to finish. It was
Mr. W. F. Schey, a Protectionist, who introduced the
Eight Hours Bill, still before the House. Messrs. Rose,
Dowell, Walker, J. D. Fitzgerald, Gough, and O’Sulli-
van, all Protectionists, were the members who investi-
gated the proposal for a national bank, and drew up a re-
port in favor of the amalgamation of the two savings
banks as a preliminary to the establishment of such an
institution. I was the member who broached the ques-
tion of the Credit Fonder system, as well as the reduc-
tion of the charge upon selections from one shilling
to sixpence per acre, and the ring-fence around family
selections. Mr. Crick first introduced the Anti-Alien
Bill, and Mr. Willis the amendment of the Masters
and Servants Act. The Art Gallery, Public Library and
Museum were opened to the public on Sundays by Mr.
Copeland ; the village settlements were established by
the same gentleman ; mining on private property was
also legalised by him. The Agreements Validating Bill
and the Conspiracy Laws Abolition Bill came from Mr.
Willis ; the Homes Protective Bill from Mr. O’Sullivan,
and the Betterment Bill from Mr. Lyne. The Land and
Income taxes were first introduced by a Government of
which Messrs. Dibbs, Copeland, Lyne, Sutton, Fletcher
and Garvan were members. Mostly the Protectionists
have been supporters of womanhood suffrage and the
referendum. Federation was advocated by the Protec-
tionists when it was opposed by Freetraders like Messrs.
Parkes, Robertson, Reid and Want. With but one
exception, the Protectionists are in favor of a reform of
the Upper House, and their leader (Mr. Lyne) has never
linked himself with the Tory element in that Cham-
ber, but has always expressed himself as being in
favor of a reasonable alteration in the constitution of
the Legislative Council. The foregoing are but a few of
the democratic and progressive ideas ventilated by the
Protectionists of New South Wales, and it follows then
that, if they came into power, their policy would be based
upon progressive lines. It is a matter of comment among
members of the present Legislative Assembly that nearly
all the practical amendments and suggestions made there
come from the Protectionists. What I have said from
start to finish in this paragraph is merely a statement of
fact, which can be borne out by a reference to the files of
Hansard. It is because the Freetrade organs do not do
us justice in their reports that these facts are not gener-
ally known to the public of New South Wales. In a recent
leading article you were somewhat harsh in your treat-
ment of Messrs. Lyne and Copeland. It was the former
who gave the pipe contract to a N.S.W. industry,
and on every occasion when he was a Minister he en-
deavored to restrict contracts for Government work to
our own people. Mr. Copeland was the man who raised
the wages of the railway workmen, and he introduced the
proposal for the establishment of Government factories
for the manufacture of boots and clothing. Most of the
good point* about the Land Act of Mr. Carruthers also
emanated from him, for it is well known that Mr. Cope-
land had drafted a large portion of the measure before he
left office in 1894. The fact that members of an Adminis-
tration of which Mr. Lyne was a member had friends and
sympathisers in the Legislativ e Council does not imply
that Mr. Lyne is a Tory, and I think when the crisis comes
between the two Houses —as come it must—you will be
convinced of that fact.
I trust that you will publish this letter, in order that
your fellow Protectionists may not be misunderstood. It
is bad enough to be attacked by the Freetrade press, and
to have our speeches abbreviated in a ridiculous fashion
by that section of journalism, but when one of our
strongest champions unwillingly misrepresents us the
Protectionists may well wrap their mantles around them
and exclaim, “You, too, Brutus !”
Victoria’s Latest Deficit.
The new Victorian deficit, which has oeen
arrived at despite a long spell of the hardest
economy on record, makes it practically certain
that more taxation—a lot more taxation, in
fact—will have to be resorted to. Victoria is
now in the position to which all the Australias
must come sooner or later. The difference
between public solvency and insolvency in this
country depends almost wholly on the land
revenue, and Victoria, as by far the smallest
province hi Australia, has got practically to the
end of ita land revenue before any of the others.
The piibljc revenue from this source now aver-
ages about 7s. per inhabitant per annum,
against £\ 16s. in New South Wales, and
£1 7s. in Queensland. If all three pro-
vinces simultaneously stopped living on their
capital in the shape of the public estate, and
devoted the land revenue to public works pur-
poses, Or to the reduction of their debt, they
would all be, so far as current expenditure is
concerned, about equally insolvent. Therefore
it follows, if the present methods continue,
that they are likely to be all about equally
insolvent by-and-bye. There is no visible road
out of this position, except by raising more
revenue, by means of more taxation. Retrench-
ment in Victoria has got down to very nearly
the vanishing point, and, as it is impossible to
sell Jlte public lands again and have the good
old days twice over, the cold, hard expedient of
.grinding more money out of the community is the
only thing left. Maoriland, in the same situation,
struggled out of the slough of bankruptcy by
levying taxation to the extent of .£3 16s. 7d.
yearly for each inhabitant, against about £2 Bs.
in Victoria and a little less in N.S. Wales ; and
all the care and economy of the Governments
which have held office at Wellington since 1890
wouldn’t have filled the gap otherwise. Vic-
toria has now brought up short against the
same fact which Maoriland struck a good many
years ago. The only means by which a country
can be kept solvent are by wise and careful ad-
ministration, and by raising sufficient revenue,,
in the least burdensome way possible, to pay
all necessary expenses, and enough over to
cover public works expenditure. The latter
item represents the much-vaunted virtue of
Thrift in its most beautiful form, and as such
it should commend itself to all consistent Tories
—who, strangely enough, have hitherto proved
the chief obstacles in the way of any move-
ment in the right direction. Thrift in an indi-
vidual consists mostly in accumulating wealth
for his old age. Thrift in a nation also consists
in accumulating wealth for its old age—when
its goldfields are exhausted, and its public lands
are mostly sold, and the whoop and dazzle of
its early riotous days is over, and it has become
a common, ordinary country which lives, like
other old countries, on raising vegetables and
making heavy iron things in ugly factories, and
digging prosaic coal out of the bowels of
the earth. Australia has hitherto had a
vague idea that it would keep young for
ever, and that because it had lived to a
great extent in the past by selling its assets
in the shape of the public estate, it could
live that way all the days of its life. Also, it
suffers from a vague impression that it is a
heavily-taxed community which it isn’t,
though it is certainly going to be in the future,
if the public Treasury is to keep its head above
water. The cold reality of things as they are
going to be is represented by that £3 16s. 7d.
per head of annual taxation in Maoriland —
which badly wants to be raised to £4 per head
to give the Public Works Department a fair
show and to stop the still-continuous increase
of the public debt. And the one visible road
out of Victoria’s difficulties is to consider ways
and means for levying an average of 10s. or 15s.
per annum more taxation for each inhabitant.
If there is any other mode of meeting the diffi-
culty it isn’t apparent up to date.
The Ways of the Detective.
The methods of the Australian detectives led
Judge Hodges, of Victoria, to make some un-
usually straight remarks to two members of the
force the other day. The case in question was
the one known as the Canadian murder. At a
little straggling settlement near Ballarat a
woman was found hacked to death in her own
back-yard, and, in the absence of any huge
footprints leading from the spot to the residence
of some person with bloodstains on him, the
detectives arrested the two nearest residents, a
pair of old women, one of them practically
crazy. The sane old woman speedily proved
an alibi, and the case against her had to be
dropped. The ot her one hadn’t enough intellect
left to know an alibi when she saw it, so she
remained in prison. In the absence of any
shred of direct evidence against her, two
above - mentioned detectives seem to have
struck the great idea of making her sup-
ply testimony against herself, and as they
couldn’t get at her in gaol, where she wasawait-
ing trial, one of them laid a charge of vagrancy
against her, and arrested her in prison for hav-
ing no visible means of support, and hauled her
away to court nominally t» answer the charge.
Then, having got her to themselves, the crazed
old female was secretly cross-examined for an
hour or more, presumably with a view to get-
ting evidence which might hang her later on,
but failing to extract anything the charge of
vagrancy was dropped. The murder charge
also came to nothing, and the whole unscru-
pulous muddle fell to pieces. The incident
throws a lot of light on the ways of the detec-
tive. The old woman wasn’t a vagrant —she
had a fixed residence and ,£6O was found in her
possession when she w’as arrested—therefore
the detectives brought a glaringly false accusa-
tion against her to suit their own purposes, and
if that isn’t perjury it ought to be. Also, they
apparently didn’t stick at trying to hang a
deranged old woman on evidence extracted
from herself in a manner which is, or ought to
be, wholly illegal. Their defence was that they
hoped the woman would “ establish her
innocence ” under cross-examination, which
was a tolerably “steep” narrative. Her
innocence didn’t need establishing, in the
first place, there being nothing at all
against her ; and, in the second place, the ’tec
who is trying desperately to establish a
prisoner’s guilt and who holds a brief for the
prosecution, is a promising individual to estab
lish her innocence by secret examination. It
would be interesting to know the processes
adopted to obtain evidence at that secret exam.
Were they simply verbal, or was it found need-
ful to shake the aged prisoner in order to
extract information from her, and, if so, was it
necessary to shake her hard, or did the D. feel
called upon to stand upon her corn to overcome
her reticence, or how did things go? There are
so many possibilities about this kind of private
examination that these inquiries are less irrele
vant than they might appear. And if this sort
of thing is done in a city like Ballarat, how
does the ’tec run riot on the far outskirts of
civilisation where the public eye isn’t upon him ?
“ Well-out-of-it ” to The Bulletin :
There was reason to hope that the old abuses re pay-
ment of N.S.W. State-school teachers, viz., payment
according- to classification of the school, to the fluctuation
of attendance therein, to condition (married or single), to
sex—matters over which the teachers have no control —
would have been rectified by the Public Service Commis-
sioners and the only just form of payment introduced, viz. ,
by personal merit judged by the teachers’ certificates and
the result of their school examinations, but the old abuses
have only been aggravated. Thus in the highlands, where
life in winter is one perpetual shiver, a few days heavy
rain or a snow-storm causes a low average attendance for
the quarter, and the teachers’ salaries suffer probably for
the next year. Truly a dispensation of Providence ! And
how tempting to a teacher to put a few criss-crosses in his
roll-book to escape such a dispensation? Then, again,
the married men receive “quarters,” or £7O, £6O, etc.,
per annum more than a single man. Is it because the
married man is really worth so much more than his single
brother? Then there is the sex question. To be a female
is a serious sin, severely punished by the Commissioners.
The crime of being a married female is quite too heinous
to be mentioned. I would advise ambitious girls, before
entering the service, to don pants ; the Commissioners
pay a high price for trousers, irrespective of the- work
their wearers accomplish. There is one odd piece of jus-
tice in the salaries. Ex pupil-teachers, male and female,-
are to be paid alike till they gain classifications. Why c.
The married men have a grievance all to themselves
They are to be charged £7O, £6O, &c., for rent of “ quar-
ters.” Many of these quarters in country parts are mere
rookeries, hardly worth £l2 a year. Why should a poor
civil slave be compelled to pay 1 60 for the Government-
tenement, when he can rent a better house foe, perhaps
£lO or £l5 a year ? It would pay some of them to set fire-
sticks in the former as soon as convenient. The new
arrangements offer a premium to trickery, and tend to
stultify merit and honest work.
