Bijou Comic Opera.
George Darrell arose on the horizon at
Her Majesty’s last Saturday, when the “ Sunny
South ” was revived for the one-hundredth-and-
blankth time before a larger audience than H.M.’s
has seen for a long while past. Audiences have
been so thin at the big theatre in these recent
disastrous days that even the large policeman that
comes round to summon the management for over-
crowding felt that he was a satire on himself, so
Saturday’s resurrection was a joyful change, which
may or may not last. The “ Sunny South ”is an
interesting study nowadays. It is growing archaic.
Anyone who sees it now, and remembers how it
looked on its first appearance, can feel that this
country has changed a lot since Darrell's drama
was first written. People don’t talk about Aus-
tralia as the Sunny South so much as they
did. They used to call it so because it
was south of England ; now a new, un-English
generation doesn’t care a straw whether it is south
of England or not. Australia was south of Eng-
land to the old generation; to the new one England
has the dashed impertinence to be north of Aus-
tralia. Also, at the time Darrell wrote, the digger
and the bushranger were the beginning, middle,
and end of Australian romance ; the big, hulking
nugget and the “ claim ” were the centre of Aus-
tralian literature ; and local coloring consisted of
the glaring red shirt of Bill or Jim. Now the
nugget is practically extinct; and the digger’s shirt
is any color he can get; and the claim is down
2176 ft., so that the limited liability co. has to let
Bill down by machinery ; and Jim is floating wild
cats in London. No one writes dranas like the
“Sunny South ” now, or feels like them either, and
the sight of it is like a pleasant whiff of a past that
has been outlived very suddenly.
* * ♦ *
Darrell’s drama, at its worst, is about as
good as the average melo-howl, and the proceed-
ings are not more wildly impossible than usual,
and Darrell, as the hero, doesn’t spread his legs
and wave his revolver in the free atmosphere of
Australia any more than the ordinary hero. The
drama is lavishly staged, too, and sundry
respectable performers—notably, Misses Gladys
Leigh, Wilde and Edith Barrow, Harry Leston,
Robert Inman, Hodson and Charles Hall—sup-
port the noble-souled G.D. to some good purpose.
The story being as badly tangled up as the
average, The Bulletin doesn’t profess to be
quite certain which is Miss Wilde and which is
Inman, but these are mere details. The general
whoop and bellow and glare are all there, and
there are a number of large wild Australian effects
which are calculated to make the kangaroo collect
his tail in affright and never stop running till he
gets to Thargomindah.
** * X
The Brough-Boucicault co. filled in two
nights this week with the ever-amusing “ Pass-
port,” and, while the co. in general shone as much
as usual, Majeroni, as the stupendous Russian
policeman, filled his part even better than afore-
time. Majeroni’s vast figure (he is so tall that he
has to ta,ke a week off to see to the top of himself)
fits in with any kind of uniform, and he is de slop-
ing into a very good actor with many different kinds
of broken English at command. The co. afterwards
took another turn at “Mrs. Tanqueray,” and the
last three nights of the season—Thursday, Friday,
and Saturday—are allotted for one-night outbreaks
of “The Idler,” “The Magistrate,” and “The
Amazons” respectively. The last is for Bouci-
cault’s farewell benefit, and a week ago d.c. seats
for this event were already unattainable, and stalls
were almost as scarce as ripe corn on a gallows.
Next Monday the Brough comedy co. starts for
Queensland, and there will be only one B. in this
favorite combination for the future.
* * * *■
The “ Trilby ” season at Sydney Lyceum
is to last till Friday of next week, and on Satur-
day (27th) Hoyt’s company opens in “ A Trip to
Chinatown.” At the Royal “The Derby Winner”
is still doing sufficiently good business to justify
its continuance, and no change is announced up to
date.
** * *
On Saturday night a new performer, one
William Gill, made his appearance at Sydney
Tivoli. He did not appear on the programme in
the usual way, but fell out of the gallery on top of
a poor fellow named Alfred Neander, whose back
was broken by the concussion, and who, as this is
written, lies dying in the hospital. Charles Cogill
has joined the Rickards Co., and this rich and
mellow comedian takes well as usual. Godfrey,
at time of writing, was doing his last week.
Altogether, the Tivoli programme has been more
varied than usual.
*****
A lot of shows are finishing, or have just
finished, their career in Sydney—for the present.
The Fitzgeralds propose to pack up their large
and deserving circus at the end of the week.
Friday’s show will be for the benefit of the
Benevolent Asylum, and practical charity calls
on the public to roll up and support Mahomet and
the lady who falls from the roof all it can. That
brilliant orator, the Rev. Charles Clark, has
finished his series of lectures—unless he decides,
after this gets into print, to himself for a
night or two longer. Parson Haskett Smith, the
man who discourses upon “the East” as one
having authority, is advertised to lecture on
“ Jerusalem ” at the Cyclorama for the last time
to-night (Thursday), and his final departure is
shortly expected. Also Kennedy, the fat show-
man with the stony eye and the great circum-
ference, has raised hopes that he will soon close up
his candle-and-grease-eating competition at the
Protestant Hall, and cease to run pins into his
more or less hypnotised “ subject,” and roll him-
self laboriously over the edge of the horizon in an
eastward direction.
* * * *
A musical season begins at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall next Wednesday, when the English
Concert Co. makes its first appearance. The
stars of this combination are Misses Thudichum
and Marian M‘Kenzie, Edward Branscombe and
Douglas Powell. Miss Thudichum’s photo,
dosn’t look at all like her name, (pronounced
“ Toodikum ”) and all four singers come here with
a long list of favorable British notices to back up
their pretensions.
* * * *
“ As You Like It,” at Melb. Princess’s, is
neither a definite success nor a distinct failure,
financially. Neglected by the great bulk of frivol-
ous playgoers, it appeals to that gloomsome section
of humanity which never attends a theatre unless
Immortal William be laid on. Last Saturday
there was a morning service of Shakespeare,
whereat the saints mustered in such goodly force
that the sinners, professional and otherwise, cast
furtive, uncomfortable glances at their neighbors,
as though they had got into a place of worship
by mistake. These brief seasons of “legitimate”
drama, The Bulletin fancies, ought to be con-
ducted by R. S. Smythe, who is more identified
with true religion than are W. and M., and better
calculated to boom devotion to the Bard Divine.
“ As You Like It ” is under the patronage of the
Nonconformist Conscience.
* * * *
Gazing through the glamour of Mrs.
Potter’s Rosalind, one finds very little persuasive-
ness in the lady’s histrionic art. Mrs. Potter
seems charmingly absurd, not to say winsomely
preposterous, for the most part. She elocutes
affectedly, yet without a settled policy of speech,
such as the old-school declaimers brought to bear
upon their patient audience. She ranges from a
golden drone to a silvery gabble when there is no
conceivable cause for going to extremes either
way. But the fact that nobody cares to “guy”
Mrs. Potter’s eccentricities speaks for itself. She
is fascinating, also very much in earnest, and fas-
cinating earnestness strikes the scoffer dumb,
otherwise she would soon be up a tree
in the Forest of Arden. The idea of
several sane people (including a Dresden china
shepherdess) accepting this kitteney Rosalind, this
intensely feminine vision in a fantastic costume,
as anything other than a girl is against all reason.
Her sex flaunts itself. She mocks at her own
assumption of manhood with every trick of her
oval eyes and shake of her bonnie back-hair. In
short, Mrs. Potter’s performance is mainly an
audacious burlesque of W. Shakespeare’s inten-
tion, unless all the rest of the world has been m;s-
taking the late poet’s intention for ages past. The
secrets of William’s works die vvith him, of course.
Maybe he was merely poking borak at the future
generations which were going to take his plots
seriously. We don’t know.
* * * •
Kyrle Bellew confines himself, as of yore,
to an imitation of Irving’s mannerisms—-in fila-
gree. He never loses consciousness of his elegant
stage appearance, or forgets to bring it right under
public notice, and this journal—remembering how
many fat, ill-favored, unintelligent politicians live
by striking abominable attitudes on every plat-
form where they can get a footing—has no
complaint against Bellew. Nature has made
nim up for sentimental-lover characters, and
he acts accordingly, likewise cleverly. He
is a thing of beauty in the first place, and
an actor afterwards, and notwithstanding that
he sighs to excess, and lays on the tenderness
with an electro-plated trowel, so to speak, there is
often a touch of sincerity in the pretty picture.
The Celia of Miss Lynda Raymond, who is. excep-
tionally natural, deserves much praise, and the
musical foresters, especially the lady soloist, can
be commended for having got into tune since the
first night.

For Saturday the French revolutionary
play of “ Charlotte Corday ” is announced, and a

* * * *
The last nights of “Silver King,” at the
Royal, were marked by a rapid improvement in
King Hedley and Stirling Whyte, the new Denver
and Jaikes, and so the finish of this capital show
was equal to its commencement in all respects.
“ Silver King ” might as well have stayed for the
remaining nights of manager Westmacott’s season,
in the interests of melodramatic art as well as for
the sake of a few people who have grown to love
Bodger, the baker. Bodger aforesaid never
becomes visible in the flesh, but spiritually he is
the livest man in the play, and his squint is every-
where regarded as an absolute fact. “ The Points-
man,” which was to succeed “Silver King” on
Wednesday, can only run nine nights. After
which Maggie Moore and a burlesque of “Trilby.”
* * * *
The unerring vigor of the Frantz Family
acrobats has carried Melbourne by storm, and
the Opera House is crowded every time. An in-
evitable question re the female element in
this galaxy of skill and muscle is whispered
in sundry places, but doubters can take
it for granted that the three active and
intelligent amazons are as genuine as they appear.
They are even said to be three bona-fide sisters, the
terribly strong one having given herself in mar-
riage to the well-balanced gentleman who asserts
his sovereignty by standing on her head.
#- * # *
A Trebelli matinee at Melb. Town Hall
last Saturday was so joyfully received that the
“star ” undertook to farewell yet once again on
Monday of this week—ere leaving for Sydney.
Mdlle. Trebelli’s visit proved a thorn in the flesh
of the opposition “ New English Concert Com-
pany,” a quartette of pleasing singers who have
found the Hall much too large for their reputation.
The N.E.C.C. comprises a dashing, resonant bari-
tone (Douglas Powell), a light contralto (Miss
Marian M’Kenzie), a shrilly-sweet soprano, and a
modest tenor. Collectively they give satisfaction,
but their season pines for the want of the one pre-
eminent warble which alone can draw the public
in these times of trouble.

At Poverty Point.
One of Mrs. Potter’s devices for concealing her —er—
limb-deficiency in Rosalind’s male-attire is a strange, un-
tidy effect, as of stocking coming down. This, together
with the vague grotesqueness of her shaggy coat—it can’t
be called a doublet - gives away the fact that the slender
lady is trying to hide her natural disqualifications for the
part. And yet Mrs. Potter is a very successful Rosalind
at times, even in the Forest of Arden. The soul is
mightier than the leg.
Paradis: “ Mrs. Potter reminds you of the shilling-
table at Foy and Gibson’s —a scrap of gorgeous old brown
lace, a tag of rich purple velvet, a bit of fichu, a morsel of
guipure, an edge of a foulard, a snip off a bridal-veil, a
fragment of chiffon, all the thousand and one trifles so
alluring to the kleptomaniac, but you couldn’t make
the shilling-table into one whole of anything. She
is one of the paintings of Mieris, of yore, or
Carolus Duran of to-day, called the ‘ Portrait of a
Lady.’ A vague, blank impossibility, of lovely combina-
tion in glints, picked, on the Albert Durer system, from
every rainbow dab of the palette. Take a woman of
Millais, and you see at least a glorified reality. However,
Mieris is ever moist, a tempting peach to the bite, and so
is Cora Urquhart Potter, a damson jam-pot, in blue
bric-a-brac.”
Paradis: “ Bellew mellows into a good sort of actor,
though, as with Rignold, the bloom, gloss, and aroma,
which enchanted the New York ladies, are gone for ever.
Rignold’s Claude Melnotte, and Bellew’s Romeo, with
Harry Montague in something else, are the unapproach-
able traditions of the N. Y. matinees, almost reaching the
dignity of lectures to Women Only, with the circles
crammed in proud flesh. What a tittupy throng, at the
stage door, to see Bellew drive off, all furred, in the dash-
ing britska he then sported ! He is sacrificed, nowadays,
to Mrs. P., who obviously directs the repertory, and
Bellew only plays up.”
The “Ben Bolt” tableau in “Trilby” is about the
most natural and best-sustained silent effect ever staged.
Intensely dramatic, yet quite unforced, it carries the
story along for fully two minutes without a word or
gesture. Ordinarily, the song or instrumental music
heard from behind the scenes has no real connection with
the play, or, in any case, it seems to be introduced for the
sake of effect. The song in “Trilby” comes straight
from the soul of the plot, and reveals the pathos of the
tableau which it creates. That is to say, reveals and
appeals to intelligent persons among the audience. About
one person in every 20 has a proper sense of the situation
in all its bearings. The other 19 are merelj’ conscious of
what they hear and see—the voice of an unseen singer,
and four motionless figures on the stage. If the song
were “ Sweet Marie,” and the stage were deserted in the
meantime, they would be quite as much impressed.
On their first Sunday in Melbourne this trip Rosalind
Potter and Orlando Bellew spent the afternoon out-of-
doors—she, with a female attendant, driving a dog-cart,
and he beside her on a bike.
“ Acomparison of Mrs. Brown-Potter’s Rosalind with that
of Mrs. Langtry,” writes a Melb. lady to The Bulletin,
“is all to the advantage of the latter. Both are beautiful
women, but one remembers the Jersey Liiy as not only a
lovely but a satisfactory Rosalind. One example comes to
mind, showing the artistic difference of the two women’s
performances. In the passage with Orlando, in which
Rosalind, in her boy’s clothes, speaks of dwelling ‘ here
in the skirts of the forest, like fringe upon a petticoat,’
Mrs. Langtry used to suddenly remember her lack of
skirts, and, glancing down, coyly draw her cloak around
her legs with a very pretty affectation of sudden shyness.
Mrs. Potter sees no such chance in the words. She just
spreads out her arms and indicates “ the skirts of the
forest ’in a general sort of way. Yet such touches reveal
the actress—or the artist.” Which is certainly the first
time Tiie Bulletin ever heard Mrs. Langtry accused of
being an artist, even comparatively speaking.
Mrs. Brown-Potter, in her latest gown (by Worth, of
Paris) as represented in Melbourne weeklies, bears a
startling resemblance to a half-peeled banana.
Brown-Potter on the stage is a constant reminder of
David Copperfield’s Aunt yelling at Uriah Heep : “ For
heaven’s sake ! man, don’t be galvanic.” Potter is gal-
vanic and angular, all knees and elbows, she prances
through her part like a plucked crane, and yet she has an
undeniable charm, and most men would be eontent to be
clay in the hands of the Potter.
S.S. : “Bellew as Orlando gives me the impression of
never forgetting his legs ; everything in the character is
made subordinate to those slim, ineffable legs. Even the
beauteous Potter herself is sacrificed before the gilded
calves as they wriggle and posture and fairly bellow for
admiration all through her most oleaginous speeches.
When Kyrle turns up his eyes and apostrophises the
divine Rosalind in the gorgeous diction of the poet of
poets, he deludes nobody ; it is obvious to the meanest
understanding that he is thinking of his legs all the time
and striving with great cunning to display them to the
best advantage. I am convinced that if Bellew’s legs
were cut off short at the butts he would make an excellent
actor on the stumps, but he will never be a convincing
pro. whilst he has a leg to stand upon.”

New York Dramatic Mirror upon Miss Olga Nether-
sole’s osculatory effects: —“ The length to which the
actress went in depicting the passion Carmen is supposed
to feel for her various lovers approached in some instances
the ridiculous, and was the cause of considerable laughter
In view’ of the number and duration of the kisses she
bestows, it is not hard to understand why the three men
who impersonate the parts of her lovers have clean-shaven
upper-lips. An ordinary moustache subject to such
treatment would soon be reduced to a shapeless hirsute
mass. It is no exaggeration to assert that if Miss Nether-
sole were to reduce the duration of her kisses one-half
the performance would be over considerably more than a
half-hour before midnight.”

Three Australian vocalists in London are said to be by
no means up on their luck—Gladstone Wright, Wood and
Stockwell. The latter, who was a sweet, sympathetic

of production by more or less losing his voice.
Leumane was not wattle-farming all the days of his re-

commercial-minded tenor sold at a good profit. Since his
return to the comic-opera business, Leumane is dieting
himself for the sake of his stage outline, and before leav-
ing Melbourne with the co. he had got rid of 141 b. avoir-
dupois.
Apropos Judge Madden’s refusal to grant a man a
divorce because he had contributed to his wife’s fall by
allowing her to travel with persons of loose morals (to
wit, the males of a variety-co.) thus Sydney D.T. “ Put
it the other way sexually, and substitute man for woman.
Suppose a wife sued for divorce and the husband pleaded
that he hadn’t been all he should be because he had to
earn his living by theatrical work, consequently was
forced into association with people who were attractive
personally, but exceedingly weak as to morals. That man
would be beaten at the trial. The Judge would take the
opportunity to make scathing remarks about his brutal
infidelity to his wife, and still more brutal excuse for it.
Yet it’s precisely the same case as the one tried at Mel-
bourne. There is only one way of justifying the court’s
decision, which is by assuming that woman is weaker in-
tellectually than man—so much weaker that it would be a
mistake to give her the franchise.”

Fitzgeralds’ Circus Combination has been engaged for
the next Jewish Carnival at Melb. Exhibition Building.
The Carnival, which intends to run three weeks on a pro-
digious scale of splendor, is not due until the middle of
October. Meantime, the Fitzes will do Bananaland after
quitting Sydney.
The N.S.W. Civil Service is a very nervous
combination just now. Every second man has a
dreadful cold on the chest caught through waiting
outside the Government Printing-office on damp
evenings to get the very first number of the
Gazette with his retrenchment notice in it; and
the rest have snuffles in the head through being
wakened by the casual goat in the yard at 2 a.m.
and rushing down under the impression that it
was the Government come to raise their salaries.
The Commissioners’ unfortunate reputation for
industry is partly the cause of this ; they are com-
monly supposed to work night and day, and until
the numbers are really up no man knows whether
he won’t be promoted suddenly at midnight, or
retrenched at 1 a.m., or have his salary raised
about 4.30 in the morning. The excitement is
something awful, and the Service has got the
jumps so badly that it can’t even shave itself with
safety.
The only point of value in connection with
the Reid-Tozer invitation to the Barrier was
wholly forgotten by the wrangling legislators.
How much does the trip to Broken Hill stand
N.S.W. in cash ? By foolish precedent a Minister
of the Crown is given carle blanche while travel-
ling, so that the item, even if Reid only played
host to Adelaide, is worth knowing. (They do
say that the Premier paid all his own expenses out
of his private purse.) When Jacob Garrard
tripped to Maoriland a question like this was
asked, but was diplomatically postponed and sub-
sequently dropped.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The English, Scottish and Australian bank’s
second reconstruction scheme—which in its original shape
was about the most superlatively cheekv thing in the
reconstruction line—is now put before the creditors in a
very much modified form. According to this latest pro-
posal, 40 per cent. (£356,582) out of the 4 per cent, deposits,
which fall due between 19,10 and 1904 is to be repaid at
once, and the rest (£534,872) is to be turned into perpe-
tual 4 per cent, stock, payable when the bank feels like
it, and at no other time. The present 4 per cent, perpe-
tual debentures remain as they were. As to the £2,023,965
of per cent, stock, one-half of this is to bear 3 per
cent, interest in the future ; and the other half,
though nominally a 3 per cent, stock, is to bear
interest at any rate from nothing up to 4J4 per cent,
according to the bank’s earnings. If the bank doesn’t
prosper it may get nothing. If it does prosper this second
half of the old 4£ per cent, stock will get 3 per cent, in-
terest in the first place ; then £15,000 a year is to go to a
reserve for the better security of the stockholders, and
any remaining surplus is to go towards raising the interest
to the original 41 per cent. Therefore the bank saves
£l4,263perannum by repaying part of the 4 per cent, stock.
It also saves £15,179 per annum for certain by reducing
interest on one-half of the inscribed stock from 4£ to 3 per
cent. And it may save anything up to £45,539 per annum
on the other half according to the interest paid, so that
the total interest saving is anywhere between £29,442 and
£74,981 per annum according to circumstances. The con-
sent of the creditors is alleged to be practically assured,
so the E.S. and A. seems likely to get rid of a good part
of its present burden.
* * * *
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Co. (Sydney), which has been struggling with pretty
hard circumstances for some time, pays a dividend for the
half-year at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum, and
begins to look better. It has now a reserve-fund of £6BOO
and a bank balance of £1957, as compared with an over-
draft of £4837 a year ago ; and £3OOO has lately been
written off the assets. Liabilities have been reduced from
£79,000 in 1891 to £19,258. At the same time, the net
profits for the half-year are only £1960, and as the divi-
dend of 10 per cent, absorbs £1584 of this, there is only
£376 to carry forward. And the profits are mostly froiii
the business done in Westralia, which is a country where
the bottom is apt to fail out of things very suddenly ;
therefore the Intercolonial would do well to go slow till
it gets somewhat stronger. Eight per cent, should be
enough to satisfy any reasonable individual.

A person named William Stokes Cottew writes
from Tottenham (England) to the Vic. Government that
the idea of a State Bank “is my copyright for England
and the Australasian colonies, and was copyrighted as far
back as Sept., 1895.” If the Vic. Government starts a
State Bank W. Stokes Cottew requires that he shall be the
first President at £5OOO a year for five years, £3OOO a year
afterwards until he retires from old age at 65, and after
that a life pension of £BOO a year. And if a State Bank
is started without his permission W. Stokes Cottew pro-
poses to raise legal Cain and tear up this country by the
roots. The Bulletin hereby returns a verdict that Wil-
liam Stokes C. appears to have a fine large copyright.

The Maoriland poll-tax of £5O a head on the
foreign bagman added £3400 to the revenue last year.
* ♦ * »
The principle that unclaimed moneys in banks
and other financial institutions, and unclaimed dividends
of all descriptions, should be the property of the State, is
gradually obtaining recognition. South Australia has
already enforced the principle, and the second year that
the Act was in operation it produced a net revenue of
over £lO,OOO. On the basis of population, a similar Act,
strictly enforced, should pan out, in Victoria or N.S.
Wales, for at least £20,000 a year as a steady income—a
considerable item of revenue. Maoriland last year passed
an Unclaimed Money Bill through the Assembly, but the
Council threw it out by a majority of three. The Vic.
Government will probably make an attempt at an Un-
claimed Money Bill shortly; £20,000 or £30,000 a year is
something just now in the land of the perennial deficit.
* * * *
Wright, the Union Bank clerk lately gaoled in
Sydney for embezzlement, had been 13 years a bank
official, and his salary was £135 a year, less £5 deducted
towards the guarantee fund. Apparently he started on
a net salary of £0 per annum, and had been raised £lO a
year ever since. The unhappy wretch couldn’t have done
much worse if he had “ gone on the land.”

The Phoenix, one of the innumerable caravan of
foreign fire insurance offices doing business in Australia,
sends along a balance-sheet for 1895. From this document
it appears that the capital is £268,380 : the total assets,
rather over £1,600,000; and that the available profit at
the end of last year was £148,208.

Col. North, it is cabled, died worth only half-a-
million. A few years ago, it was believed, he turned the
scale at six millions, and he has been taking off weight
ever since. He had more faith in Australian mines than
the money he made out of them. The sudden cutting out
of the Londonderry staggered him, and he took the
chance of growling savagely at three of the discoverers who
happened to be m London spending some of his gold.
Harry Dawson stood up to him, sympathising with his
misfortune but declining to admit any fault.

Weekly statements of B.H. Proprietary output
having become little better than rough computations, the
directors have determined to henceforward make known
the product only once a month. The report for the
future will be in effect a refinery statement, as the whole
of the product now goes through this branch of the co s
works. For some time to come the output will be reduced,
as it is officially announced, for about the 20th time, that
“ it is inadvisable to continue extracting the oxidised
ores at a high rate.”
What the B. H. Co. proposes is toerectalarge con-
centrating works, capable, with the present plant, of dealing
with about 10,000 tons crude sulphides weekly, and to use
the lead concentrates for blending with the oxidised ores,
of which it is estimated 1J million tons still remain in the
mine. The zinc concentrates are to be “reserved for
future treatment ” —in the indefinite period when a satis-
tactory process is offered to the co. The Ashcroft and
llowell (lllawarra) people don’t suit for the purpose, as so
tar they, as buyers only of ore straight out, want to make
the profit. The present announcement is afrankacknow-
lu i'i'ifH*' though “ it is now proved beyond question
that the B. H. sulphides can be profitably worked,” the
best available methods of treatment are merely tentative.
Concentration and the subsequent treatment of the lead
product alone signify at the most the immediate saving of
55 or 60 per cent, of the total value of the metallic con-
tents of the ore. Certainly the zinc product is “ being
reserved for future treatment; ” but they would be ven-
turesome directors who would, in their statement of
assets, take credit for a dump Of these tailings or slimes.
The English holder of B.H. Proprietaries seems
to get hold of earlier information about the mine and its
prospects than the Australian holders can. There is little
doubt that it was on the strength of a coming reduction
in output that some English holders let out scrip several
weeks back. On the whole, though, it is doubtful if on
the circular issued to shareholders any fall is justified.
Divs. may be occasionally passed (the extreme probability
of that was officially stated six months back), but the
statement as to the quantity of oxidised ore remaining—
H million tons —is far more satisfactory' than the last pre-
ceding statements of the kind justified one in expecting.
Certainly it may not be all good smelting ore ; anyway',
the lead contents —average, 12 per cent. —are comparatively
small, and for blending it is good lead stuff that is wanted.
The B.H. directors also state that the sulphide
ore explored above the 400 ft. level north of Jamieson’s
shaft, and above the 500 ft. south of this point—about mid-
way along the property—amounts to 2,019,000 tons, with
a probable 600,000 tons only partly explored. This ore
has been carefully sampled, knd assay results show an
average of 24 - 3 per cent, lead, 21 "9 zinc, and 18’5oz. silver.
A very'good ore indeed. That sulphide upon which the
South has been operating contains from 30 to 40 per cent,
lead, from 16 to 20 per cent, zinc (the more zinc the worse
for present purposes, generally speaking), and soz. toBoz.
silver. Block 14 began on ore containing from 20 to 24
per cent, lead, 20 to 28 per cent, zinc, and 12oz. to 20oz.
silver. But, for all that, the directors’ statement might
be more satisfactory. This is, it will be seen, a statement
of average assays : what it would be better to know is the
quantity of ore containing more than, say, 20 per cent,
lead, 12oz. silver, and not more than 20 per cent, zinc—or
whatever the grade is under which profit is impossible
under present known and available methods of treatment.
What, though, can have been the object of an-
nouncing that such-and-such a quantity of ore of a given
average grade had been developed above this 400 ft.-level ?
(The 500 ft.-level south of Jamieson’s,is, by the wa.v, not
500 ft. deep, on account of this section of the mine occu-
pying a natural depression). Comparatively speaking,
and so far as the sulphides are concerned, 400 ft. is the
surface. And assuming a consumption of 10,000 tons
sulphides weekly, the quantity stated—about 2) million
tons—would last only five years. Why' not a word of re-
ference to the sulphides lying Underneath—those “prac-
tically unlimited millions” of which so many eloquent
things have been said? We have got so used to directors
reminding us of the cheerful features of a case that, when
they neglect to do so, we sniff suspiciously. That, at any
rate, is what the public have been doing; hence a small
slump in all Barrier stock.
The Ashcroft and Howell co’s may be able to
treat the sulphides, extracting front them practically the
whole of the meta.l, But, as before mentioned, they are
not content to run mere custom-works. This is the point
of view of those who favor the sale of the South. “ Quite
likely,” they say, “the mine is worth more than the
£240,000 which will probably be the price agreed upon.
But what is the use of the mine if we have to sell our con-
centrates for a few shillings a ton to people who make
almost as many pounds out of them ? ” The processes are
patented, in short that is the trouble. And no doubt
the sale of sulphide ore and concentrates has not gener-
ally been satisfactory The South, after over two and a-
quarter years’ operations with the mill, has paid no div.
The Junction has paid nothing commensurate with either
the quality or quantity of the ore extracted. Block 14
began to pay dividends when it began to buy ore largely.
So far, the buyers have had the best of the bargain ; and
the companies which long gave the cold shoulder to
private experimentalists, and even to the experimentalists
among their own officers (Ashcroft, for instance, who was
at length obliged to take his laboratory to his private
house !) have themselves to thank for it mostlv.
* * * *
In his annual report the N.S.W. Inspector of
Mines placards the B.H. Block 10 and Central mines for
doing “ scarcely anything in compliance with the (Lead
Poisoning) Act. This is especially the case,” he adds,
“ in regard to changing-house and washing and bathing
accommodation ; and I am of opinion that strong mea-
sures will have to be taken before the companies named
will comply with the Act in this particular.” Can these
be the same companies whose directors but lately stood
aghast at their miners’ alleged disregard for “law’norder ” ?
Are you listening, Premier Reid ?
Eighty-eight cases of lead-poisoning reported to
N.S.W. Mines Department from May 1 to December 31 last
year. The co’s which make these reports bring the
figures as low as possible by discharging men who have
once been leaded, and who, in consequence, are predis-
posed to the affection. Hence the steady importation of
men from Victorian fields. Are you listening, Premier
Turner ?
It cost the B.H. last year If lives for every div.
paid, besides 4 non-fatal accidents.
* * * *
Prospect of an early call is “ knocking ” the
North B.H. market, although the crowd is doing its
level best to keep up the scrip.
* * * *
To produce .£1,315,929 worth of gold in N.S.W.
last year 21,343 men, including 833 Chinese, labored. The
average value of gold per man was thus about £6l 10s.
To win the £1,644,563 worth of silver and lead mined and
exported from Broken Hill 4150 men were employed,
giving an average of £396 10s. per man. The value of
machinery employed was approximately the same. But
the coke, timber and coal charges against the gold-mining
industry are infinitely smaller than in the other case—a
circumstance which the wage-working silver-miner is apt
to forget when he enters on a crusade against the silver-
mining co.

Jerusalem Creek, N.S.W. Better extraction is now being
got by the use of caustic soda. A large percentage of old
tailings are being re-treated.

has been made by Gillan and party at Hargraves. Rich

* * * •
“ Cobar is full of experts aud speculators, but in
the majority of cases they are looking for certainties, and
are afraid of speculations.” The writer can quite imagine
the kind of specs, the Cobar ducks would like to work off.
There are good mines at Cobar but, so far, they can all be
counted on the fingers, and the chance of finding any
more in the immediate vicinity is remote. Cobar could
attract a lot of Bull’s money, but for a mine bringing, say,
two or three shillings a share on the market—which is,
after all, the only gauge of value—they usually want six
or eight shillings to sell out.
* « * *
Gorgeous descriptions continue to reach Aus-
tralia of the magnificence in which Mr. Frank Gardiner,
erstwhile well-known in this country as a successful silver-
speculator and also as the spouse of Miss Carrie Swain, is
living in the world’s hub. The marble-faced, black-eyed
Frank is a born poker-player, and could pose at any time
as the impassive Indian Sitting Bull. This paper regrets
to hear for the sake of N.S. Wales that that big venture,
the Brown’s Creek Co., which appears to be mainly Mr.
Gardiner’s, has not turned up trumps, the bottom of the
mine having proved infernally hard and poor to match ;
while it has been found impossible to treat the mud of the
upper ground with the heavy stamps sent from England
—they have been beating the bottoms right out of the
boxes.
* * * »
There is in Sydney an organising genius who
has shown himself in many spheres to be the possessor of
an extremely level head. Some of his big schemes have
come off absolutely, others haven’t. One of his boldest
proposals was during the Boom, when he took steps in
the direction of effecting a gigantic combine among all
the big dailies of Australia. When he sought, for a start,
to combine the S.M. Herald and the Sydney D.T., the
Herald people wouldn’t bite.
* * * »
The Great Eastern Co’s lease at an island in the
Yarra, at Warrandyte, has been declared forfeited by the
Vic. Minister of Mines on the application of a party of
working men, for breach of labor conditions. “ Unlock
the Victorian goldfields ” might well be made a political
plank in mining districts, for the area of shepherded land
is enormous, and tends to make Victoria, whatever its
gross gold-yield, by no means a poor man’s field. Apathy
on the part of miners is responsible for much of the
abuse, for the labor conditions are usually stringent. The
success in this case, up to the present, should induce
“ jumpers ” to perform a real public service in other
directions. The forfeiture was preceded by an unsuccess-
ful attempt to bringabout an amalgamation of theopposing
interests.

* * * *
The Mt. Hepburn estate gold-mine has reached
London at last. Many a futile effort has been made to
get it there. Capital £200,000. The object as set out is
“ to carry on the business of a mining, milling, smelting
and metallurgical co. in all or any of its branches, and to
lay out towns and villages, and to promote immigration.”
It is a cheerless-looking hole stoped out to a depth of 300
or 400 ft., and has long remained unworked because —well,
it doesn’t look quite as good as the Long Tunnel. Now it
is self-condemned : “ To promote immigration ! ”
• * * *
Nearly every Vic. mine is for sale—at a price.
Recently arrived representative of several British mining
corporations had 40 claims offered him in three days, ana
crowds of “shepherds” are hustling for his address. By
the time he quits Vic. the visitor will have a few tons of
cheap options.
* * * *
Smellbourne’s small French community will
provide a big wash of dirty linen in public sometime next
month when the case of The Alliance and Liberty Co. v.
Russell comes to court. This is an action arising from
the miraculous sale of a Westralian gold-mine in London,
and subsequent repudiation of the deal by the syndicate
that sent the salesman ’Ome. As a lesson in Franco-
Melbournian sentiment re gold-mine ventures and values,
and things in general, the A. and L. Co. v. Russell will be
found very interesting. By the way, Matthew Davies will
here make his first professional appearance in any r big law-
suit. Matthew is engaged for the defence of the
mounseers against the furious London company.
* * * *
Adelaide men are doing as well out of the W.A.
boom as they.did out of the Barrier silver rise. C. H. De
Rose, who owns valuable Kalgoorlie town lots and is a
very big holder of Great Boulders, was formerly a little-
known Adelaide land-agent. He was Brookman’s mate
when they, with Pearce, pegged the Boulder.

W. A. Horn (“Lord Tin-ore”) Kintore’s mil-
lionaire friend, C. G. Millar, W. R. Wilson, S. R. Wilson,
J. Reid, and three other of the Horn brothers, compose
the “ Octagon ” syndicate which has had a big say in the
Western boom, and owns most of the Menzies properties.
It was W. A. Horn, by the way, who pegged out the
famous Wallaroo (S. A.) copper-mine, which turned out a
big thing in the old S.A. copper days.
* * * *
On the inside front cover of this issue appears
the prospectus of the Sir John Franklin G.M. Co., Wood’s
Point, Vic. The co. has a 221 a. lease in the famous Wood’s
Point district, which yielded fabulous returns in that
period vaguely described as the “ early days ” and then
was practically abandoned, and which is now resurrecting
with prospects of future success. The mine has a good
record, and the capital is strictly moderate.
* * * *
It is not the batteries that are coming to a field
but the batteries which are up that tell the story. Here
are a few around Coolgardie : Mt. Burgess, 15 heads, no
returns for many months; Bendigo Coolgardie, erected
nearly two years, 15 heads, never had one payable crush-
ing; Bayley’s Reward, 20 head, shut up ; Golden Bar, an
Otis ball mill (equal to 10 heads), nothing to crush ; Em-
press of Coolgardie, 10 heads, never had anything to
crush ; Tindall’s 10 heads, mine working, battery idle.
The list could be extended if necessary.
The impudence of the Westralian governing
ring knows no courts. In a mining case, heard at Kal-
goorlie last week, the warden is reported to have said
that “he had received instructions not to receive any
more appeals.” It was pointed out that the Mining Act
specially provided for appeals, and the warden then said :
—“ lam advised that I am to sit ministerially, and not
judicially, and that my recommendations are dealt with
by the Governor-in-Council, and that there can be no
appeal. I don’t agree with the instructions that have
been sent to me, but they definitely instruct me that I am
to allow no appeals to the Supreme Court.” Apparently,
the Govt, is cancelling an Act of Parliament to suit its
own pleasure, or convenience, or mining interest. They
are a long-suffering lot in Westralia.

Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, 230 tons, 5370 z. These
shares have bounded up on account of the richness of a

to be the Boulder lode it is a poor look-out for the Boulder
northern blocks about to be floated in England, and from
which big pup divs. are expected.
* * * *
The Sunday tipster has had a slight fall. On
Sunday he continued to strongly advise the buying of
scrip in Mt. Malcolm, a W.A. mine floated in Sydney and
which, by report, lately struck a 13ft. reef going lOoz.
On Monday the new manager wired : “Prospect mine
disappointing, nothing payable found worth prospecting ;
locality good ; title insecure ; defeotive pegging ; litiga-
tion probable. Report follows.” A pure gem of con-
demnatory abridgment which applies to nine-tenths of
the W.A. mines which, having really said nothing coher-
ent for themselves, are being tipster-boomed. There is no
better way to find out such mines than by sending along
new managers.

The General Gordon (W.A.), of which great
things are being alleged, was found by some prospectors
sent out by an Inverell (N.S.W.) syndicate, the members
of which lately received £250 a man as a first instalment
of the purchase-money. How many prospecting syndi-
cates were sent out from N.S.W. altogether, and how
many returned a div.? Boomsters never ponder over
trifles like these. The public might do worse. No more
prospecting syndicates are going out, except perhaps one
from Inverell.

The long line of mountain peaks, stretching
from Mount Sorell northward to the Murchison River,
Tas., was named by naturalist and geologist Gould after
the 10 professors : Darwin, Huxley, Jukes, Owen, Lyell,
Sedgwick, Tyndall, Dundas, Reid and Murchison.

SOCIETY
COOLALTA
CAMEO
The secret divorces granted from time to
time by N.S.W. Supreme Court constitute a
blazing scandal and a bitter satire on the boasted
equality of all before the law. For it is a signifi-
cant coincidence that when the approaches to the
divorce-court have been barred during ordi-
nary sitting-hours, or whenever the Judge has
sat at half-past eight a.m. to grant a divorce, the
persons most directly interested have been of much
political or social importance. You never have
strangers shut out of court or the name of a
coming case kept out of the official list where the
parties are a laborer and his wife —any more than
you ever see a black-coated man flogged at the
triangles.
* * * *
Darley, C.J. (N.S.W.) : “I have never yet
tried a (divorce) case in camera, but, if occasion
arose, I would not hesitate to do so.” His Honor,
therefore, claims the statutory power to hold Star
Chamber trials whenever he is game to say that
“ occasion arises. ” What constitutes an “arisen
occasion ? ” Is it defined by law, or by liver? If
by law, the law must be amended. If by liver,
try calomel over an adjournment. Hitherto,
THE DROVING OF THE FUTURE.
Wool being low, and kangaroos and rabbits having become
valuable, the drover of the future will drive before him, not sheep
or bullocks, but kangaroos and rabbits.
“ occasion ” has only “arisen ’’ when some of the
“ nicest people ” wanted to be unspliced.
* * * *
Said Vic. Chief-Justice Madden one day
last week, as he made the usual decree in the case
of a couple whom Parson Kinsman had united:
“ Really, I think a good- deal of time and trouble
and expense would be saved to parties generally
if an Act of Parliament were passed making all
marriages celebrated by the Rev. N. Kinsman
dissolvable after two years.” Kinsman seems to
celebrate more marriages than any other three Vic.
parsons, so he naturally has more divorces than
any other rev. in the province. And as he is a
cheap, unfashionable person who throws people
together in a common sort of way in a furniture-
shop, Kinsman isn’t popular, and the bench makes
weak jokes about him in consequence, and the
Church won’t play in his yard. Still, Kinsman is
more in keeping with the fitness of things than
many other parsons ; marriage is a pretty squalid
affair in most cases, and when the second stage of
it is a wooden cottage on piles, and a waste garden,
and a gate that hangs on one hinge, then the first
stage may as well be the furniture-shepherd’s back
room as any other place. Marriage, with the
average woman, is mostly kitchen and back yard,
and ash-keg, and piles of greasy dishes, and an
expectant milk-jug on the window-sill and a
sodden loaf on the doorstep, and why a woman
should wear orange-blossom and be married in the
light of a big stained-glass window, merely that
she may wash up dishes for the term of her natural
life, is a mystery. It is like opening a mud-cart
with prayer.
* * * *
A witness who came too drunk to give evi-
dence in the George Love murder-case in a Sydney
court, last week, was sent to the cells for an hour
or two by Acting-Judge Rogers, and then dis-
missed with the threat that if he did not come
sober to court next day he would be sent to gaol
for three months. The witness came sober, and
so the matter ended. Just GO years ago Judge
Burton behaved differently with a similarly in-
toxicated witness in a murder-case in a Sydney
court. This witness was summarily fined £5O for
contempt and was not afterwards called on for
testimony. The notorious Lynch was the prisoner
in the dock on that occasion, and he was acquitted
owing to the absence of that drunken witness’s
evidence, and was thus aUle to complete an appal-
ling career of murder and outrage.
* * * *
A question asked in Sydney : Why did not
criminal proceedings of some kind arise out of the
developments consequent on those anonymous
letters lately sent to Mr. Justice Simpson in a
divorce-case ?
In Melb. recently two men
were fined ss. each for breaking
the Sabbath by drying bear-
skins on the Day of Rust. Bear-
skins, it appears, take eight or
ten consecutive days to dry,
therefore they have to dry on
at least one Sunday ; and the
furriers weren’t actually drying
them—their crime consisted in
leaving the skins in the sun on
that day and allowing Provi-
dence to break the Sabbath by
letting the law of evaporation
go on as usual. The magistrate
who inflicted the fines had pro-
bably broken the same Sabbath
in the same way by drying
himself on that day, or even if
he didn’t dry himself it is to be
that he dried natu-
rally, just like the skins. It
would be interesting to know if
a case would lie against a man
who grew his beard on the
Sunday in the city of Fitzgibbon,
or whose hair was found increas-
ing in length, or whose infant
didn’t stop growing. If it
wouldn’t, why wouldn’t it ?
Why doesn’t some one start a
society for carrying the Sabbath
law to its logical conclusion, and
issue 420,010 summonses on
Monday morning against the
whole population of Melbourne
because their skin evaporated
on the previous day ?
* * *
Anglican Primate Smith
gravely reminded a Sydney sub-
urban parish, the other day, that
it ought to “ get beyond the
narrow circle and reach the
wider one,” and that it should
“ remember that they were all
members of a complex, com-
posite body.” That’s just what’s
the matter with the whole hier-
archy of which S. Smith is a
shilling bishop, and with every
other religious hierarchy. Those
bodies forget that they are “ all
members of a complex, composite body ” called the
human race.
* * * *
Those who argue that sons, or very near
relatives, of Judges, who practise as barristers in
Courts over which these Judges preside are likely
to acquire adventitious favor in suitors’ eyes, may
illustrate their argument by comparing N.S.W.
Divorce Court proceedings, from a barristerial
point of view, in the new regime of Justice Simp-
son with the average of proceedings in the days of
his Honor Windeyer. Then Mr. Richard (son), if
he did not appear for petitioner, generally figured
for respondent or co-respondent, and it was con-
sidered passing strange if Mr. Ralston (son-in-law)
had not a hold of some end of every case. Now
new barrister faces are seen and new barrister
voices heard from day to day, and the Windeyer-
esqueness of the proceedings no longer offends eye
and ear.

* * * *
Sydney Evening News, in discussing John
Haynes’s motion to abolish capital punishment in
N.S.W., instances the case of the celebrated
Lynch, of Berrima, as that of a pre-eminent
assassin about whom there was no suspicion of
insanity. Only the other day the present writer
heard an eminent “mad-doctor” incidentally
“DUCKS!”
mention the case of Lynch as that of “ a typical
homicidal maniac—no doubt of it. ” Anyone who
reads Lynch’s confession, as set forth'in Judge
Therry’s “Reminiscences of N.S.W. and Vic-
toria,’ can hardly fail to come to a similar con-
clusion about the man, whose habit it was, before
committing murder, to kneel down and pray to
God for “ strength and direction.”
* * * *
Benalla, Vic., possesses a clerical innova-
tor who illustrates his sermons with magic-lantern
slides. In the dead middle and centre of firstly,
fifthly, or lastly, brethren, lights are switched off,
and worshippers are favored with a gorgeous car-
toon to point the moral and adorn the sermon
This, the shepherd hopes, “will attract the non-
churchgoing people of Benalla.” Well, it ought
to. There is no sense in spooning your girl out in
the frost and fog these winter nights if you can do
it in a warm, dry church in the dim, religious light
when Moses is coming down the Mount on a wet
sheet, or when the congregation is being won over
to righteousness by a lime-light picture of Sheol
with the Devil in the foreground stirring up fat
sinners with a hayfork. Artemus Ward long ago
discovered the popularity of a magic-lantern show
amongst young couples, and it is pleasant to
find the clergy of our time not above taking a
pointer from the genial showman.
* * * *
Hint to the New Woman : A skirt divided
against itself cannot stand.
An Avenel (Vic.) defunct
had willed that his widow’s al-
lowance should cease if she
remarried ; also that, in such
event, her control of the child-
ren should cease, because he
would “not give any man the
chance of ill-treating them ”;
but that she should still have
a house and land at Avenel, “so
that she shall have a house over
her head if her second husband
deserts her.” Queer compound
of care and jealousy is a truly-
loving hubby ! This one evi-
dently foresaw the possibility
that his “ownest'’ might be
worth marrying—and deserting !
The will is in contrast to that
of the historical old curmudgeon
who left his wife “ .A2OO a year
so long as she remains single,”
but left to any future husband
she might get “ .£5OO a year,
as compensation, and because he
will find that he will need every
penny of it.” Which of these
twain loved his lady the more ?

One half of St. Andrew’s
kirk, Newcastle (N.S.W.), want,
according to the case put by the
other half, to inflict upon a
meenister “life-long punishment
for one fault ” —“ an indiscretion
at Broken Hill years ago. ” Thus,
you see, human nature —even the
human nature of kirk-men-re-
volts at the undue severity that
it daily preaches according to
its ritual and catechism. The
Pharisaical section, in this in-
stance, is at least logical. For
the dogma of Calvindom is that,
if a man dies with one little
peccadillo unfory.ven, he must endure everlasting
punishment “in a bottomless pit, full of flames
and brimstone. ” Hell!

* * * *
Interesting case from Bendigo. A book-
maker’s clerk was up on various charges before the
local “ Binch.” Result, as Age reported
For striking the barmaid, fined £2; for striking the
constable, fined £3 ; for using obscene language in the
hotel, discharged ; and for using obscene language in the
presence of the constable, fined £2.
So it is cheaper by £1 to lambaste a barmaid than
a policeman, and whilst a man may curse a bar-
maid with impunity, he runs a great risk in cor-
rupting a constable with swear-words. This is
what is called police protection.

With the beginning of the really cold
weather in Melbourne, it is strange to see the
leading drapery houses sacrificing their winter
goods to make room for an early show of spring
fashions. Such is the case* however ; and it is
marvellous to note the extreme manner in which
the prices of articles which one would have
thought would just be in the greatest demand—such
as furs, sealskin jackets, winter mantles, dress
stuffs, &c.—are reduced at Messrs. Hicks Atkin-
son and Sons’establishment in Collin-street during
their existing stock-taking sale.*

foolisTTfy ventured into a Salvation 1 f anny
meeting, where, in a weak moment, he allowed'
himself to be converted. One of the female cap-
tains took him unawares, and cried, “Come!
come ! come now ! ” into his expansive ear until he
tottered up to the penitent form and became a
saved soul. The Harmy entered his soul in the
official ledger and the War Cry included him in
its weekly total of conversions. Three weeks of
new life made James so strong in the Lord that
lie rose Upon the platform, at the end of an excit-
ing Sunday might service, and swore himself into
.the Harmy ranks by ordering a scarlet guernsey
and cap at lSs. The next thing heard from James
was a story of his having been stuck up by high-
-waymen and robbed of the money he had set apart
for-the purchase of his Harmy regalia. But the
penitent’s tale soon turned out to be a pure and
beautiful lie. It was a . highway woman whom
James encountered. The naughty creature led
him away by the quiet waters of the Yarra
and it is believed he gave her a sovereign
instead of a sixpence. James is not certain
upon that point. All he will swear to is
his present deep regret for a burst of religious
fervor which was the root of his moral disaster.

A pathetic bush-story full of human nature:
Dear Bulletin, — I camped with an ancient whaler the
other night. When he unrolled his swag there dropped
out, to my surprise, a pair of lady’s silk stockings. “ What
do I want them for? Well, I tell yer : when I goes to a
boundary’s hut for rations, I tells him I’ve got no rations-
bag, an’use these here things. The boundary-riders don’t
often see a woman, an’ how do they fill up those stockings
with flour ! Fill, they do fill ! 6x 8.
* • * *
A Sydney registry-office case which should
warn women seeking work in the country. A
mother and daughter engaged in Sydney to act as
cook and housemaid respectively to five persons on
a station between Cobar and Wilcannia. On ar-
rival (so says a Bulletin correspondent) they
found eleven people instead of the agreed-upon
five, and demanded more wages. They were re-
fused, and, being penniless, they had to irnlk into
Cobar, a distance of nearly 100 miles. They had
neither blankets nor tucker, and the station re-
fused all assistance in the shape of vehicle, money,
or food.
* * * *
The fantastic anomalies of the Tas. police
force under municipal-control are borne with by
police and public as a less evil than the system
of Govt, centralisation which obtained in the bad
old days. It is useless pointing out that the con-
ditions have changed since the palmy days of the
policeman. The horrors of that sort of Govern-
ment control have left memories that stick fast,
and the shades of Muster-master Mason and John
Price are Called up directly police centralisation is
mentioned.
* * * *
A small, pale, scared-looking member of
the N.S.W. Cabinet attended the recent Sydney
dog-show with his family, and, as usual, the
smallest progeny got away and tried to fool with
the ugliest bull-dog he could find. The conster-
nated Minister rushed after him, and hauled him
off by the back of his clothes, just as the bull dog
began to grow friendly with his small visitor.
Then a ribald Opposition scoffer raised a guffaw :
“ Look at ’im,” he said ; “ that’s our Dog-fearing
Ministry! ”
A queer case, in which a complexion-fakir
claiming to be endowed with miraculous powers
seems to have persuaded a very nice girl that he
was a heavenly messenger, is now being investi-
gated by the police.

years old and prime, and with every appearance of

Some Goolgardie gossip :
Coolgardie’s seven nr eight two-chain wide streets are
up to 4in. deep in a finefy-pulverised dust that rises as a
mist many feet even when the air is still. You can’t
escape breathing it—dust of all sort s, including decom-
posed human excreta. No wonder typhoid is not abating.
Fully five persons a day are being planted in this filthy
plao'e. A late victim, 'Mr. A. A. Naumann, was much
liked, and died worth some t'60,000. One deal he never
knew about was his purchase of 603 Bank of Englands at
about £l. Before advice came he was in the hospital, llis
executors can now get £8 a-piece for these shares. A boom
is on, and one that promises to eclips? any preyihus booms
experienced in Australia. Some 30,000 shares change
hands daily in Coolgardie alone. In Adelaide a record of
84,000 was reached. Take a few stocks :

The other day a Melbourne woman ran
down a total stranger, and put the “lor” on to
him, declaring that he was her long-lost husband.

The stranger, a reputable barrister, protested that
he was somebody else’s husband, that he was not
claimant’s property and never had been, but the
woman knew the wart on his neck and the color
of his whiskers, and swore to him by his ear and
back teeth, and the law took its course, the man
in dispute proved, with some difficulty, to the
satisfaction of the Bench that he was the person
he claimed to be, and then claimant admitted that
perhaps he wasn’t her long-lost husband after all,
but insisted that he was so like the missing pro-
perty that she ought to be allowed the benefit of
the doubt. But the man escaped, and the woman
went out on the trail again. The female in pursuit
of a misplaced hubby is more to be dreaded than a
ravening lion.
The suicide at Gilgunnia (N.S.W.) of a
miner, by placing on his tongue a plug of racka-
rock with a lighted slow fuse and dynamite-cap
attached, conjures up to the fancy a gruesome
picture. The nerve (or deadly coma) of a man
who can lie down and watch ignition stealing
slowly along the fuse, with the full knowledge that
in a few torture-lingering seconds his skull will be
blown to bits, is not comprehensible to a healthy
mind. Heroism isn’t in the same street with this
—a method of suicide not uncommon among Aus-
tralian miners.

Cigars are fair-weather acquaintances —a
pipe’s a friend.
Smoking is at first an adjunct to a meal, but
soon a meal becomes the supplement of a pipe.
Every man makes his own pipe you can only
buy the skeleton.
A patent pipe is a type-written love-letter.

A RECEIPT IN VERSE.
O’, party possessed of a live sense
Of duty and honor and shame,
We beg to acknowledge the fivepence,
And place to your credit the same.
You were caught, like a ram in a thicket,
As Satan remarked with a sneer,
But, now you have paid for the ticket,
Your conscience is clear.
You walk as before in tlje right way,
Relieved from fivepenn’orth of sin ;
The Kingdom of Glory, one might say,
You’ve merely to go in and win.
Whilst many a weak, erring creeter
Stops short at the gateway in fear,
You’ll pass, with a note from St. Peter—
“ His conscience is clear.”
And yet, had you died in a smash-up,
A railway catastrophe sad,
On the day you omitted to cash-up,
No doubt you’d have gone to the bad,
Deprived of all hope of salvation,
Cut off from repentance sincere.
You lived to make full reparation—
Your conscience is clear.
Your Bulletin sisters and brothers
Rejoice at your cleansing from crime,
More than over the ninety-nine others
Who paid up their fares at the time.
Pear holds craven souls in subjection :
They’d cheat every day in the year
But for dreading the risk of detection—
Your conscience is clear.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

Sydney, June 16, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
N.S.W. Women’s Suffrage League continues
to —no, not buck-up—pig-jump is more expressive
of the situation. At the last Quinquatria, as one
may call it, the outlook was pretty gloomy, from a
well-wisher’s standpoint. The lady who presided
Bu ßg'Gsted cold cream more than common-sense,
and the attendance about reached the ominous
total of 45, including a parson and a doctor, of
course, for without at least one of each no feminine
crisis can ever be called complete. The secretary
made an elaborate address which consisted of
enumerating the lone, lorn blue-stockings dotted
all over the globe who had become sanitary in-
spectors, and things like that, which somehow led
gently up to three cheers for Parkes; but there
was only -modified enthusiasm when a friendly
M. Prose and asserted that it was very probable
that in five years or so N.S.W. would give women
the suffrage. Upon which a spinster was heard
remarking in a disheartened tone of voice that the
women of N.S.W. didn’t feel disposed to wait five
years, and the meeting terminated. But what a
world it is for poor weak woman—especially of the
Woman-Suffrage sort! No sooner does she give
up waiting for a husband than she starts on the
old, dreary, hope-deferred racket after a cold and
bloodless vote.
Christianity is going to be the swagger thing
this season, and Anybody who is Anybody will have
to convert the nearest Chow or other bad charac-
ter if he or she wishes to prove a claim to
belong to the Nicest people. Tracts will go up to
five shillings a dozen, I expect, now that there is
to be a Bible-class for the forelock-pulling Factory
Club girls on Sabbath afternoons at Guvmentouse.
But it’s really amazing to me to contemplate the
unblushing versatility of the “great,” who on
Saturday graciously lend their patronage to what
they attribute to the Devil, but who are found on
Sunday expounding the Word and singing hymn
one noughty-nought with their eyes rolled up. Is
“ official ” character conveniently made in parti-
colored layers like moral Swiss rolls ?
Apropos the Factory Girls’ Club, rather an in-
comprehensible line crept into the last official re-
port therefrom : “The hon. treas. to the mother’s
sick maternity fund stated . . . there was a
good balance in hand. It was stated that as this
fund had proved such a boon to mothers, it had
been determined to start one for girls on the same
lines.”
This appears to be good weather for deputations
—the last feminine one of importance in Sydney
being headed by the Acting-Governoress, who
bearded Georgey-Porgey on the subject of seating
accommodation for shop-girls, shorter hours, and
a, universal weekly half-holiday. It was very
fitting that a deputation of women should tackle
the subject, for you haven’t far to “ chercher la
femme ” when you come to the sorrows of Jenny
Wren. It was no doubt to soothe the self-love of
some snobess, who would rather shake hands with
a cat than a couturi&re, that serving-girls were
first forced to stand deferentially on one foot while
the snobess flopped all over a chair, and even now
how many arrogant nobodies are there who would
gasp like fish out of water if a girl attempted to
sell them a packet of pins sitting down—the girl I
mean, not the pins !
There has always been something particularly
heinoqs in the idea of a “ young woman ” sitting
down in the presence of those who are not women,
thank goodness—and in a variegated community
like this, where if the sewing-women didn’t stand
up it would generally be difficult to distinguish
which was which, you can’t say there hasn’t been
some method in this milliners’ martyrdom. It’s a
gentlemanly act, anyhow, to hand a lady a chair,
and it will be a serious blot on Georgey-Porgey’s
reputation as a ladies’ man if he doesn’t see the
thing right through.
It is barbarous to insist on girls standing up so
long. I don’t think, mind you, that they should
sit while they are serving—that would look too
much like Tasmanian notions of business energy—
but in between they should certainly be allowed
to rest. It is high time, too, that something was
done. In quite a number of shops lately I have
been struck by the _ bloodless, woe-begone, un-
healthy faces of the girls. And they are naturally
a nice-looking lot, are Sydney shop-girls, if you
give them half a chance.
A grievance comes to me from up North —but it
really is common all over Australia, wherever
mixed public balls eventuate. It is merely the
old, old story of the girl whose pa has struck it
lucky, and her terrible scorn when she finds her-
self, at a public ball, involved in a “ square ”
without epaulettes or gold buttons upon her
vis-a-vis. The Society damsel in this case, who
left in the middle of a set of Lancers, is only one
remove from a grocer’s shop, and would be selling
soap over papa’s counter had mere chance not
changed all that. However, this sort of thing,
common in Australia as ticks, is the very worst
possible form (for there is nothing easier than
standing out if the personnel of the set doesn’t
suit you), and yet the girls who do it vainly
imagine that it shows their superiority. And
thus is Guvmentouse rewarded for years of shining
example! To see the sublime patience and un-
varying sweetness with which her Ex. jigs with
Alderman Tallowfat doesn’t seem to be an object-
lesson to these shoddy females, who are so fright-
ened _of meeting their tailor in the grande chatne
that it almost looks as if they still owed him for
their bloomers.
In the teeth of such moral cramps it is really
refreshing to hear that at Gundagai (N.S.W.) the
other extreme has been rushed to. At a recent
meeting to arrange a bachelors’ ball squatters and
miners clinked glasses and vowed that no girl in
the district should go uninvited or do the Cinder-
ella act for want of a card. Governesses and
scullery-maids were alike greeted out of the same
mail-bag with resplendent cards of invitation dif-
fering in no wise from one another, Mrs. Pure
Merino’s lettering being no more golden than Mary
Ann’s. On the eventful evening the gathering
must have looked almost a length ahead of the
millennium. For my part, I would much rather
undertake to reconcile the lion and the lamb than
induce Mrs. Borndrunk to stand up in the same
set as her cook.
The reported death of sculler Stan bury, which
some irresponsible sensationalist cabled to Aus-
tralia on the strength of a mere capsize, must
have been a terrible ordeal for Mrs. Stanbury,
who has had the somewhat rare experience of
knowing exactly how a widow feels while she was
happily nothing of the sort in reality. It is a
wonder to me that the penny papers haven’t tried
to interview Mrs. S. as to how she felt before and
after the cable. A detailed description of her
mixed feelings would have supplied yards of copy.
It was evident all round that his native city meant
to get all the advertisement out of the allegedly-
drowned oarsman that it decently or indecently
could.
Ten minutes after the silly cable landed there
appeared at one of the Sydney florists a portrait
of the cahle-killed sculler surrounded by a funereal
wreath. When one saw this one could hardly
help a small, satirical smile—it was such a lovely
example of purely commercial sentiment caught
on the hop, so to speak—for there is really no
more sentiment in such displays than when a
draper puts an alluring spring costume on a winter
dummy. The only really sentimental window-
show I ever saw was in a Melb. florist’s a day
after the Cup. It was the saddle, bridle, whip
and cap, made up in flowers, of the colors of a
horse which hadn't come off. Yet they gave it
window-room for days.
Even at the best of times the wreath-racket
stirs one’s bile. The senders of wreaths are so
often pushing people, who take advantage of a re-
speetable person’s death to exaggerate their inti-
macy with the corpse and jump on it, as it were,
when it’s down and can’t retaliate with a frozen
stare or decline anything with thanks. And then
there is another phase of wreath-sending which is
just as interesting, when Great Folks suddenly
overflow on to the coffins of lower-class notorieties
who have been murdered or drowned, with big
white .wreaths and large visiting-cards attached.
When this happens you can always see an
election in the middle distance, or some event in
which the potentates and the public have a hand.
But perhaps the most barefaced wreath of all is
the wreath that gets sent by the actress, who is a
frost on foreign shores, to the year-old grave of the
sister actress with whom she was notoriously at
daggers-drawn during the latter’s life. However,
this is the age of advt., and corpses afford a golden
opportunity for a mob of people who cannot ex-
actly attract public attention to themselves per
medium of sandwich-men.
Some departed Personages, who make believe
that they still think of nothing but Australia,
have got the wreath business down to a fine art.
For example, the simple little cable words “Tobo,
Parkes,”from Lord Ear waggle, of London, to Perns'
the Sydney florist, really means : “ Lay a three-
guinea wreath on Parkes’s grave, charge me with
same deducting two-and-a-half discount for cash
within four months.” Whereas “Decent Jobson”
would mean, “ Lay a two-guinea wreath on Job-
son’s grave.” Judging by what one sees of the
aristocracy, I should imagine that “Decent” did
more submarine travelling than the more expensive
Tobo.
The ever-popular Tempe Ball, for which Mrs.
(Dr.) Maguire and Mrs. Brunton are hon. secs,
takes place at Sydney Town Hall on 30th June
and is going to break the record. Tickets always
sell well for Tempe Ball, which is known as the
Men’s ball, though I really cannot fathom why.
Two big concerts this month are Mdlle. Trebelli’s
show at the Town Hall this (Saturday) evening,
when that plump and polished vocalist will raise
her perfectly-trained soprano in song; and the
comp. ben. to organist Wiegand at Sydney Town
Hall on 27th instant (John Laure, hon. sec.), when
a gorgeous array of talent will give the gifted city
organist a fitting sent-off on his holiday tour.
Government seems to be making headway with
the Parkes family allowance debate, and the poli-
tical veteran’s widow won’t have to depend on
ravens for a crust, anyhow. By the way, Mr.
Affleck, M.P., rose during the discussion and
tried to insert a clause, disentitling the lady from
receiving the proposed allowance (£lOO a year)
“should she re-marry,” but the cruel amendment
was promptly pooh-pooh’d. The idea of anybody
bearing the name of Parkes being prevented from
committing matrimony as oftenas he or she pleased,
struck everybody as being too incongruous al-
together. •
When people reputed to be extensively in debt
promote charitable entertainments, in aid of not-
very-clearly-specified objects, suspicion, however
ill-grounded it may be, is necessarily aroused.
At the opening of Sydney Parliament, the other
day, the most tiresome bit of solemn fiddle-faddle
(which is saying a good deal on an occasion given
over to fuss and feathers and Mumbo-Jumbo) oc-
curred when the band was booming the Anathema
in the middle distance, and two ex-military men
standing near the Australian Club solemnly and
reverently uncovered to the distressing strains.
The worst part of it was that both were wearing
thin, not to say billiard-ball-ish, on top —through
wear and tear in saluting the many national idols,
I suppose. The average polite Englishman, punc-
tiliously disposed towards his Royal fetishes, runs
more danger from neuralgia in the head than a
dozen Australians put»together.
The latest fashionable craze is tattooing—a pecu-
liarly naughty effect being got by having the
lower eyelid tattooed, and no engaged girl is ortho-
dox without her Gussie’s image in Indian ink in
some partially-confidential position which yet may
be revealed to the right man at the psychological
moment. But any girl bent on illustrating her-
self ought to hunt up the Herculean cheap-Jaek
who performed at the last N.S.W. Agricultural
Show. He was a unique specimen who called
himself “ Charlie Nelson, the tattooed Irish
sailor,” but he was obviously more or less of the
race of Israel—probably less, as one rarely sees so
solid a man of the Hebraic persuasion. The mar-
vellously - energetic humourist was liberally
decorated with tattoo, and stood on his rostrum
with bare arms and trousers turned up past the
knees, the hero of the hour with the women.
Though, mind you, it isn’t altogether safe to plod-
dingly interpret the designs on every tattooed tar.
Most of the little weaknesses of Jack ashore have
a place in the picture-gallery, and so the design is
occasionally not adapted to exhibit in mixed com-
pany.
The latest romantic story is that of Constable
Bradley, who died of fever at a Westralian mining
town, and would have seen a baronet in his look-
ing-glass if he had lived long enough. His real
name was Lionel Bradley Pilkington, and he was
possible heir to an English baronetcy. If the
story is true, Lionel was a bold, bad baronet of
the most approved Family Herald type—he
eloped with the wife of an English milord before
he was of age, squandered a fortune, and came to
Australia on the good old English principle that
rubbish may be shot here. Then, of course, he
turned bobby in the old, old way of “ the nicest
people,” whose daughters have to turn dressmaker
(or waitress at six shillings a week—that, I am
told, is the usual Sydney-society-tea-shop figure)
when the money goes, and whose sons too often
find themselves intellectually on the level of rouse-
abouts when Pate sets them finding an occupation.
It somehow sounds romantic to hear of baronets
—but, after all, what are baronets ? Mostly the
seed of pushing pork-butchers who became mayors,
and so bought a title to distinguish their descen-
dents by. The only baronet I ever remember
having questioned on the subject of his earthly
greatness told me that his ancestor, who seemed
to have been an unspeakable kind of person, bought
the family brand for a couple of hundred pounds.
But that baronet looked cheap. I remember his
manners were en suite. He used to travel about
with a fox-terrier, upon whom he played off rude
remarks. “Toby,” he would say, gazing in the
tyke’s comparatively intelligent face, “you’ah
bored. Confess it, Toby, this pawty is boring you
dwedfully ! ”
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“Mrs. Be van and four youthful Be vans have
gone ’Ome and left the venerable-looking divine to
the care of the youngest daughter and eldest son.
When the boat was on the point of departure a
rapturous girl-friend flung one of the Miss Bevans
a diamond locket. Whether this was spontaneous
or a pre-arranged signal is not known, but the
large number of friends on the pier at once began
to shower some of the more valuable coin of the
realm upon the departing quintette, much to the
amusement of bystanders. The boys on board
caught quite a substantial sum in their hats.
“ The Rontgen photos will have much to answer
for. Already the rays have made revelations.
A needle was located in a patient’s hand at Melb.
hospital last week, and lively IMrs. Freeman
Thomas, the Governor’s daughter, had her hand
taken the other day, when it was discovered that
one of her fingers had been broken. Pa Brassey
turned a severe glance upon her, and enquired
when it happened, remarking that it was the first
he had heard of it. Thus was a youthful escapade
brought to light.
“By the irony of fate, baritone Douglas
Powell—from the English point of view the least
illustrious of the new quartette party—has turned
out the trump-card in Melb. He was simply a
promising junior thrown in as a fill-up, whereas
the other three are described as festival artists,
w r ',,' ° we h s local hit is perhaps as much attri-
butable to his gushing, dAbonnaire stage
manner, youthful good looks and muscular
appearance, as to his fine voice and dramatic
singing. The Melb. girls, having lost the beau-
teous Brownlow, have now transferred their affec-
tions to baritone Powell aforesaid.
1 Victorian Soft-goods Association ball at
Melb. was a crowded affair, with a really gorgeous
show of dresses, perhaps the most noticeable being
that worn by an attractive milliner—a sumptuous
brocade with skirt distended in crinoline-form.”
. . A Melb. penlady has lately got married, and

writer. But the matter has been kept so profound
a secret that nobody in the press-world seems to
be quite certain whom the lady has married, with
the consequence that all the ostensibly unmarried
pressmen are more or less suspected. An acci-
dental mixing of copy has, however, it is thought
sheeted the matter home to the agricultural man!

Hops all at once into a yarn about turnips and the
