SPORTING NOTIONS .
An Australian abroad gossips to The Bulletin
by last mail “ The match against Lord Sheffield’s
Eleven, at Sheffield Park, is generally considered ‘ more
bother than it is worth,’ and very rightly. This noble
patron of cricket has all kinds of fads and fancies and
rules and regulations, and, as it is his private ground and
he pays the Australians a lump sum to play, the only way is
to grin and bear him and get grey. Stoddart, as already
noted, has managed to quarrel with his Lordship, and a
good many other prominent cricketers wouldn’t mind if
they had.
“The Australian cricketers are having a good
time amongst the London theatres and music-halls? In
consequence of manager Musgrove’s connection with the
business, not only members of the team, but any friends
they like to take with them, are admitted to almost every
house in the town. J
“ The present team is going very slow re ban-
quets, &c., and in the first two weeks only attended a
couple. The invitations which pour in have on previous
occasions been accepted as a matter of course. Manager
Musgrove sticks to business, and is probably the first man
in the position who has made it a hard-and-fast rule not
to drink in the day-time. “Come and have a small bottle ”
is met by a cold and unbending refusal.
“ Though at first rather diffident, the cricketers
are gradually appearing in bell-toppers and frock-coats
Frank Iredale was the first, and it looks as though the
manager would be the last. When the metamorphosis is
complete a photograph of the team will be worth money.
“The net profits of the Sydney test-match to
the Australian Eleven amounted to £B2B—or over £6B per
man—not a bad start for the tour. Clem. Hill and Kelly
not being members at the time of the match, don’t share ”
* * • *
The time has arrived when Australia must pay
a substantial price for good professional cricketers, or be
content to serve as a nursery for English county-elevens.
The Giffens of the future will not stick to their native
land for the sake of a £3-a-week billet in the Post Office
when they might achieve.a comfortable competency at
’Ome. Why should they stop out here, any more than
native-born singers, painters, or sculptors of special merit
stop here, unless the fat-man “patrons of the noble game”
assist the local cricket-managers to make a market for
their talent? The S.A. Cricket Association has met the
inevitable half-way by offering Jones a fair inducement to
sell his services in Adelaide instead of Sussex. If N.S.W.
and Victoria want to retain the best of their non-amateur
bowlers they must recognise them as professionals and
pay them something like the English price, which is a
half-yearly income of £lO or £l2 a week, with the possi-
bility of complimentary benefits looming in the future.
* * * *
Ernest Jones, before he left for England, avowed
his intention to accept the first good offer from an English
county, so the offer from S.A. Cricketing Association is,
perhaps, too late. Jones was employed as an occasional
hand at S.A. Exhibition grounds, and was to have been
curator of the new S. Adelaide Oval, but that project is
still far off. J
* * * *
Providence, no doubt, ordained that awful col-
lapse of Trott’s Eleven at Lord’s as a punishment for
Melbourne Age. The journal in question, being a bare-
faced panderer to Young Australian prejudices in the
matter of cricket, slipped a saving clause (as it thought)
into its first cablegram re the M.C.C. match. The cable
said “ wickets heavy,” but seeing that the Englishmen
had scored 137 runs for 3 wickets down, the Age felt
bound to rub the gilt off their performance. Therefore,
the bare item of news was published in this form : “ The
wicket, however, is a little heavy owing to recent rains,
but experts describe it as being in an easy-playing condi-
tion.” For the initiated it goes almost without saying
that the “experts” and their opinion were possibly in-
vented by the Age sub. -editor, or somebody, who couldn’t
permit patriotic feeling to be wounded by the chance
of Australian bowling getting pasted when it ought to
have been the “bowlers’ day out.” Then came the judg-
ment on Ananias. “ Australia all out for 18.” Pougher
5 wickets for 0, J. Hearne 4 wickets for 4—and the ground,
alas for the Age !“in an easy-playing condition.” Hing-
lish barrackers may fairly argue that the whole team
(with Giffen prudently absenting himself) would have gone
down for 4 runs, but for the captain’s stupid mistake in
permitting Attewell to take a hand in the attack. The
Nottinghamshire man has always been regarded as a fair-
weather bowler, and in this instance, anyway, he was a
failure, with 14 runs scored off him for no wickets. The
fact that Attewell didn’t come off is additional testi-
mony to the merit of Pougher’s phenomenal record.
Pougher at his best was the most deadly of the
English bowlers last season, but he is less reliable than
some of the others.
* * * *
So far, no “ century ” has been compiled by an
English cricketer against the Aus. Eleven. On the other
hand, three have been placed to the credit of the Austra-
lians—Gregory (154), Darling and Giffen (each 115).
* * * *
As showing the looseness of English fielding at
the beginning of a cricket season. On Mav 7 Surrey
scored a first innings of 443 against Leicestershire, whilst
Yorkshire was making 400 runs for 5 wickets against
Warwickshire.
* * * *
Mold, the Lancashire bowler, proves that the
average English batsman has something to learn from the
Australian. A steady success in first-class matches among
his own people, Mold has generally failed to do much
against the visiting teams. Young Townsend, of Glouces-
tershire, seems to be another crack English bowler whose
art is simple enough to our boys.
* * * *
The formerly-consistent batsman Iredale’s dis-
mal failure to date in England is coupled by his friends
with his recent wedding. Athleticism and honeymooning,
apparently, mate no better in Iredale’s case than in that
of Zimmerman. “No man is worth much either six
months before or six months after marriage.”
*** s *
The Australian aboriginal runs as naturally to
cricket as the Maori to football. The other day a Kempsey
(N.S.W.) storekeeper offered a silver cup to be competed
for by local cricket-clubs. The darkies about Kempsey
immediately formed a club and entered under the appro-
priate name of “ True Australians ” ; and notwithstanding
they played against four other clubs they gloriously won
the cup. The white youths of Kempsey rather shun the
cricket subject now.
* * * »
Noted as a peculiar coincidence that in England
and Sydney Giffen is always attacked by sciatica when
he has to bat on a bad wicket.
* * * *
H. K. Foster and Hartley seem to have been
the most serviceable men in the defeated Oxford team.
Foster made 66 and 40 by brilliant hitting, and Hartley
finished first and third for the bowling and batting aver-
ages. This is not saying very much for him, ad the same.
* * * »
The funniest authentic sporting incident The
Bulletin ever heard of is the case of that Sydney football
referee who last Saturday had to draw on a dozen players,
furious at his decisions, the revolver “ he had brought for
the purpose of protecting himself.” “Does Football
Brutalise ? ”
* * * *
Betting on Sydney football isn’t as remunera-
tive as aforetime. Results are too easily gauged. Rand-
wick barrackers, by the way, have increased tenfold, and
monopolise the city on Saturday nights. They want the
whole arena and most of the reserved seats. A boss club
has the most barrackers. Always whoop for the top pup.
* * « *
Saturday’s swell football function, Randwick v.
University, excited little enthusiasm, the play being
monotonously commonplace. The forwards of both
tackled excellently', and the dashing backs shivered ex-
pectantly waiting for chances that seldom came. Con-
sequently', the hideous yells that greet the streaky fire-
brigade run 3 of a spririting-god weren’t as frequent as
usual. The U’s played strongly in the first term, but got
waterlogged afterwards, and Randwicks beat them
decisively—lB to 0. The winners, for whom Henlen
got three tries, missed three goal-kicking chances, too, a
couple of the shots going dangerously close to the exact
location. The U’s played without two of their principal
men—knocked out the previous week. Rugby would be
much safer, perhaps, if played with horse-pistols as s
guard against accident.

A Sea Captain’s Story.
What a Grand Old Sailor Told a Press
Reporter.
What He Has Seen and Found in Nearly
Every Port oe the Globe, and the
Value He Has Received.
I met a happy old sea captain at the wharf yester-
day, and had a pleasant and profitable conversa-
tion with him. I think he had travelled to all
quarters of the globe, for he mentioned Sitka,
Stockholm, Rio Janeiro, London, and South
Africa, in addition to all the principal seaport
cities of the world.
If one wanted to find a healthy, ruddy specimen
of humanity, this captain furnished it to such an
extent that I remarked about it. He laughed,
and said :
‘ ‘ But you should have seen me a number oi
years ago. Why even a pale-faced cabin-boy
looked healthy compared to me then. Command-
ing a ship is no easy task, and I suffered from
rheumatism, debility, and, it seemed to me, many
of the ills we hear so much about. I won’t tell
you all the details, but I will say that for the last
nine years I have been not only healthy, but
robust. I believe it is all due to one cause ”
Naturally I was interested, and urged him to
tell me his secret. Speaking very deliberately, he
said :
“ I have found that by care, and the use of just
the right thing, I can preserve my health and pro-
long my life almost indefinitely. Indeed, I think
I have discovered the secret. No matter where I
go, I carry it with me, and I tell you that in what-
ever port I stop, I can always find it. Do you ask
me what it is ? I will tell you : Warner’s ‘ Safe’
Cure—nothing else. I have purchased it in the
Mediterranean, in Australia, in England, in
America, in South Africa, in Bombay, and, in
fact, in every quarter of the globe, so it must be
that people all over the world are learning, as I
have learned, how valuable it is. Now don’t think
I am telling you this to advertise the medicine,
for I don’t know the people who make it, and,
indeed, don’t know what it is made of, but I do
know that for broken down men and weak women,
it is the grandest discovery of modern times, and
I don’t hesitate to say so. ”
I left the old captain, whose ship was about to
sail, much impressed by his sincerity and earnest-
ness. Certainly he is a living sample of what the
world calls health, and hence what most of us con-
sider happiness.*

POLITCAL
POINTS
“Toby” Barton, it is again whispered,
may lead N.S.W. Protectionists to the poll on
next appeal to the people. Ranks at present
divided will, it is assumed, close up at his magic
call.
Bent’s South Melb. voters were (pathetic
gratitude !) mostly railway laborers, because, as
Minister of Railways, he had stuck out for six-
pence a day extra in their pay. Men knocked off
work to go and vote for him.
‘ ‘ Scotty the Wrinkler’s ” proposal to tax the
Chinaman’s employer instead of the Chinaman
himself is not so impracticable as may appear at
first sight. In England the employer of every
male servant has to pay a tax of 15s. per annum.
Premier Turner, who drew up and passed
the Vic. Income Tax Act, sent in his own tax
schedule early, and had it promptly returned to
him with a demand for “ further particulars.”
Even the man who makes a law seldom really
knows what is in it.
Grrown-up men in the employ of Vic. Rail-
way Department get 2s. 6d. a day for 5J days a
week. Government explains that they do boy’s
work. And boys do men’s work for something
less, no doubt. Will the Government strangle the
sweater —will it suicide ?
A Potts Point admirer of the ways of a
political Providence notes that by the re-election
the other day of Jenkin Coles to the S. Australian
Speakership the three provinces of Australia with-
out titled Premiers —N.S.W., S.A., and Vic.—are
blessed with titled Speakers.
The Late Judge BUCKLEY, of M.L.
Photo, by Kinsey, Wellington.
Brewer N. Fitzgerald, M.L.C. (Vic.) told
A meeting of R.C. young men the other evening
that the goal of their ambition should be—the
Legislative Council! Some men are born Fat-
headed ; some achieve Fatheadedness ; but few
have Fatheadedness thrust upon them in this bare-
faced fashion.
Coincidence. When Murray Smith was
Vic. Agent-General he had once to officially deny
the late Lord Lamington’s assertion that in Vic-
toria black labor had reached the extent of slavery.
Kow Lamington’s son, the present lord, is
Governor of that Australian province where black
labor actually is slavery.
“ There is no demand for land was the
Maoriland Tory-partv’s constant reason for op-
posing the Seddon Government’s scheme of cutting
up big estates for settlement. The other day one
of the resumed estates near Oamaru was offered
in 64 allotments, and 8000 applications were lodged
—125 for each allotment.
Most of the Bananaland Labor organs have
successfully sent an editor as candidate for Parlia-
ment Noticing which fact, defeated candidates
(in Gym pie, Cairns, &c.) are busy starting, Labor
papers to work up enthusiasm for future con-
tingencies. The Radical press up North is the
best stepping-stone into the Legislature.
The most remarkable curiosity in North
Queensland at present is the Bowen railway. In
April, ’95, this line earned .£37 15s. sd. ; in April
last the receipts reached the magnificent sum total
of 19s. 6d. Consequently, the Bowemtes are
agitating furiously for an extension—probably to
catch the whale-trade in the Never-Never.
The Reid Ministry faces Bung zigzagly
thus —Six members of the Government will vote
for Local Option without compensation. If com-
pensation be conceded, seven members will vote
for it. Two members are flatly opposed to Local
Option without compensation, but are only
theoretically opposed to it if compensation be con-
ceded and if Local Option on this basis be carried
these will then go for such ponderous compensation
as will render the measure nugatory. Local
Option or no Local Option stands, therefore as 7
to 2 • Compensation or No Compensation as 3 to b.
It is interesting, in connection with the
miscellany of legal findings and assumptions in the
Mercadool and other land cases, still pending in
N.S.W. Law courts, to note that, since the
Robertson Land Act of 1861, only one person has
been sent to gaol for dummying, and that indi-
vidual was found guilty because he himself con-
fessed.
A State school-teacher who keeps bees is
not allowed to sell any of the honey, as that would
be competing with the professional beekeeper. A
policeman whose wife keeps hens is not allowed to
sell any of the eggs, as that would be competing
with the professional poultry-keeper. And yet,
under the same civilised Government, an Attorney-
General who is paid =61820 a year is allowed to do
as much private law business as he likes.
Lothaire, the Belgian officer alleged
(by the British) to have cold - bloodedly
murdered a British trader in the Congo,
has received a tremendous reception from ap-
plauding multitudes in Belgium. And the
Englishmen who are wildly applauding Jameson
for unlawfully butchering certain Dutchmen in
South Africa, complain bitterly about this “un-
friendly act,” and think that something should be
done.
It was plausibly pleaded by certain Parlia-
mentary hangers-on of the Sydney daily press that
the reason why the papers suppressed all report of
the application to N.S.W. Full Court for a
summons against the management of Tooth and
Co., brewers, was that it was unusual to report
ex-parte proceedings. Which excuse contained
not even half the truth. The last application of
the sort (the well-known case “ ex-parte M‘Kay”)
was at once reported in full by S.M. Herald.
As a sample of the inherent crookedness
of party-government: the N.S.W. Ministry would
have been beaten by at least four votes, the other
night, over Labor-member Hughes’s motion of ad-
journment re the non-issue of a summons against
Tooth & Co., the big Sydney brewers, charged by
an informer with defrauding the revenue, had not
the Opposition refused to touch the matter, on the
ground that it wasn’t going to minister to the
Solidarity Labor-party, by whom the motion was
practically, though not avowedly, fathered.
The decision of the N.S.W. Ministry to
fix a date for the release of the surviving Mt. Rennie
prisoners is probably, in some measure, intended
to make things nice and pleasant at the present
juncture for Judge Windeyer, whose appointment
to the Privy Council the Ministry is engineering.
r public has no more memory than a mud-
Vrtle: the Judge’s friends reckon that with the
release of the survivors recollection of the “ trial ”
will fade, and all will be harmonious. When
one considers the relations publicly existent
between Judge Windeyer and Premier Reid when
the latter was at the Bar, the conclusion is
irresistible that the Premier is afnost conciliatory
person.
N.S.W. Premier Reid badly
wants to get in the income-tax in time
to square this year’s financial state-
ment, so the department, in its tax-
paper, demands the money on or before
June 25, with an intimation that “for
neglect to pay tax-payers will incur a
fine of 2s. in the £.” The Act gives 60
days grace beyond the date mentioned,
so that the fine doesn’t begin till Aug
24, but this fact is judiciously left out.
Meanwhile, the Premier lof-
tily observes that it really
doesn’t matter whether any of
the money comes in this year
or not, as the finances will
square without it, and consid-
ering that the “ cash basis ”
man has just charged back
A 900 .000 of his expenditure to
previous years it seems quite
probable that it will. When
one remembers how G. Reid
squared his accounts before
by charging some hundreds
of thousands of ordinary road
and bridge repairs back to his
predecessor, on the ground
that Dibbs ought to have spent
more money than he did, and
put nearly J 1400,000 of loan-
money into revenue because
his predecessors had been pay-
ing things out of revenue
which, he alleged, they ought
to have charged to loans, it
looks as if he could square
anything.
N.S.W. Custom House law-
needs the immediate attention of
Parliament. Imputed fraud on any
other public department may be car-
ried straight to a public law-court, but
in the case of the Customs a tribunal
may sit in secret and prevent an alleged
imposition being threshed out by legal
process. It may also, and has done so
lately in respect of charges against
import jewellers, determine, sitting in
secret, to accept a fine from accused
persons in lieu of remitting the accusa-
tions to the criminal court. In the
light of the present day things like
these can be defended on no ground of
equity or expediency.
The N.S.W. Labor-party stands at the
parting of the ways, and can now either impreg-
nably entrench itself as the champion of equal
justice to all citizens or indelibly brand itself as a
mere aggregation of blithering pay-seekers. If it
refrains from insisting that the charges of fraud
made against Tooth and Co. be heard and disposed
of in open court, it deserves to be summarily extin-
guished by the electors.
Mr. Alex. Sutherland, lecturing Melb.
Reform Club on “ The Share that Goes to Brains,
demonstrates that the worker is already too well
paid, since brains are responsible for the largest
share of production. But does that share go to
brains ? Does the monopolist represent brains ?
Does the landlord ? Do one in ten of the wealthy
represent anything but fat and greed ? The Tories
make the mistake of confounding brains and mere
paunch, and claim the former’s share on top of all
else they can extort or steal.
One reason advanced
why the proposed Syd-
ney railway - extension
should be on the east
side of George-street with
a terminus at King-
street, instead of along
the w T est side—where d
would be conveniently
situated for further ex-
tension across the harbor
when circumstances
justify the building of a
bridge—is that in the
former case Hyde Park
could be used as the site
of the new terminus,
whereas in the latter a
site would have to be
purchased at great ex-
pense. But this isn’t
strictly logical. If the
city is to lose Hyde
Park, it matters very
little whether it is used
as a site for a station
or whether it is sold for
building-purposes and a
site in the vicinity of,
say, Wynyard - Square
bought with the
money. The loss is the
same in either case, and
the Park isn’t an argu-
ment one way or the
other.
In connection with the Tooth and Co.
brewery hubble-bubble, N.S.W. Assembly dis-
tinctly demonstrated that when its talkers get on
to the subject of beer, there is no question under
the sun that they do not consider relevant to the
issue. Quite right. Beer embraces all human lile
and human interests, all weal, all woe, all joy, a
iniquity. But beer was made to be drunk, ana
not talked about. Beer was made to be drunk by
man, and man was made to be drunk with beer.
Let us leave beer alone —in speech, thougrv our
tongues and throats lave lovingly in it. or beer
is art, science, theology, psychology, literature,
music, dynamics, philology, philosophy, me a-
physics, history, medicine, numismatics, pnilace y
and delirium tremens.
HE MEANT IT.
HE: “ Since you refuse to marry me, I refuse to live.”
SHE: “ Manly?”
HE: “No! Football !”
The scandal arising out of tacit or other
interference by Government with the discretion of
the police magistracy has now reached such a point
in Australia that the day is not far distant when
stipendiary magistrates will be placed by Parlia-
ment on as independent a footing as the Supreme
Court bench. A complement of this reform will
be the abolition of the Attorney-Generalship as a
political office.
N.S.W. Treasurer Reid predicts a con-
siderable surplus for the year ending June 30. Ile
also states that over £900,000 of the expenditure
has been charged back to previous years. Isn t
this the same Reid who was going to abolish the
misleading and confused practice of charging ex-
penditure back to former years, and put things
on a simple “cash basis,” so that it would be
really possible to know how the accounts stood .
Nothing has been heard about that famous cash
basis ” for quite a long time now.
A TRULY BRITISH IDEA OF THE UNEMPLOYED.
“Pm sorry I can’t help you, my man; I’m sure it must be very hard
to live.”
“It ain’t the living, sir, ’cos the wife washes—it’s the little lux’nes we
misses !”
Fearsome Bananaland ! The rabbits are all
over the South-west; the ticks are raging in the
North ; the caterpillar is devastating the centre ;
the Jap is descending on the coast towns ; the
men of Malayta have just made a fresh outbreak,
as prelude, apparently, to a cannibal feast; and,
over all, there lies the joyous shadow of the Boodle-
wraith !
Sydney Daily Telegraph —a Pharisee of
the Pharisees of Foreign-trade—is actually seri-
ously discussing the details of a possible British
Imperial Zollverein ; is admitting into its ques-
tions of “ practical politics ” the issues of differen-
tial and preferential tariffs —in other words, of
Protectionism. Is this the first quiver of a coming
wobble ? The very same issue of the paper speaks
feelingly about “those considerations of oppor-
tunism and expediency which are the first lessons
that the practical lawmaker has to learn.” Hand
over the “ Rontgen-rays ” machine, and view ’bus-
boss Carey (the D.T. chairman) as a convert
to communistic socialism.
The Bulletin advises every N.S.W. elec-
tor to read in Hansard the clear, logical and tem-
perate speech of Hughes, M.P., in stating the
alleged case of fraud against Tooth and Co., of
Sydney, and to contrast with it the flabby irrele-
vancies of Premier Reid —representative in the
Assembly, by the way, of the liquor-interests of
East Sydney. As all details of this most startling
affair have, for whatever reason, been kept dark by
the Sydney daily press, it is essential that the
Tooth case should be read up by that section of
the public which labors under the delusion that
N.S.W. at last possesses an impartial Govern-
ment.

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

Personal Items.
A newly-admitted solicitor has forwarded
by post a photo, of himself, with “ Yours truly to
command ” underneath, to most of the mercantile
houses of Sydney.
Speaker Abbott has now held N.S.W.
chair for five years, thus tieing with Daniel
Cooper in duration of office. Wigram Allen, how-
ever, scored seven, and Arnold nine years.
Among the many bills sent in to a suc-
cessful candidate at last Q. election was one from
a man who claimed compensation for being
“ enthusiastic ” at the meetings of the aforesaid
candidate.
A Sydney Domain orator assured his au-
dience on Sunday that three N.S.W. Ministers of
the Crown have had their matrimonial affairs
adjusted by the local Divorce Court, sitting in
camera.
The veteran journalist, John Ferguson,
of Parramatta, has gone to Parramatta Gaol for
four months for a severe and stupid Little-Ped-
lingtonian libel on an alderman and Jaypee. The
air of “ the historic borough ” conduces to things
of this sort.
Because Griffith, C.J., has codified a
number of statutes, admiring claqueurs have begun
to call him the “ Queensland Higinbotham.” It
takes more to make a Higinbotham than mere
index-compiling.
On June 13 Mr. and Mrs. Ephraim Close
celebrated their Diamond wedding, having been
married at Scots Church, Church Hill, Sydney, 60
years ago, by Parson M‘Garvie, a colleague of old
John Dunmore Lang.
Sir William Harcourt recently described
young Tom Brassey—expected out here shortly
with Lady Idina Brassey—as “ a chip of the old
block.” Which implied that, in Sir Historicus’s
opinion, papa Brassey is wooden. Good !
The latest Sydney clisnaru has, up to date,
quite outwitted the detectives, who believe him to
be lying low somewhere in town. Several exten-
sive forgeries, which have hit Ikey Mo under the
fifth rib, are alleged against the gentleman.
Both Gov. Buxton’s daughters are now
engaged, the second to the dapper-looking pri vate-
sec. Conybeer. The elder daughter is shortly to
be married to the gilded Rev. Hawker, son of the
lately-deceased S.A. squatter, Geo. Hawker.
At a recent dinner to one of his party,
Premier Kingston recited “Who Fears to Speak
of ’9B ? ” But the local scribes put it down as
“ Who Dares to Speak of ’98,” and the West Ade-
laide bhoys are wondering “what has come over
Charlie ? ”
To be pasted in the hats of Lord Hamp-
den, Primate Smith, and the Sydney Charity Or-
ganisation Society :
Careless their merits or their faults to scan,
His pity gave ere charity began.
—Goldsmith.
Hercules Robinson, now in trouble over
the Transvaal raid, was the first Australian
Governor to notably forward the cause of Federa-
tion by his public speeches. To his harangues on
the theme may also be attributed Albury’s belief
that Nature intended it to be the seat of Parlia-
ment in the new nation. Robinson, it would
appear, looked on the Transvaal as another Fiji.
Englishmen threw Fiji into disorder, and set up
old chief Thakombau as king, but gradually
worked affairs around till the islands were ready
for Robinson to annex. But that was a quarter
of a century ago, and Boers aren’t Fijians.

The rule of precedence at public functions,
drawn up and signed by Card. Moran and Primate
Smith on their own behalf and as a guide to their
successors, is that the ecclesiastical dignitary,
whether C.E. or R.C., who has resided longest in
Australia goes up top.
Veteran James Smith of the Argus is
announced to lecture at Melb. Working Men’s
College on the “ Usefulness of New Ideas.” This,
from an Argus leader-writer, is simply barefaced.
Mr. Smith will presently be regarded in the office
as a most dangerous man.
Australia’s richest woman, it is announced,
will shortly marry Mr. Desmond Richardson, a
Victorian gentleman. Which would seem to
effectually dispose of the rumor that the lady,
who takes much interest in church and missionary
matters, was inclined to smile on the alleged suit
of a gartered personage adored of cabbies.
Zox, of the waistcoat, at Vic. Artists’
Society the other night, suggested that each artist
in Australia should paint a picture commemora-
tive of the 60th year of Queen Victoria’s reign,
and send it to Windsor Palace in 1897 as a present
to H.M. And yet Zox pretends to be a loyal
citizen.
Louis Becke left per s.s. Parramatta for
England, with a view to improving the market
for his literary wares. He has ready a volume of
characteristic South Sea stories, but does not pur-
pose publishing them before next year. Mean-
while, he will work for the magazines, and con-
tribute to The Bulletin. The Saturday Review
publishes a column and a-half notice warmly
eulogising his “Ebbing of the Tide.”
“Blue Ribbon” Clarke, of
Brisbane has been defying a Q.
Royal Commission to drag him to
give evidence. It would be inter-
esting to watch the Commission’s
myrmidons trying to skulldrag
the bald-getting, long-legged left-
handed (Clarke is left-
handed in everything but
carving), ropable, Gospel-
temperance lecturer. The
writer once saw a wicked-
looking, muscular, color-
ed gentleman, a Tem-
perance “ brother,” try
conclusions with the bony
John A. C. The nigger
was “on the bounce,”
and had a reputation as
dark as his skin for his
supposed fistic abilities.
He sailed in. The apos-
tle of Gospel-temperance
met him more than half-
way, and landed him one
with his left [his right)
that sent him fairly
across a moderately-
sized office and bang
against the door - (the
darkie had closed the
door in order to prevent
Clarke from running
when the ebony circus
was started !)—met him
on the rebound with “ the other right,”
and “ chasseed ” him into dizziness before the
writer had time to get into a safe corner
from which to look on. Weak and trembling with
fear, the nigger turned to go. “ No, you don’t ! ”
said the long mail of anti-beer, as he strode to the
door; “first of'all apologise, and then pay up ! ”
“I will, Mister Clarke, I will,” whimpered the
quite tamed negro—a full-blooded bully lie was,
too, and married to a fine white woman—“l will,
but, by Ga a d ! I thought you was a man of Gawd ! ”
—Dipso.
The valuable old newspapers that have
mysteriously disappeared from Melbourne Public
Library included the story of the late James B.
Patterson’s little cattle-trouble. And the S.M.
Herald file reporting the Mt. Rennie trial has
gone from Sydney School of Arts.
Canon Potter utters a dyspeptic wail about
Democracy in Melbourne, evidently after a dive

B. R. Wise is said to have found a number
of sensational letters among the Parkes papers com-
mitted to his trust. Purcell’s revelations concern-
ing Manning are mild tilings compared with what
some of the documents tell of Parkes. And several
high-headed people who passed as the warmest
friends of politician Reid were, it seems, all the
time secretly informing Parkes by letter that the
little man was only fit to run a lollie-shop. If
Wise would resist the temptation to edit, and just
print the lot as it comes to hand, N.S.W. politics
would be lively for quite a long time.

PRACTICAL.
FOND MOTHER (in Backblocks): “Now, dear, if
you are bad you will go to where the big five
is. Bat if you are a good little boy you’ll go
to the good place where the angels are, and
hear the singing and music and all nice
things. ”
TOMMY : “ But, mum, ain’t there no fire in the good
place?”
F. M.: No, Tommy, dear.”
TOMMY : “ Then how do the angels boil the billy?”
The Bulletin has said before—or, if it
hasn’t, it should have said—that there is a fortune
awaiting the compiler and publisher of Youliana.
One of the latest ana :
The Coroner (Youll) remarked that the woman should
have been tapped. He knew of a man who had had quarts
of fluid taken from his lungs, and the same night that
man had danced in a pantomime. (Laughter).
Mr. Corr : But what became of the man the day after ?
The Coroner : He’s alive to-day. He makes a noise at
the back of my house, and I wish he was dead.
Hideously funny picture ! An escaped subject of
Dr. Youll making night horrible for the person
who intended to dissect him and make humorous
comments upon his anatomical structure !
In Franklin’s life it is stated that Disraeli
once thought of visiting Australia. This was, of
course, long before the gold-discoveries which first
made the existence of Australia known to the mass
of Englishmen. Up to the gold-epoch, the average
conception of these countries was scarcely more
intelligent than that of Castlereagh, who took Gov.
Macquarie to task for not building a bridge over
Bass Straits.
The late “Tarbrush” Woods, of Yic.
railway fame, was a terrible dyspeptic, and abso-
lutely detested seeing anybody enjoy himself at
table. At a banquet in Rochester a local gour-
mand who sat near the Minister and had been
vigorously stoking himself was asked by a waiter
“Can I bring you anything else, sir?” Where-
upon the disgusted and curious Woods chipped in
with : “Yes, bring the a bran-mash.”
Saturday Review on Rolf Boldrewood’s
atest: —-
Here is a man with a vast island-continent at his call,
so to speak, for material, and for the major portion of the
book we are shut up with a few ordinary, middle-class
English people in a drawing-room of unmistakeably sub-
urban origin—“ dado,” “ Erard grand,” and “ half-exe-
cuted needlework.” Meanwhile, no doubt, suburban
novelists are hard at work making Australian local color.
The continent at Boldrewood’s “ beck ” and call
seems almost satirical. The Saturday should
really have added the final “e.”
Charles Gavan Duffy, who for 15 years has
lived at Nice, enjoying his £lOOO a year Victorian
pension, lately celebrated his 80th birthday.
Although the most delicate in health, he has out-
lived all his political compeers of 1844 and ’4B ex-
cepting one, Dr. Kevin Izod O’Doherty, of Queens-
land. Of the first Ministry which he joined in
Victoria (1857), but J. D. Wood remains; of the
second Ministry (’5B), all but Duffy are dead, and
of the third (O’Shannassy Govt., ’6l), but three
others remain—J. D. Wood, J. S. Johnston, and
Duffy.
Old Coroner Youl, the Melb. humorist,
waxes especially funny at the expense of the ac-
complished Dr. Mullen, who is generally under-
stood to be waiting for the veteran’s shoes.
Mullen has doubly qualified himself by becoming
a lawyer as well as a medicine man, and when he
heard a whisper that Youl’s successor would be
chosen from the Civil-service he took a Govern-
ment appointment at some small infirmary, or
asylum, in order to be more qualified than ever.
Whereupon the reigning coroner was chaffed by his
friends. They wanted to know how he could hope
to defeat the watchful Mullen’s purpose after
that. “ I’ll outlive him,” said Youl. The grand
old man, it seems, has a talent for outliving every-
one who calculates on his decease. “And, as a
rule,” says he, “ I hold an inquest on ’em.”
J. C. F. Johnson is at present a howling
swell in London, where he has just sold a .£lOO,OOO
Westralian gold-mine. He presided over the
“gold-grabbers’” table at the anniversary lun-
cheon of the Colonial Institute. About 20 years
ago J.C.F.J. was on the wallaby in S.A. His
first billet in S.A. Register office, in the publish-
ing department, is» said to have been worth quite
15 bob a week. Then the energetic one wormed
in as a reporter, the “shows” being his specialty.
He is a good amateur conjuror and knows a
wizardy secret or two in the matter of optical
illusions—ghosts, “Fatimas,” levitated humans,
&c. He ran one or two small specs, of the kind
in Adelaide, as side-shows to Adelaide Punch, of
which he became proprietor. The story of his
first rise in connection with gold-mining is quite
a dramatic tale of a prospector’s gratitude.
Poor old “Billy” Pattison,” of Mount
Morgan, is dead. Pattison was a peaceful butcher
at Rockhampton when his ill-luck led him into the
golden Mount and he became a millionaire. Then
he went into Parliament, and all manner of people
lived on the well-meaning meat-man, and let him
into all manner of splendid speculations, and the
result was that after a brief spell of magnificence
he came back to his former haunts as poor
as before Mount Morgan began. Also, he came
back older, disillusioned, worn out with politics
and with the constant struggle to keep himself
from being cleaned out by politicians, loaded
with dishonored bills and bad I O U’s, and partly
paralysed. Indirectly it would seem that the
golden mount killed Pattison, who was a decent
old man and deserved better luck.
Captain Russell, leader of the Tory Oppo-
sition-party in Maoriland Parliament, complains
bitterly that things “ain’t what they was.” When
he first arrived in the province in ’5B, as a young
officer of the army, said the venerable politician
recently, men depended on themselves ; “ but the
old self-reliance has departed, giving place to
dependence on Government.” Whereupon some
one unkindly points out that in these days a mili-
tary officer, after five years’ residence, was entitled
to a free grant of 400 acres ; but that Russell, after
only three and a-half years’ residence, howled so
bitterly outside Parliament House for a special
grant that an Act was passed giving him 800
acres gratis. Also that his father, then a politi-
cian who had also grabbed much land gratis, voted
for the measure and shoved it through the nominee
Legislature with much vim. Australasia owes
a big debt to the pioneers who came here
in the old, wicked, roaring days, and took
the land for nothing, and borrowed the money
to open it up with roads and railways and
harbors, and left the present generation to wrestle
with the bill for these improvements to the old
Captain RUSSELL, Leader of the M.L-
Opposition against the Seddon Govt.
Photo, by Wrigglesworth & Birins, Wellington.
men’s property. And now the survivors of these
pioneers put on airs of superiority about their
self-reliance, and ask the people of to-day—who
are engaged struggling with the bill aforesaid-
why they don’t do likewise. There were some
valuable men among the old identities, but the
best thing aUcut the great majority of them is
that they are dead and seem likely to remain so.
Their deadness, in most cases, deserves the warm-
est encouragement.


The Queensland deer on Maryvale and
Cressbrook don’t increase very fast. The indis-
criminate “ sport ” is their worst foe ; then comes
the eagle-hawk, which attacks the fawns; and,
lastly, the native dog, which finds venison an ex-
cellent substitute for kangaroo.
A. W eatralian item : —George Taylor, who
sacrificed himself for the Q. shearers during the
90 strike, is reefing at Lake Darldt, W. A. He is in
charge of the camels, and is known as “ Afghan
George.” He is quite as vain as ever, but does
not blacken his boots on the track as of yore.
Beware!

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
qnyer of all other Australian gems.*

dissembling name.

This company is being formed for the purpose of
acquiring and working the gold leases numbered
23/90, 29/90, 30/90, situate at Mount Lyell, County
of Montagu, in the Colony of Tasmania, now held
by the North Mount Lyell Mining Company No
Liability.
REPORT ON THE PROPERTY BY MR. H.
S. MUIR.
Mount Lyell,
Tasmania, May 23, 1896.
The Chairman and Directors of the “ North Mount
Lyell Mining Company.”
Gentlemen, —
Having had the opportunity during the past two
years of thoroughly examining the progressive
prospecting work at the North Mount Lyell mine,
and also its surface indications in relation to other
surrounding mines, I now have the pleasure of
supplying you with the following particulars of
development made since the mine has been opened,
and inferences that may reasonably be drawn
from the data at hand and the developments of the
field generally.
SITUATION, &c.
The North Mount Lyell property consists of
three 10-acre blocks held under gold lease (which
embraces all other minerals), numbered 23/90,
29/90, and 30/90, being situated on the southern
slope of Mount Lyell, and the eastern slope of the
ridge between Mounts “Lyell” and “Owen.”
The general fall of the ground being from the west
to the east, it will be seen that the property is
adapted for working by adit levels, which is of
great importance for economical mining.
SURFACE, &c.
At about the centre of the property, and travers-
ing sections 23/90 and 29/90, occurs a formation of
barytic “hematite” and “quartzite,” which
marks the course of the metalliferous ore belt
underlying to the west. This hematite and
quartzite outcrop usually lays on a conglomerate
deposit, which forms the footwall of the ore
bodies.
The strike of this lode through sections 23/90 and
29/90 is west of north over the whole distance, but
subject to sharp deviations. The rock outcrop to
the west of this hematite lode is an iron-stained
schist, and is the locale where pyrites ore veins
will be found, therefore the whole of the property
is so positioned that the lode is contained well
within it. .
From the intersection of the hematite with the
gullies and following the natural fall of the country
east can be found alluvial deposits of gravel
carrying gold and copper, also large deposits of
pug on the course of the creeks, containing native
copper (leached from the lodes) is added evidence
of the extent of the ore bodies that will be found
in the development of the mine.
ORE OUTCROPS.
On section 29/90 an outcrop of dense copper
pyrites (contained in schist) has been trenched on
in various places, and to prospect this a tunnel
was started on section 23/90 to intersect the ore
body.
At 200 ft. in from opening an ore body (crubes-
cite) of high grade, corresponding with that on
surface, was passed through, its strike being to
east of north. This important development occur-
ring in soft ground has not been further explored.
The surrounding country being of a swelling
nature, it has been left for future prospecting.
UNDERGROUND WORKINGS.
The underground workings consist of a main
tunnel 420 ft. in length, passing through mostly
Prospectus.
broken country. From the head of main tunnel a
western crosscut (220 ft.) intersects the stratified
schists, showing them to be highly mineralised.
A south drive off western crosscut, commencing
82ft. from end of main tunnel, has been following
a body of copper pyrites and erubescite. This
drive follows the footwall of the ore formation for
about 70ft., and is most likely a portion of the
main “ ore body,” marked on surface by the hema-
tite.
A “winze” being put down in the ore body
near the footwall is opening up well, showing a
marked improvement in every foot of sinking,
some veins being very rich. The value and appear-
ance of the ore to-day at this level is highly satis-
factory, being only 120 ft. beneath the natural
surface.
Taking the rock formations covering the surface,
together with the development underground, it
proves that the metalliferous lodes exist at the
junction of the “ conglomerate ” with the “schist”
rocks, mostly contained in the schist, and under-
lying west, also, that these lodes have been de-
nuded of their metals on the surface and to some
depth below, by natural leaching, the lost product
being found at a lower level in the pug deposit in
the form of native copper. It will at once be seen
the importance of intersecting the lode at a depth
where it may safely be assumed ore will be found
of higher value and intact.
As the mine is situated so that fairly deep pro-
specting can be got by adit, it may be expected
that large bodies of payable ore will be met with
as the mine is opened up by this means. The
underground workings having already proved the
existence of payable ore at the level now being
explored, it now requires greater depth (which can
be got by adit) to further determine the extent and
quality of the lode', and from evidence deduced
from immediate surroundings and the workings of
other mines it suggests that considerable improve-
ment in size and quality will be found than that
existing in the upper levels.
I am, gentleman,
Yours faithfully,
H. S. MUIR,
Manager Mount Lyell Extended
and Consols Mine.

CONSULTING ENGINEERS.
Messrs. J. Wildridge and Sinclair, Consulting and
Refrigerating Engineers.
It is proposed to form a company for the purpose of
erecting cold storage works at Fremantle, Western Aus-
tralia, and suitable inland mining centres, and providing
regular supplies of cattle, sheep, beef, mutton, poultry,
dairy produce, fish, &c., in Western Australia, and, if
thought desirable, to hereinafter extend the operations of
the company to other Australian colonies. Powerful ice-
making machinery will be worked in conjunction with
the cold-storage depots. It is proposed that the main
depot shall be at Fremantle, from which smaller inland
depots will draw their requirements.
It is intended to erect works on the dry-air system. The
estimates of Messrs. Wildridge and Sinclair for the works
at Fremantle, including duplicate portions where advis-
able, with capacity for 10,000 sheep, or equivalent in
other products, and a daily output of
Eight tons ice is (*see footnote) £9,500
Works at Kalgoorlie, the present terminus
of the railway 1,500
Sites and contingencies 1,500
£12,500
This will leave a balance of £12,500, less preliminary ex-
penses for working-capital, &c.
*Note. —Alternative works for 15,000 sheep and 12 tons
ice, £14,000.
It is considered that for the present Sydney is the most
suitable port for the purchase and shipment of frozen
meat, the facilities in all respects being most convenient.
Satisfactory provisional arrangements have been made to
this end, which can be definitely concluded at any
moment; as also the provision of the necessary freight
tonnage should local steamers be unwilling to enter into
the trade.
It is well known that Western Australia cannot supply
its already large and rapidly-growing population with
suitable food requirements. The quality of the food at
the present time is very inferior. Already the import of
livestock is assuming large proportions, and it is con-
sidered very certain that with regular supplies of prime
quality always on hand this company can practically com-
mand a very considerable portion of the trade.
It is expected within a short time of the commencement
of operations to work into an output of at least 2500 sheep
per week, and an equivalent in beef or other meat, which,
at even a small profit per pound, will show a most ample
net return.
For poultry, fish, dairy produce, fruit, and other com-
modities a very large profit can be looked for, and from
the sale of ice a handsome income during the summer
months should result.
The promoters have sent gentlemen in whom they place
reliance to Western Australia, who have returned fully
assured that a good business can be done, and who are
satisfied with the arrangements of the Government for
cool carriage on the railways.
The Railway department already have on hand six
refrigerating cars, and 12 more are in course of construc-
tion.
The state of public feeling on the gold-fields points to
the removal at no distant date of the present duty of ljd.
per lb. on fresh meat. This would materially increase the
prospects of the company’s business.
The company has secured the pecuniary support and
co-operation of Messrs. Wildridge and Sinclair, electrical
engineers, Sydney, who have had large experience in the
frozen meat trade, and are willing to take a considerable
interest in shares of the company. The necessary
machinery can be landed and erected in ample time to
meet the coming summer’s requirements.
An agreements dated the 28th day of May, 1896, has
been entered into between the promoters on behalf of the
proposed company and Hurtle Morphett. This agreement
may be inspected at the offices of the solicitors.
Upon the formation of the company a meeting of
allottees of shares will be held in accordance with “ clause
17 of the Companies Act of 1892,” for the purpose of
adopting memorandum and articles of association, for the
election of directors, &c.
Dated at Adelaide this 20th day of May, 1896.
J. B. WERE and SON, Brokers.

An historic house, in Woolloomooloo, a
relic of the days of the System, will be auctioned
next week. Barham Hall, Bourke-st., was built
by Col.-Secretary E. Deas-Thomson many years
ago when the neighborhood was almost bush.
Old pictures of Sydney show the house in course
of erection and standing alone crowning the hill.

pROSPECTUS OF THE
New MoQarcb Coippaijy.
Corindhap, Rokewood.
To be Registered as a No Liability Company under
Part 11. of the Companies Act, 1890.
Capital—.£l2,soo, in 25,000 Shares of 10s. Each.
No Royalty to paid-up Shares.
16,000 shares are offered to the public at 2s. each.
The whole of the proceeds from such sale (less
brokerage and flotation expenses) to be placed to
the credit of the company. 9000 shares to be
retained by the vendors, who are also to be paid
<£looo for the mine and the machinery—viz.,
,£5OO on the registration of the company and

Provisional Directors —Josiah Mager, Esq.,
Geelong; J. Y. McDonald, Esq., Ballarat;
Walter P. Wynne, Esq., Ballarat; John Cham-
pion, Esq., Ballarat; Robt. F. Bryant, Esq.,
Ballarat.
Legal Manager
(To be appointed by the shareholders.)
This company is being formed for the purpose
of purchasing and developing the valuable gold-
mining lease and machinery formerly owned by
the Monarch Company at Corindhap, Rokewood,
together with a first-class 10-head battery in course
of erection by the vendors.
The lease (No. 2405) contains an area of 29 acres
3 roods 30 perches, situate on the main French-
man’s lode, at Rokewood ; also an application for
another area of 25 acres to the east of the jjresent
lease, which, when granted, will be transferred to
the company.
The main shaft, on which the plant is erected,
is divided mto three compartments, sunk to a depth
of 208 ft., and a crosscut opened out to the west.
There is also a tunnel 330 ft. into the hill, from
which a good quantity of stone is obtainable. Two
known lodes traverse the property, from which
highly payable stone was obtained down to the
water-level, but further operations were hampered
owing to lack of capital and not having a battery
on the ground to treat the stone.
The machinery comprises a complete and power-
ful winding plant, capable of working to a depth
of 1500 ft., and the mine is well furnished with
trucks, cages, ropes, tanks, &c. ; also a good 10-
head battery with all necessary appertenances.
The whole of the plant is in perfect order, and has
been erected in a capable manner, with due regard
to economy in working.
The amount to be placed to the credit of the
company should provide funds sufficient to reach
the lode and open up the mine. It is not antici-
pated calls will be necessary ; in any case the calls
will not be heavy, the machinery being paid for,
and not much dead work to be done.
Early application for shares is necessary, as the
list will close immediately all the shares have been
applied for.
Applications for shares, accompanied with cash
or cheque, will be received by
BELL, LAMBERT, and Co., Sharebrokers.
Lydiard-street, Ballarat.
Prospectuses, reports, and all information
obtainable from the brokers.
CHARLE S WESTBROOK
116 Pitt Street, SYDNEY (opposite G.P.0.).

The aquatic contest. Pirates v. Wallaroo, was a
better show than anticipated. Pirates started with a
dozen, and played three pick-ups. Even then, had their
backs shaped in style, they might have got closer. The
ground was suitable for a model-yacht race, and players
floundered about looking for the ball, which got lost
occasionally. It was reminiscent of wading through a
swamp after a wounded wild-duck. The marsupials won
by 11 lengths ; scores, 16 to 5.
» » * *
The Wentworth-Paddington match was got off
on a sliding-scale. The slippery ground being adverse to
hoof locomotion, pulling down, getting up, and mud-
munching at intervals occupied most of the time, and the
ball got more peace than is customary. Paddingtons,
strengthened somewhat, surprised everybody who, a week
previously, deemed them the veriest novices. The W’s.
only got over their line to score once. This they equalised,
but a free and a mark gave Wentworths the win by 10 to 3.
* * * *
Melb. senior football clubs settlein to their premier-
ship positions. Undefeated Essendon leads Collingwood
by 4 points, others nowhere. South, third on the list,
was beaten, 6 goals to 4 goals, on Saturday, by Essendon,
Melb. and Ports also losing to Collingwood and N. Melb.
Fitzroy, on the improve, beat Geelong, 4 goals to 3 goals—
four sterling matches, and not one good crowd.
V.F. A., now in the throes of the professionalism
trouble, begins to see that the game is losing ground.
Committees are to be appointed to thresh out the payment
question, and also to devise means for making the game
more popular. Too late !
* * * *
Coolgardie papers give details of Sunday foot-
ball tussles controlled by the local association. The only
published complaint concerns the two-hour use of the
great Australian adjective in both its original and up-to-
date form. As Coolgardie is itself one huge curse the
item was probably shoved in to fill up space.
* * * *
Queensland Rugby authorities are searching the
province for the best talent available for *he trip to M.L.,
where they play seven matches, incl ding one against all
M.L. The selection will be the strong to. .aid heaviest yet
made, and bulky forwards are in demand. Three parti-
cularly fat Sandows are said to be playing at Charters
Towers, and one or two will likely be picked. The fatman
curse seems to pervade even football.
* * * •
Victorian football, in its boomtime, was cracked
up to be the perfection of all leather-kicking games. Now
that football is dropping out of favor with everybody save
the Collingwood and South Melbourne toughs, the high
priests hope to bolster it up with improvements. The
situation is pathetic.
* * * *
The milk in the football cocoanut. There are
11 lacrosse teams in Melb. and 14 in Adelaide. There is
only one junior football association in Adelaide, and as
fast as football clubs die their grounds are taken by
lacrosse clubs. When junior football declines, then comes
the beginning of the end.
* * * *
Various Sydney papers are now advocating the
electoral football scheme. Rather late in the day, but
early enough for next season. The possibility of the
Randwicks being “too good” for the rest, and the unlike-
lihood of the public paying to see “walkovers,” are now
troubling even the thick-headed Rugby Union. That
“ ground of their own ” and proposed ’97 ’Ome' trip doesn’t
look like realisation at present.
* * * *
The alleged drowning of the champion will
arouse public interest in his go with Harding next month.
London papers, by jthe way, say that when the Hawkes-
bury giant arrived at his “diggings,” and was introduced
to his opponent, the latter, who gazed long and curiously,
looked like Jim’s little brother.
* * * *
One of Stanbury’s mates: “ Does the paper say
the body has been recovered?” “No.” “Well, then
Jim must have recovered it himself.” And so it proved.

as trainer, ought to be about good enough to put down

* * * *
The unconvinced Clarence River choppers put
up their cash royally, the other day, for a second tilt at
M'Pherson (Tas.). They parted without a murmur. Cur-
ran, varying the style exhibited when he lost the cham-
pionship, chopped Mac. for £25. Mac. won. Then
M’Lennon and Dwyer sawed M‘Pherson and Moore (Tas.)
for a tenner, and the latter had the cash won before their
opponents were half through. Not content, Dwyer put
up a stake for a standing-tree match, M’Pherson romping
in by lengths. The Tas. champion is now prepared to
“ wrastle ” wood with any man m the world, three styles
or mixed.
* * * *
Favorite Dart, carrying 12st., was far and away
the best of the seven saddled for Y.R.C. Steeple on Satur-
day. The poverty of the field, so far as good ’uns went,
was due to obvious causes —disinclination to show an early
hand before the appearance of the G.N. weights being
one. The Millers are particularly susceptible to small
fish, but Dart on this occasion went down before Rorke’s
Drift, who had a 211 b. pull. If his leg stands, however,
Dart will likely win a good ’chase ere many weeks roll on.
* * ♦ »
The real truth about that little “ringin’’ up
Wodonga way is badly wanted. A city mare won a
double under a slightly-altered name, only her party’s
“ commissioner” being present. Her seemingly mythical
owner didn’t turn up even at the enquiry, when all three
were passed out. The “commissioner,” a known man,
now appeals on his own behalf, and if a bookie and a civil
servant are shivering in the background, as hinted, it’s to
be hoped they aren’t influential enough to stay further
proceedings.

TheV.A.T.C. vainly believed that owners and
breeders would see them through with their rich First

Instead of fixing a <£so stake value for selling-
races, the A.J.C. should have wiped these smellful con-
tests out altogether. An event for bona-fide knock-kneed,
pigeon-toed butchers’ hacks would be much more interest-
ing, and provide better sport.
* * * *
Even a cursory glance at V.R.O. G.N. weights
furnishes a puzzling double-problem. A great many of
the entrants do not possess the requisite stamina, but as
the element of .luck enters largely into the question, that
doesn't always count. Also, the majority are one-pace
horses, and haven’t much chance in a go-all-the-way
gallop, especially when the fences are high, as on Flem-
fngton. Redleap has a winning chance in the hurdles
with 12st. 131 b., and, judging by the company he has to
keep, is not out of the steeple with the figures reversed.
But he is not worth discussing until one knows he’s
going:. His flat-racing proclivities have earned novice
Preston a startler in the hurdles, but Manifold’s string
appears to be justly dealt with in both events. One blot
in the big-timber race is Piritea’s 12st. The M.L. horse
might have had a chance with a stone and a-half less.
Mutiny also has too much, but he is a more brilliant
horse than his mate. On the whole, the Manifold stable
has a high chance of scooping the double.
* * * *
The scratching of Persimmon for the Two
Thousand Guineas was delayed until a week or so before
the race, wherefore “ Wales ” was rather freely anathema-
tised.
» • * *
When The Harvester showed a fine winning
front as a three-year-old, owner Cook had visions of a
future Melb. Cup. Sheet Anchor’s fast son was with-
drawn a month or so before the big race last year, and,
according to current rumors, it is a pound to a parsnip he
won’t start this time.
* » * *
Dunlop, inventor of the pneumatic bicycle-tyre,
less than 10 years ago, made a stroke more generally ap-
preciated than any discovery since the telephone. Yet he
has never ridden a bicycle to this day. He only accom-
plished the invention to make a toy bicycle for his little
son. Cros, the exploiter, rapidly swims up as top-dog
English millionaire.
* * * *
There is nothing wonderful about sprinter
Hutchins’ defeat of cyclist Osborne at ’Ome over 100yds.
A flying ped. ought to romp in every time. He attains
top speed long before his opponent (not allowed a shove-
off) gets under weigh. Hutchins was 10yds. ahead at fifty,
and Osborne only gained 6yds. afterwards. Starting a
bike yourself is much slower than getting up steam on a
horse. The writer saw a champion ped. beaten twice by
a fast-beginning horse over 50yds. Confident of victory,
he lined up a third time. But the prad was a man-eater,
and, turning round, caressingly chewed his arm. The
ped. has never hankered after horse-matches since.
» * * *
Full programmes of the pettiest race-meetings
are published in Melb. dailies to the disgust of ordinary
readers. Half-a-column devoted to lists of entries for
little £lO handicaps, whose top-weight wouldn’t fetch
much more than a “ tenner” at auction. Yet there must
be jugginses to keep these rag-tag and wrangle meetings
going.
* * • *
The major club up N.Q. way has a bad pain in
its temper just now. Three defaulters were struck off for
life ; a bookie who swore at sundry punters was fined £5O;
the stiff brigade received seasonable consideration, and
an alleged pressman was disqualified for life from entering
any course in North Q. for using gutter language on
Queen’s Birthday. Stiff !
• * * *
The wholesale way in which Sydney papers
ladle out tips for the daily trumpery lilliputian flutters re-
gardless of whether the mokes are likely starters, triers,
or are in or out of form, prompts the idea that a set of
forecasts for each club is kept standing, and inserted with
periodical changes, advt. fashion, as occasion requires.
The tips seldom come off because the writers, who don’t
profess to be endowed with the Divine gift of sporting
prophecy in connection with swindles, have no special in-
formation on the subject, and select promiscuously, their
insidious advice being showered on a large number of in-
offensive people who have never done them any harm.
Pony fixtures receive as much attention as a Melb. Cup
carnival. Cause : the all-powerful advt., of which a
quarter-column of prognostications is the outcome. If
anyone gets his money’s worth out of the press it is the
private sport runner and Ikey Mo.
* * * *
Mr. C. E. Greener, of the eminent gun firm,
returns to England in a few weeks’ time, and will take
with him the vivid memory of the two crimes perpetrated
against him in Australia. The Abrahams filched from
Mr. Greener his good name, and the Melb. lawyers helped
themselves to Mr. Greener’s purse. Australian law
allowed Mr. Greener to buy his own name back for £4OOO.
Mr. Greener, before leaving, has a few words for
Australian sportsmen : “ Remember that at 40yds. your
charge is stringing 15ft. and the bulk of the charge 10ft.
That is with an ordinarily good choke; shot from a
cylinder barrel strings 30ft. At 60yds. the shot has
dropped three feet below the height of your barrel—this,
perhaps, is why you missed the duck so often. Use the
best chilled shot—the velocity of the charge up the barrel
is as much as 1100 ft. a second—conclude how much the
compression will flatten softer pellets. The fulminate of
mercury in good cartridge caps, and the bichloride of
potash in cheaper makes—especially the latter, rusts your
gun to its deterioration. Therefore, after a day’s sport,
clean your gun as soon as possible, using turps or benzine.
Dry the barrel thoroughly, and then smear it inside and
out with rangoon oil. Greener guns won £7OO in prizes
alone at Monte Carlo last season.”
* * * *
Ascot (Vic.) Pony R.C. offers Mr. Buckland
£5O, beside the stake, should he trot Fritz the mile in 2.10
or under at its July meeting. Pretty safe !
* * * *
The poor, loyal, old S.M. Herald just now evi-
dently dunno where it are (writes a correspondent):—“ His
Ex. the Governor, accompanied by Capt. Sloane Stanley,
A.D.C., Captain the Hon. F. Egerton, A.D.C., and the
Hon. Gathorne Hardy, P.S., returned to Sydney by the
early morning train on Saturday from Mullengudgerv, on
the Western railway line, to which place they had gone
on a shooting excursion at the beginning of last week.
. . . Altogether, there were about 12 guns in the
party, and very good sport was obtained, chiefly duck and
wild turkey, of which a large number were bagged. . . .
The experience was a novel and enjoyable one, and his
Excellency was very much pleased with the expedi-
tion.” All that is needed to complete his Ex-
cellency’s pleasure is a police prosecution against
himself and “shoot” for killing native game
out of season. (It isn’t out of season, but Tire Bulletin
hasn’t the heart to spoil a good par.) Why, it’s levy and
distress to be found with a 4oz. teal in one’s pos-
session at this time of the year (or it would be if
this was the close season, which for the pur-
poses of this paragraph it really ought to be).
Further down the Herald’s paragraph “ Last even-
ing a small dinner-party was given at Government
House, the guests being Mrs. Brough, Mr. Boucicault, the
Hon. Rupert and Mrs. Carington, Mr. and Mrs. W. R.
Mitchell, and the members of the house party.”’ Which
causes one to wonder whether in the prosecution that
must now happen, the people who helped His Ex. to put
away the illegal game (we mean the game that might
have been illegal) will be held particeps criminis.
Cutting from police-report of next week : “ Florence
Brough, charged with eating at Guvmentouse ducks
shot on Mullengudgery during the close season, pleaded
guilty, and was fined £5.” (What a pity this isn’t the
close season!)
* * * *
From a monetary aspect Coolangatta stud-sale
was a disheartening fiasco. A “ century ” was the highest
figure reached, and the majority went back to the pad-
docks. Horses now-a-days don’t total much if they have
to go under the hammer.
* * * *
True Blue, Sydney’s most promising mainstay
in spring interj rovincial races, will be burnished up on
the Newcastle track, owner Hooke having an alleged
aversion to the Randwick tan. Which may be so, besides
there aren’t so many prying eyes up-country.
* * * *
The wisdom exercised by the A. J.O. in binding
proprietary clubs to a £4OO programme, instead of £350
as heretofore, will have due effect at a later stage, when
Sydney people get horse-sick and the boom bursts. A
£5O rise is a painful knock for the proprietaries, but that
doesn’t matter. It is to be hoped that the A.J.C. will
next year increase it to £450, or to any amount that will
ensure less racing, or knock half the clubs on the head
altogether. There is far too much horse sport in the
saddle-ridden metropolis, and compulsory high values is
one step towards reducing it. When the M.L. racing
conference a few years ago raised the added money all
round, puny clubs that weren’t required died on the spot.
And when the Govt, restricted tote licenses, others were
buried also. And still the cry, “Too much racing,” is
heard in the land.
* * * »
Now whispered that the alleged forestalling of a
certain stable awhile back was simply turfy moonshine.
When people learned that the horse was good enough
Ihey rushed in, took current odds, and Ikey Mo fattened
or a fortnight. The horse was out of sorts all the time,
and didn’t start.
* * * *
Pickles will likely be the smallest of the Caul-
field Cup starters this j’ear. His owner fully believes the
little ’un is up to a fast mile and a half. As a rule, Caul-
field pickles are pretty hot, and the bottled up, well-pre-
served brand finds most favor.

Thirteen members of the new S.A. Assem-
bly are South Australian born.
Chief-Justice Madden told a Melb. jury,
the other day, that “ perjury of a superlatively
audacious character ” had been committed by one
side, or other, in a seduction-case. Superlatively
audacious nonsense. Mr. Madden has heard plenty
of such swearing and counter-swearing in divorce-
cases. And isn’t baby - maintenance a fruitful
subject for perjury?
Another letter a Gippsland (Vic.) M.P-
long carried about in his pocket-book for the edifi-
cation of his friends :—
Honerd Sir, —You no me I kanfist for your last ’lection
and got you lots votes. Hogan at —— was goin to vote
for , but I told him no, so he vote for you. Reed and
Kato and White, they was goin to vote for him to, but I
ast them notto. You better have me made into a J.P.
Esq. cordin to promist, elst I wont ast nobody vote you
next lection.—Yours trooly &c.,
That active and intelligent elector is now, it is
hardly necessary to say, a J.P. and an honor to
his country.
“In each of Mr. Wilde’s plays there has
been one really profound saying, which serves to
mark it in my memory. In ‘ Lady Windermere’s
Fan ’ it was : ‘ There are only two tragedies _ in
life: not getting what you want —and getting
it.’ In ‘A Woman of No Importance’ it was :
‘ Thought is in its essence destructive ; nothing
survives being thought of.’ In ‘ An Ideal Hus-
band ’ it was : ‘ Vulgarity is the behaviour of other
people.’ Simple as it seems, there is in this a
world of observation and instruction. Oscar
Wilde is a born playwright, who happens also to
be a man of letters, both born and bred.” Thus
William Archer.
Oongregationalist parson Ilayner, of
Glenelg (S.A.), “ can sing the National Anathema
with fervour,” but likes the second verse
omitted. S.A. ’Tiser, with a local glare at its
man, presumes that the parson can’t square his
peace-and-goodwill conscience with the blood-
thirsty request to the Almighty to “ scatter her
enemies and make them fall.” If that’s the hitch,
pastor Rayner should omit the first verse also,
since “ send her victorious” can only be an in-
direct hint to the Deity to wipe out somebody
else. The straw-splittings of the average Kerris-
tian conscience are as trivial as the average
lawyer's fiddle-faddle. Question: How much
would the “goodwill” of existing Kerristianity,
as “ a going concern,” fetch in the open market ?
Dear Bulletin, —A last word. The truth
of the snake-swallowing controversy is (vide my
writings on the subject S.M. Herald, March, ’88)
that the mother-snake calls her young in strange
hissing or clucking accents, opens her mouth
widely, and the young ophidians rush to her, as
chickens would to a hen, and glide down her
throat. I have known the Lace Lizard (Varanus
Variegata, Shaw), when its “ nest ” has been dis-
turbed, to scratch up its eggs, swallow and carry
them a distance of 15 chains, scratch out a fresh
“nest” or hole in the ground, disgorge the eggs
into it, and cover them up. Was this also canni-
balism?— H. J. M‘Cooey.

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I pan with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully, .
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

A woman walked unobserved into an open
cutting where blasting was going on in Omeo dis-
trict the other day. Only her hat was destroyed
in the concussion that followed. “The woman
devoutly thanked God for her deliverance,” says a
local paper. From which The Bulletin concludes
that the hat was pretty old.
King O’Malley, M.P. (S.A. )
It was because men drank champagne and gave up been
ate heavy dinners when they had been used to bug-back,
and because they became swills and puffed up, that God
raised up the Labor-party.
Bachelor O’Malley, by the way, created a sensa-
tion on a recent ministerial tour through his dis-
trict by nursing a lady elector’s baby on the
platform.
The Presbytery of Melbourne South lately
passed a solemn resolution to hold its meetings
always on the first Monday in each month, except
when Christmas Day (25th), Queen’s Birthday
(24th), or Prince of Wales’s Birthday (9th) falls
on such day. After awhile the collection of sour
saints became aware that the first Monday in each
month can’t happen later than the 7th, and the
resolution was amended. Then a saint wanted to
make special provision in case Good Friday fell on
the first Monday in the month.
Evangelist M‘Neill, holding forth at Balla-
rat to a Baptist congregation, pronounced the
doom of the Brassey family. The preacher was
remarking that Lord Brassey throws open the
Sunbeam to the public on Sundays. “Asa result
of this thoughtless action, watermen and others
were induced to work on the Sabbath in plying for
hire with their boats, and the Lord’s Day was,
therefore, desecrated. This work of Sabbath-
breaking, he predicted, would end disastrously,
and Providence would some day cause the Sun-
beam to strike on a rock and sink to the bottom of
the sea.”
There’s One above has got
His eye on Brassey’s yacht,
Which will surely give its “noble owner” one day
A sudden fatal shock
By colliding with a rock,
Since ’tis open to inspection on a Sunday.
Good shepherd J. M'Neill
Was commissioned to reveal
To the sheep whose simple souls he’s been awaking,
That the purpose of the Lord
Is to sink (with all aboard)
The Sunbeam, on a charge of Sabbath-breaking.
The well-upholstered boat,
Though at present quite afloat,
Will shortly disappear beneath the water,
Where the winkles, prawns, and sharks
Will play gastronomic larks
With Lord and Lady Brassey and the daughter ;
For Providence —so kind
To the piously-inclined
And sinners who are stricken with contrition—
Can he very hard, we know,
On a Sabbath-breaking show
If it snavels useful shillings for admission.
Yea, that’s where Brassey’s sin
Most undoubtedly comes in :
He doesn’t stick at charging for inspection,
And the blessed Cause he robs
Of some nimble little “ bobs”
Which might have fattened J. M'N.’s collection.

America leads again. Chicago city council
fives medical men, for the consideration of a two-
shilling piece (50 cents), a little badge with a red
cross on it, which gives the wearer “right of way”
for himself and his vehicle in Chicago streets as
against all processions, persons, vehicles or animals.
A full-length portrait in oils of F. C.
Mason, M.L. A., hangs around the ground floor of
the Vic. Artists’ Gallery, facing the refreshment
bar. Gordon Coutts, who painted it to somebody’s
order, has caught the true sentiment of this painful
subject. There stands the wooden-looking Mason
in a new frock coat with velvet collar, new
trousers fresh from the tailor’s press, newly-oiled
hair, and new boots pointing left and right. The
velvet collar is characteristic of the man, and the
portrait, as a whole, harmonises with the collar.
Indeed, the work is good of its class, which class
is good enough for Mason, M.L. A. The
glorified chairman of Committees will, in due
course, be formally presented with this mark of
esteem.
