Wot Made in Heaven .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had a bad lower lip, a sloping occiput and
dyed hair and moustache. She used to have to
brush the dye on for him. I thought that a ver?
hard clause of the matrimonial contract. She
thought so, too. She was 22. He was 48 She
was extremely and delicately pretty. Reminded
me of eggshell china. He was tall, swarthy and
stout, and until you got to hate him, which you
did in 20 minutes, you would consider him hand-
some.
Mour wife does not appear to be very strori"' ”
We were standing on the verandah with our
after-dinner pipes when I made the remark. He
sent a sullen glance through the open window to
W fra^le creature was nursing her baby
bhe is not strong,” he grunted. “I’ve been
married five years, and in .that time I’ve not had
her one day sound.”
He was a lawyer, and had been struck off the
rolls in another province. In this one he
had managed to secure a clerkship at A 4 a week.
His name was Grantby, and with his wife and
delicate, ill-tempered baby he was staying at the
same second-rate East Melbourne boarding-house
as myself. Mrs. Grantby was known amongst the
other boarders as “A Study in Shudders,” a
compliment to the effect which her hus-
band’s basilisk glances produced on her
The landlady told me that Mr. Grantby
loved Mrs. Grantby “something frightful, and
could not bear her to speak to any other gent. ”
Also, she mentioned that if Mrs. Grantby left
her room in the morning before her master, or did
not return to it at the same moment as he did, he
would pinch the sleeping infant, set it screaming,
and so fetch the mother at the double. If a ques-
tion or remark were addressed her in Grantby’s
presence, she always glanced at him before answer-
ing, as if to get his permission to speak, or to read
in his bad face what her answer ought to be. At
meals she never took a second helping or cup of
tea unless he told her to pass up her plate or cup.
If he was off his feed, she ate nothing. When he
laughed, she smiled. When he was ill-tempered,
she wept—he gave her good cause. She was as
slavish and terrorised as any aboriginal woman.
“ Get your wife a couple of dozen of stout,” said
I to Beauty’s Beast one day. She was recovering
from the birth of a dead infant. He was recover-
ing from a drunken spree. I was a medical
student and had learned something that made me
want to murder him. He got the stout, and with
the exception of two bottles, drank the whole of
it himself. The eggshell-china wife resumed con-
sumption of the boarding-house boiled tea.
“Get some more stout,” said I, “and leave it
for her.”
“ No,” he answered ; “ it’s too much temptation
for me to have in the house. I daresay she wants
it, but a woman ought to make some sacrifice for
her husband.”
As for the woman, all her faculties were con-
centrated in love for her child, whilst her
nervous system had turned fluid under her
husband’s quiet brutality. Out of my modest
medical knowledge I gave her a year to live.
Three years had sped before I met the Grantbys
again. I was practising in Sydney and happened
to drop into a seat beside them in the Lyceum
dress-circle. I was surprised to see the little
woman alive, let alone so altered for the better.
She had gained at least 2st. in weight. There was
color m her cheeks, light in her eyes, ease in her
manner. Presently I doubted if the change was
tor the better. Her face and voice were hard and,
in a sense, unpleasant.
We chatted for a while. When the curtain fell
on the end of the second act Grantby scowled and
averred that “ the play was rot.”
Ah ! ” yawned his wife, “ I’ve no doubt those
pork-producing animals of which Chicago is so
fond would grunt out the same idea if someone
threw pearls into' their trough. ”
Grantby must have heard the gasp I emitted.
1 put on my glasses and had a good look at the
eggshell-china woman who used to be afraid to
take a second cup of tea unless her husband bade
her do so.
“ Well,’’answered Grantby, quietly savage, “I’m
going home. Come on.”
She looked up at him and laughed derisively.
‘ Certainly not. I’ll stay till the gas is being
put out. But don’t you wait. My old friend the
young doctor will see me home— sweet home.”
cn so ' le hved in a cottage at the North
Shore. On the way I got her to talk, and so
learned the reason of the change in her. The ill-
tempered baby was dead a year before. And
with it had died the mother’s ability to
feel, had died also love of life, hope, and
the remnant of the affection she once bore
her husband. A curious physical and psycho-
logical change followed. Freed from moral
and personal fear, her health grew better. The
emotional side of her nature shrivelled, whilst the
intellectual aspect brightened and hardened.
Where she had been nervous she was now defiant.
Grantby’s key to the nature of the wife of last
vear was useless for the woman who had taken
her place.
“ I hold him of such little account,” said she, as
we stepped on to the wharf, “that I don’t take
the trouble to leave him. His great joy in life
was to see me in a state of abject fear and fawning.
Now he is miserable because lam not. It was his
fault that my child was so frail, so I look upon
him as the cause of her death. But it lessened
my grief a little to see that his was great, for he
certainly loved her too. This is our place,”
The cottage before which we stopped Was in
utter darkness. No answer was returned to my
repeated ringing and knocking.
“He has locked up the house,” remarked Mrs.
Grantby, “ and forbidden the servant to answer.
I must see that this doesn’t occur again. Go on
to the road, doctor, and get me a nice handy piece
of road-metal. ” I obeyed, then followed her to
the side of the house.
“This is my husband’s room.” She indicated
it by hurling two pounds of blue-metal through
the window. An angry shout came from within
the darkened room, the hall door was flung open,
and Grantby, muttering threats and imprecations,
pulled his wife inside and banged the door. I felt
very unmanly as I sneaked off hurriedly. The
brute would certainly beat her. If she taunted him
he might kill her. His horrible threats seemed
to echo in my ears as I walked back to the ferry.
Suddenly I turned and fairly raced over the
ground I had just covered. He must be beating
her. I panted up to the cottage. A room in front
was lighted with two flaring gas-jets without
globes. The blinds were not lowered. Mrs.
Grantby sat at a little cottage piano strumming a
noisy music-hall air. I slipped along to where I
could view the man’s room. Grantby was in his
pyjamas and the light in his rear showed that he
was stuffing papers and clothes into the gash in
his window.

Some Mummeries.
Mr. F. J. Hallewell, the father of singing in Sydney, has
sedulously cultivated the art. He is a gentleman who
can cry to order—an artist—and is extremely partial to
old English glees. His pupils reverence his artistic in-
stincts, and bend the knee to his interpretation of ora-
torio. For a famous singer, he is very unassuming, and
has informed his friends that he is only now learning how
to sing. “ Old Dad,” as he is familiarly termed, admires
ungrudgingly the great Gounod, and echoes the senti-
ments conveyed in that musician’s confession regarding
Mozart. Said the composer : “At2l it was I. At a later
stage of existence, I and Mozart. As years rolled by,
Mozart and I. In the evening of life, Mozart.” Mr.
Hallewell was, once upon a time, choirmaster at St.
Mary’s Cathedral. He is always willing to lend a helping
hand to youthful singers, whom he advises to “Try not
to try ” when singing.
There is one character in which Bellew doesn’t hesitate
to cover up his classic beauty. That is “ Charlotte Cor-
day,” the eponymous heroine of which stabs him while he
is taking a medicated bath. Bellew makes himself as
hideous as possible : dirty, physically feeble, and catarrhal.
By the way, on the first night of this tragedy-in-a-bath-
tub the barrel in which Bellew was taking his last wash
toppled over : it was perfectly empty, and the curly one
was seated in it, comfortably clothed from head to foot.
Pro. : This story is true, of a well-known Sydney
singer, Signora . She was at the head of a small
opera-co. in the country, playing a couple of “ show-
dates.” “ 111 Trovatore ” was the opera. Signora——,
as Leonora, sang in Italian, and a portion of the audience,
stupid with vine (or gum) leaves in its uncombed hair,
was noisy and hisssed. Signora submitted for a time, but
in Leonora’s dying-scene lost her temper, and instead of
the operatic words sang out “Ignoramuses! Ignor-
amuses ! ! Ignoramuses 1!! ” The audience accepted the
fiery censure as part of the role, but the rest of the per-
formers standing at the wing nearly died laughing.
We have just seen departing from our shores Miss
Colborne-Baber. This lady was wont to be asked by
youthful aspirants for musical honors what throat process
was necessary to be “in form.” She used to tender
advice as follows ; “ Eat a hearty meal at, say, 4.30 p.m.,
wash it down decently, and read a pleasure-giving novel
until the time approaches to sing. Take nothing in the
meantime, but if absolutely needed, a medium cup of tea
would not be quite amiss.” To Miss Baber’s intimate
friends, it is known that she has been spurred on to
greater ambition by the unequivocal successes of her
once friendly rival, Miss Frances Saville. When the
latter was here, there Was certainly some division of
engagements between the two cantatrices. Ultimately,
when Miss Saville courted leisure moments in preference
to the continuous plaudits of the populace, as became her
independent circumstances, Miss Baber had the field all
to herself.
“The Silver King,” least melodramatic and best of
nielodramas, has one incident which shows how impossible
it is for the melo-dramatist to refrain entirely from melo-
draming. In the scene in the den by the river, Denver,
disguised as an idiot, having heard The Spider accused of
the murder of which he believes himself guilty, gives
himself away purely for the sake of a tableau. “Itis I,
Wilfred Denver ! ” he shrieks, tearing open his shirt and
jumping into the limelight. The Spider (who shot Ware)
lets his man skip without drawing on him. The audience
likes the scene, but it is bald, inartistic dazzle, and a blot
on a great play.
Williamson only gets ,£6O a week outof the Melb. “ Silver
King,” and wonders why he did not produce it himself,
and collar the swag.
Somebody sends along a playbill of a recent performance
of “ Silver King” by a scratch co. Out Back. The leading
mummers announce that they played their parts for 1200
nights in Melbourne and Sydney, and, as no one is re-
ported to have expressed dissent with an egg, the audience
probably believed it. Wilfred Denver and Jaikes were
played by the same man, therefore the impossible old
servitor didn’t meet Denver and give him his life’s sav-
ings ; he simply soliloquised to the effect that he would
offer Wilfred the money when he came in ; and then Wil-
fred arrived and monologued to the effect that he would
take the coin if Jaikes pressed it on him. Truly it is a
hard life to be a cheap mummer in the bush. The chief
points about it are the periodical breakdowns of the ghost,
the seizure of the props, for the unpaid board-bill, the
ejectment of the co. into the street by the infuriated pub.-
keeper, and then the long row of homeless tragedians in
broken boots pondering on the pavement, and wailing at
intervals to the unsympathetio groggist : “ Gimme back
me clothes.”
Pro. : ’Twas the first night of a new piece, and we were
all a bit nervous. In one scene I had to read a long letter,
and, knowing I would have the paper in my hand, had
omitted to learn the text. On this epistle the plot turned.
Just before the cue came for me to read, I discovered I
had forgotten the precious writing. I had the stage to
myself, so could not get it brought on. I whispered to
the prompter, “Have forgotten letter.” As the time came
nearer I was in an awful funk, but at the critical moment
the author, who was at the wings, came on, and held out
the important paper. He grasped my hand and exclaimed,
“My dear fellow, forgive the liberty of an old friend.
You dropped this out of your pocket. And now, pardon
my departure—my bride awaits me 1 ” Who the author
was, and what his bride had to do with the play, was
never explained to the audience.
Critic Bernard Shaw on Geraldine Oliffe, in Jones’s new
play : “Miss Oliffe, who played Mrs. Prothero, was new
to the London critics, though I had had the luck to dis-
cover her at the Avenue during the illness of Mrs. Alma
Stanley some time ago. She deepened the favorable im-
pression I received on that occasion, and will, I have no
doubt, soon be a familiar and indispensable figure in our
London drawing castes. The end of that will be, I sup-
pose, that she will give up acting and have all her parts
written expressly for her.” When a London critic happens
to be the first to praise an actress or an author, he maims
to have been a discoverer. It is Cockney cheek.
A Frenchman gives the latest explanation of the
Indian juggler’s “mango trick.” In this famous con-
jurors’ puzzle the operator plants a mango-seed in some
earth he brings with him, covers it with a cloth, and in a
few minutes the cloth begins to be pushed up by a grow-
ing plant to the height of even a yard. “M. Ragonneau
noticed that the jugglers invariably brought their own
earth, and at last found that it was always taken from
ant-hills. Analysis of such earth showed it to be strongly
impregnated with ‘formic acid,’a substance which causes
the bad smell of ants or bugs, and which chemists obtain
by boiling down red ants. The Frenchman tried common
earth impregnated with dilute formic acid (1 in 5000), and
promptly duplicated the performance. The growth is a
natural process due to the acid most rapidly consuming
the hard outer skin of the seed, and strongly stimulating
the growth of the germ within.” All a fake. The best
accounts of the “mango trick” include the growth of
flowers and fruit; and you can’t get a tree a yatd high,
with flowers and fruit, out of a seed in a few minutes
with the assistance of any amount of “ formic earth ” or
“ formic acid,” any more than you can get a full-
grown rooster out of a new-laid egg. The “mango
trick ” isn’t an actuality : it’s an illusion; and the
only feasible explanation of it is that all the specta-
tors are hypnotised by the performer. The writer
once saw a certain amount of “mango trick” dur-
ing a steamer stoppage at Colombo. The performer
admitted that he was only a tyro ; he had to make three
attempts before he got a tree ; and the spectators were
kept about 20 yards off. But he had no earth and no
cloth ; only a little wicker basket; and his basket, him-
self, his loin-rag, and his stage were well scrutinised
before and after the performance. Nevertheless, he laid
the basket mouth downwards on the stones of a court-
yard, incanted and pattered a bit, lifted the basket and
showed a miniature tree about four inches high. On
went the basket again ; more incantation ; basket off ;
tree about a foot high, seemingly growing quite naturally
between two stones. Performance repeated, and the tree
grew another six inches; basket put on and taken off
again—tree gone altogether ! That man couldn’t make
flowers or fruit; said he didn’t know enough. But,
barring hypnotism, a dozen pair of sharp eyes could find
no clue to the mechanism of his phenomena.
Joe Jefferson claims to be the original infant in the
well-worn property yarn of “ The Scalping of Rolla.” He
says he was a startled child in a white tunic, beautifully
striped with gold bands, and in the grasp of an infuriated
tragedian crossing a shaky bridge In a blaze of fire and
smoke. To little Joe the position was perilous, and he
seized Rolla by the hair of his head. The noble Peruvian
cried, “ Let go ! ” but as “ Joe ” was obeying the first law
of nature and not “ Rolla,” he gripped the top-knot
tighter, and the next instant the chieftain, bereft of his
feather-duster head-dress and wig, was publicly scalped.

Silly Willy !
Gov. Hampden’s opinion of the art-value
of the pictures hung in the Sydney vice-regal
residence is as low as, and, in some respects, lower
than, the condemnatory opinion the late Gov.
Duff expressed soon after arrival. And yet the
canvases cost N.S.W. taxpayers a lot of hard
cash.
The best cow can give 11,0001 b. of milk
per annum, which, on the separator system, will
give over 4601 b. of butter. A poor cow will give
rather less than a third of that. Also, shallow
pan setting takes 251 b. of milk to lib. butter; deep
setting takes 24|lb. ; separator 241 b. Verdict of
Canadian Agricultural Department.
Cheerful item from Sydney Bankruptcy
Court :
Insolvent said he was formerly an undertaker, now an
acrobat. Insolvency due to depression of trade. Assets
included a hearse worth £l5, and two horses, one valued
at £l3 2s. 6d. and the other at £4. During the recent hot
weather he engaged a carpenter to make coffins, as he had
a rush of work for the Asylum. His wife hoped to do a
brisk business in Liverpool in winter, as there were
generally a good many deaths at that time.
An English sassiety paper reports : ‘ ‘ One
of the latest whispers from the upper circles is
that boiled beans and bacon have been served
lately at some very smart dinners in the best
houses. ” A democratic cynic says of this startling
circumstance : You may not see why the upper
circles should eat beans and bacon in fear and
trembling, and whisper the fact with awe ; but,
then, there is a reason.” A strange mystery
attaches to certain dishes. The writer remembers
an instance of one fat feeder breathing solemnly
in the ear of another fat feeder, “ The finest meal
to be obtained in the city of London at the present
moment is— ham and peas. ” He spake the words
“ ham and peas” so softly and slowly that one
could have heard a pin drop between them. After
which, he recoiled a few paces and waited for his
agitated friend to recover from the shock of the
announcement. Then they sighed in unison and
went away to obtain ham and peas at a restaurant.

The Shooting of Saysoon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had had so many masters that no one knew
who had christened him, but his name was bay-
soon when he followed the butcher to the door of
a new customer, and promptly chewed a cat which
attempted to pass him at the gate, then he
sneaked his head in under his master s arm and
grabbed two pounds of rump steak, which went
down with a gobble. . ,
The missus happened to be passing the door.
‘ ‘ You old wretch ! ” she cried. .
Saysoon licked his lips and waved his great
dirty-white tail; then, with a grin which bared
his well-worn fangs, he flopped down at her feet
and laid his huge, square jowl upon his crooked
forepaws. , , . , ~.
“ Why don’t you teach him manners—letting
him steal like that ? ’ the missus asked.
“ That’s more than anyone has ever been able
to do. If a ladylike yurself ’ud had ’m from a
p U p I—but he’s too old now ; he’s the wickedest old
devil in the town.”
“He doesn’t look it—such lovely, mild brown
eyes.” , , ,
” “ Just you ’it ’im a clip on the ear and see em
redden.” ' . _ , ,
The missus concluded not to try the effect ot a
“ Is he a Newfoundland ? ” she asked.
“ Yes—pure bred ; stole in the beginning, they
sa y —he’s somewhere about eleven; I ve ad im
myself for four year.”
The butcher turned to go, and Saysoon rose with
difficulty and staggered as he regained his feet.
He was but a bag of bones, with patches of
mange showing pink at every joint, his black and
white coat matted, harsh and unhealthy. ,
“ Mangy from raw meat,” the man explained ;
“ if you don’t give it ’im he’ll kill it for ’imself—
and ’is loins is gone from the boys riding im in
the streets—he’s rare good to the pups an the
kiddies.” „
“ I suppose you wouldn t sell him . , „
“ Times are that bad, I would for five shillin s.
“Then bring him round to-morrow with his
collar and chain. ”
The man chuckled.
“ He’s never ’ad a collar, being known ter the
perlice;’ and he’d slip his ’ead through any leather
tanned.” ...
The missus smiled. She was a clever contriver,
and there was a snug loosebox to the stable.
Saysoon arrived next day. He howled a bit
when the butcher drove away and left him in the
loosebox, then stood at the barred-door looking
piteously at the missus when she came near.
He took very kindly to her caresses and the
warm boiled food she gave him, and the family
congratulated her upon her purchase as the even-
ing passed without sound from the captive.
In the morning one of the bars of the loosebox-
door lay in fragments on the ground, and Saysoon
had taken his aching teeth home to the shop.
They lined the door with stout wire-netting, but
he grubbed a hole in the clay floor under the wall
and went home again.
They tied him so tightly with a collar that he
appeared choking, but he slipped that with ease ;
and then they devised a girth with a strap from it
to the collar, but he wriggled round and chewed
the connecting leather, slipped the collar again,
and went home with the girth on him. I hat ex-
periment cost them ten shillings. _
A.t last they euchred him with a similar fixture
made from dog-chains, upon which he only once
tried his teeth, then resigned himself to his fate.
He sustained his reputation in the district,
worried cats, fought dogs at every chance, brought
threats of sudden death upon himself from travel-
ling shepherds whose mobs he loved to scatter.
It was no use calling him, he would go on, and,
after the mischief was done, would come back
waving his tail and grinning with delight.
At first they tried to beat him, but he convinced
them that it would be “ a bite for a blow.”
The missus could always manage him, when she
was by. A tweak of his ear was enough—her he
never rebelled against—but he knew how to get
what he wanted from her.
When he came she had said, “ He shall have a
good time for a year, then we’ll shoot him ; I can’t
think how anyone can be cruel enough to let an
animal die of old age.”
“ Wants shooting himself if he does,” the
master agreed.
The year passed—then two ; Saysoon’s looks
testified to the good times, but there was still no
word of the shooting.
January came, with Christmas bills unpaid and
pinching poverty.
“ Shall we register him for just one more year ? ”
the missus asked.
The master looked at her wistful face. “It
won’t break us, I expect,” he said.
“ Nonsense, pater,” the eldest daughter put in ;
“ he’s cost us a mint of money already, what with
his food and his freaks. He’ll be certain not to
live through the winter.”
The family was breakfasting ; there came a
knock at the door ; a small boy handed a note to
the eldest daughter, who took it to her father.,
“Something more about Saysoon, I bet,” she
grumbled.
It was ; a polite request that Saysoon might be
kept to his owner’s bounds, and an intimation that
if he did any more pounds’ worth of damage the
writer would act as executioner.
“ I wish he would make away with the old brute
for us,” the master sighed.
“ I never saw such a fuss—shooting a dog ;
why, I’ll shoot him if you show me how,” the
eldest daughter snapped.
“There, there, that’s enough ; we’ll agree to
pop him off on the thirty-first —that’s the regis-
tration limit,” said the missus, with misty eyes.
A little lad slipped from his chair and went out.
He cast himself full-length beside Saysoon, who
sprawled all over him.
“ He was a busy dog, and then they scold him—
poor old dearest.” _ _ _ _
Saysoon rubbed his nose with one paw and smiled
as usual. .
That evening they noticed that his hind-legs
were shaky, and two days later he could only drag
them feebly in a clumsy crawl.
When the small children were in bed the missus
said :
“ What about Saysoon ? ’
“I suppose he’ll have to go now,” the master
groaned.
He fetched his gun and went to where the dog
was lying in the new-cut hay.
Saysoon had been a good retriever, and usually
bounded at sight of a gun. Now he looked up
quickly, sniffed, and flattened his brows.
He refused to rise, no coaxing would move him ;
and then when the master gave it up and turned
his back Saysoon crawled into the kitchen and
pressed close to the missus’s skirt.
She looked down ; there was water in her eyes.
She took the great head in her hands, put her
brow to the thick frontal and whispered,
“ They shan’t shoot you, Say., they shan t.
He raised himself on his crippled quarters,
pawed her lap till she shook hands, then rolled
over and licked her shoe.
The master appeared in the doorway.
“ The old dog wouldn’t !! ”
The missus pushed him out into the passage and
shut the door behind her. .
“ Sh-h-h ! Never mind to-night; hes in no
pain.” -
When she went back to the kitchen Saysoon was
not there.
In the morning the missus stood at the break-
fast-table as the family seated itself ; the little lad
came in.
“ Oh mother ! I can’t find Saysoon anywhere ;
there are marks all along the road where he’s been
dragging along.”
The eldest sister s eyes grew dim, then liquid-
bright as she glared defiantly round the table,
daring notice ; the missus shaved ham with mar-
vellous dexterity, and dealt the plates round like
playing-cards.
As the master came in she said :
“ I might have known it —he’s crept away to die
alone.”
The master pushed away his porridge and blew
his nose ; the little lad sniffed and went out. _
After a few minutes he came back in eager joy.
“ I’ve found him ! He’s in the trees down the
paddock, but he can’t get up ! ”
In revulsion, the missus’s grief turned to anger.
“Walter !” she said severely to her biggest son,
“ go to town at once and bring me the proper dose
of prussic acid for a large dog ! ” _
“Nonsense!” the master cried, “you’ll be
poisoning yourself. I’d look well at the inquest —
d the dog ! Fetch me the gun, Walter ! ”
As he passed the missus she grabbed his sleeve :
“You can’t now, with everyone about. I’ll pay
someone to do it.”
“Yes, and have him half-killed —besides, I’ve
not a cent available until next Monday.”
All day long Saysoon lay in the shade with one
and another watching him. Walter scoured the
district, but no one could or would come over, and
the master returned at six o’clock sick at heart
and trying to hope it was done.
Dusk fell, the children were in bed ; the master
took the gun and walked firmly to the paddock
fence; he called the missus from among the trees.
When she came close he saw her anguished face.
“I feel a vile traitor,” she moaned. “I pro-
mised him you shouldn’t.”
He dropped the gun to his side.
“ And lam a beastly coward. I’ll make a mull
of it—it’s like a blooming murder—l can’t! Don’t
be ashamed of me ! ”
“I love you for it,” she whispered. “You go
inside; I’ll see to it.”
His face paled.
“ You’re never going to attempt it yourself ? ”
“No, no. See those men coming down the
road? They’ll do it; and we’ll never see them
again, thank goodness ! ”
“ But I’ve not a penny to give them ! ”
“ I have, my lucky half-crowns.”
The strangers were lads with kindly faces ; they
had no objection to shooting a strange dog. The
missus gave them a hunk of meat, which one was
to offer from the front while the other sneaked in
behind ; he would never know.
“Mind!” she said; “two half-crowns each if
there is only one shot —and —no other sound ! ”
Then they fled indoors.
The eldest daughter sat at the piano. Her
father shouted, “ Play ! play ! as loud as ever you
can! ”
She straightened up, struck a feeble chord ; then
her hands fell limply to her lap, and great drops
splashed down upon the keys.
In the corner the missus was hunting the half-
crowns among the rustling papers of a drawer,
where she well knew they were not.
There came the dull “ plop ” of a charge fired
into the earth —and no other sound.
The master helped himself to a big nip of
whisky.

Tb« NEW “LIP-FLANGE” FRUE VANNER BELT.
“ Will Otitlast Two of tlje Ordioary.”
Genuine “ Frue ” Belts (obtainable only from
the patentees or their authorised agents) have, owing
to the great care and attention paid to their manu-
facture, invariably given good satisfaction, but, after
being in use a long time, the old flange had a ten-
dency to become weak and lose its vertical position.
This prevented the machine carrying the proper depth
of load, and allowed the sand to slop over and get
into the bearings. The new “ Lip-Flange,” which
SECTIONAL VIEW OF FLANGE.
has just been patented throughout the colonies, does
away with this difficulty almost entirely. It is
made of a very superior and elastic rubber prepared
in a special manner, and its peculiar shape prevents
it cracking in going over the rollers, or losing its
vertical position. All “ Frue Yanners ” sold in the
future will be fitted with this new “Lip-Flange”
Belt without additional charge, and the belts them-
selves may be procured from the patentees at a very
slight advance on the old price.
